Divine Transcendence
beginner
4 min
The Creator's Incomparability: Genus and Form
Baqillani argues that the Creator cannot be similar to contingent beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Creator cannot resemble His creation in genus or in form, and the implications of this for divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God is absolutely unique—resemblance to creation would make Him contingent.

Imam Baqillani begins this chapter by establishing a fundamental principle of Islamic theology: God’s total incomparability to His creation. He argues that any supposed resemblance would either be in genus or in form, and both possibilities lead to absurd consequences. This lesson unpacks that argument and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let Baqillani's own argument flow through before you interpret.
Notice that the argument is structured as a conditional: 'if He resembled them...'
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents two ways resemblance could occur: in genus or in form. Find where these are introduced and note how Baqillani defines each.
Resemblance in genus means sharing the same category; resemblance in form means sharing a specific shape or composition.
3
Understand the argument against genus
Focus on the reasoning: if the Creator resembled creation in genus, then both would be of the same kind, making the Creator contingent and creation eternal. Grasp the proof using 'substitutability' (sadd aḥaduhumā masadd ṣāḥibihī).
Think of two blacknesses: they are interchangeable in sight. That shows they are of the same genus.
4
Understand the argument against form
If resemblance were in form, the Creator would not be one thing but composed, requiring a form-giver and making Him contingent. This leads to an infinite regress.
Baqillani references an earlier argument: 'form only occurs from a form-giver.'
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Both alternatives are impossible, so the Creator cannot resemble His creation. This establishes tanzīh (divine transcendence).
This is a cornerstone of Islamic theology: God is unlike anything in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في أنه لا يجوز أن يكون صانع المحدثات مشبهاً لها. ولا يجوز أن يكون صانع المحدثات مشبهاً لها لأنه لو أشبهها لكان لا يخلو أن يشبهها في الجنس أو في الصورة ولو أشبهها في الجنس لكان محدثاً ولكانت قديمة كما أنه قديم لأن المشتبهين هما ما سد أحدهما مسد صاحبه وناب منابه ودليل ذلك أن السوادين المشتبهين يسدان في المنظر مسداً واحداً وكذلك البياضان والتأليفان ولو أشبهها في الصورة والتأليف لم يكن شيئاً واحداً ولوجب أن يكون له مصور جامع لأن الصورة لا تقع إلا من مصور لما قدمناه من قبل ولوجب أن يكون من جنس الجواهر المتماسة وأن يكون محدثاً وذلك محال.
Chapter: That it is not permissible for the Maker of contingent things to resemble them. And it is not permissible for the Maker of contingent things to resemble them, because if He resembled them, He would inevitably either resemble them in genus or in form. If He resembled them in genus, then He would be contingent and they would be eternal just as He is eternal, because two resembling things are those one of which takes the place of the other and substitutes for it. The proof of that is that two resembling blacknesses take the same place in sight, and likewise two whitenesses and two compositions. If He resembled them in form and composition, then He would not be one thing, and it would be necessary for Him to have a form-giver, because form only occurs from a form-giver as we have previously mentioned, and it would be necessary for Him to be of the genus of contiguous substances and to be contingent, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This chapter establishes divine transcendence (tanzīh). Baqillani argues that the Creator cannot resemble any created being. If He did, the resemblance would be either in genus or in form. Resemblance in genus would imply that both are of the same kind, making the Creator contingent and the creation eternal like Him—but that is impossible. Resemblance in form would mean the Creator has a form, which would require a maker itself, leading to an infinite regress, and would also make Him composed and contingent. Thus, the Creator is absolutely unique and unlike anything in creation. The argument uses the logical principle that two things that are substitutable are essentially the same, which cannot apply to God and the world.

Key Terms
صانع
محدثات
تشبيه

صورة
تأليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's attributes (e.g., power) resemble human attributes, mistakenly equating terms with essence.
Correction: In kalām, 'resemblance' (tashbīh) refers to essential similarity, not merely shared names. Baqillani argues that even if we use terms like 'power' for God and humans, the essence of divine power is utterly different—not comparable.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what would happen if the Creator resembled creation in genus?
A) He would be eternal
B) He would be contingent and creation would be eternal
C) He would need a form-giver
D) He would be composed of parts

Key Takeaway
The Creator cannot resemble creation in any way; any supposed resemblance would entail contingency, which is impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'seeing' or 'hearing,' do you sincerely believe that His seeing is utterly unlike any human seeing? How often do you catch yourself imagining God with human-like attributes? Today, commit to replacing every anthropomorphic mental image with the concept of absolute transcendence.
?
Baqillani proves that any resemblance to creation would make God contingent—not God at all. Do you live as though God is truly beyond comparison, or do you secretly treat Him as a bigger version of a human? Identify one area where you have been treating God as like creation (e.g., expecting Him to act like a human). What will you change starting now?
?
If God were composed of parts or belonged to a genus, He would need a creator. Do you ever act as if God needs something or is dependent on His creation? Examine your supplications: do you ask as if God is constrained by your requests? How can you purify your worship to reflect His absolute self-sufficiency?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
6 min
The Creator’s Unlikeness to Creation
Baqillani proves the Creator cannot be similar to creation in genus or form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani’s logical argument that the Creator cannot resemble originated things in any way, whether in genus or form.

Core Idea
If the Creator resembled creation, absurdities would follow.

Baqillani presents a rigorous logical argument that the Creator of the universe cannot be similar to His creation in any way. Using a simple dichotomy (genus or form), he shows that either the Creator would be originated or creation would be eternal—both impossible. This lesson walks through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question
Start with the key claim: the Creator cannot resemble originated things. Ask yourself why this matters for Islamic theology.
This is about divine transcendence (tanzih).
2
Follow the dichotomy
Baqillani splits the possibility of resemblance into two exclusive options: resemblance in genus or resemblance in form. Notice that no third option is considered.
This is a classic proof by contradiction.
3
Examine the first branch
If the Creator resembled creation in genus, then either the Creator would be originated (since genus implies temporality) or creation would be eternal (since the Creator is eternal). Both are false.
The example of similar blacknesses illustrates that two things of the same genus are interchangeable.
4
Examine the second branch
If the Creator resembled creation in form, then He would be composite and require a designer (musawwir), which leads to an infinite regress or origination.
Form implies composition and need for a maker.
5
Conclude the argument
Since both branches lead to impossibilities, the original assumption that the Creator resembles creation is false. The Creator must be utterly unlike anything in creation.
This establishes tanzih as a rational necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في أنه لا يجوز أن يكون صانع المُحْدَثات مُشْبهاً لها ولا يجوز أن يكون صانع المحدثات مشبهاً لها لأنه لو أشبهها لكان لا يخلو أن يشبهها في الجنس أو في الصورة ولو أشبهها في الجنس لكان محدثاً ولكانت قديمة كما أنه قديم لأن المشتبهين هما ما سدّ أحدهما مَسَدَّ صاحبه وناب منابه ودليل ذلك أن السوادين المُشْتبهين يسدّان في المنظر مسدّاً واحداً وكذلك البياضان والتأليفان ولو أشبهها في الصورة والتأليف لم يكن شيئاً واحداً ولوجب أن يكون له مصوِّر جامع لأن الصورة لا تقع إلا من مصوّر لما قدمناه من قبل ولوجب أن يكون من جنس الجواهر المتماسة وأن يكون مُحْدَثاً وذلك محال
Chapter: That it is not permissible that the maker of originated things be similar to them. And it is not permissible that the maker of originated things be similar to them, because if He were similar to them, He would be, without exception, similar to them in genus or in form. If He were similar to them in genus, He would be originated, and they would be eternal as He is eternal, for the two similar things are those where one takes the place of the other and stands in its stead. The proof of that is that two similar blacknesses take the same place in sight, and likewise two whitenesses and two compositions. And if He were similar to them in form and composition, He would not be one thing, and it would be necessary that He have a composing designer, because form only comes from a designer, as we have stated previously. And it would be necessary that He be of the genus of contiguous substances and that He be originated, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the creator of originated things cannot resemble them. Baqillani argues that resemblance would be either in genus or in form. If in genus, then either the creator would be originated or the creation would be eternal—both false. He illustrates with examples: two similar blacknesses are indistinguishable. If in form and composition, the creator would require a maker (musawwir) and would be a composite substance, hence originated. Both possibilities are impossible, so the creator must be utterly unlike creation.

Key Terms
مشابهة
صانع
جنس
صورة
مصوّر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God shares some resemblance with creation, e.g., having a form or being of a similar 'kind.'
Correction: Baqillani shows this leads to logical absurdities: either the Creator would be originated or creation eternal, or the Creator would need a designer. Thus, God must be utterly unlike anything in creation.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what would follow if the creator were similar to creation in genus?
A) Both would be eternal or both originated
B) The creator would need a form
C) The creator would be one thing
D) The creation would be like the creator in power

Key Takeaway
The Creator must be utterly unlike any created thing in genus and form, as proven by logical deduction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you find yourself picturing something familiar—like a king on a throne, a father figure, or even light? How does Baqillani's argument that any resemblance leads to absurdity make you feel about those mental images? Will you actively work to purge them from your mind?
?
The proof shows that God cannot share even a single attribute with creation in the same way. Are there attributes of God (like seeing, hearing, or mercy) that you secretly imagine 'like' your own, only bigger? What would it mean to truly believe that nothing—absolutely nothing—is like Him? How would that change your worship today?
?
Baqillani's argument forces you to accept that your intellect cannot fully grasp God. Does that humble you, or does it frustrate you? Are you willing to surrender the pride of thinking you can figure God out, and instead rest in the fact that He is utterly beyond your comprehension?

God's Creation Is Not for a Purpose
Baqillani rejects purpose in divine creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that God's creation of the world is not motivated by any purpose or inducement.

Core Idea
Purposes imply need, which cannot apply to the Eternal God.

This passage presents a theological objection to the idea that God created the world for a purpose. Baqillani responds by showing that purposes and motives are only relevant to beings with needs, who can benefit or be harmed. Since God is eternal and all-knowing, He is free from such limitations.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the questioner's objection carefully. It lists several possible reasons for God's action: inducement, mover, motivator, purpose, or idea.
Notice the structure: the questioner presents a dichotomy—either for something or for nothing.
2
Identify Baqillani's response
Baqillani first seems to concede that God created for something, but then he provides a proof that such reasons cannot apply to God. Read the proof slowly.
The concession may be rhetorical to set up the argument.
3
Understand the proof's two parts
The proof has two main points: (1) Purposes and motives indicate need and temporality, (2) Motives only affect those who are heedless, ignorant, or fear/hope for outcomes. Both are impossible for God.
The passage cuts off, but the conclusion is clear.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: What does this argument say about God's attributes? It emphasizes God's self-sufficiency, eternality, and omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في أن صنع الله للعالم ليس لغرض
فإن قال قائل : فهل تقولون إن صانع العالم صنعه بعد أن لم يصنعه لداع دعاه إلى فعله ومحرّك حرَّكه وباعث بعثه وغرض أزعجه وخاطر اقتضى وجود الحوادث منه أم صنعه لا لشيء مما سألت عنه؟ قيل له : إنه تعالى صنع العالم لشيء مما سألت عنه - فإن قيل وما الدليل على ذلك؟ قيل : الدليل عليه أن الدواعي المزعجات والخواطر والأغراض إنما تكون وتجوز على ذي الحاجة الذي يصح منه اجتلابُ المنافع ودفع المضارّ وذلك أمر لا يجوز إلا على من جازت عليه الآلام واللذاتُ وميل الطبع والنفورُ وكل ذلك دليل على حَدَثِ من وصف به وحاجته إليه وهو مُنتَفٍ عن القديم تعالى، وكذلك الأسباب المزعجة المحركة الباعثة على الأفعال إنما تحرك الغافل وتنبه الجاهل وتخطر للخائف والراجي الذي يخاف الاستضرار بترك الأفعال بإيقاعها الصلاحَ والانتفاع والله يتعالى عن ذلك لأنه عالم بما يكون قبل أن يكون وبما تؤول إليه عواقب الأمور ويعلم السر وأخفى، ولا يجوز على من
Chapter: That God's making of the world is not for a purpose.
If a questioner says: Do you say that the Maker of the world made it after He had not made it due to an inducement that called Him to His act, a mover that moved Him, a motivator that motivated Him, a purpose that agitated Him, and an idea that necessitated the existence of events from Him, or did He make it for nothing of what I asked about? It is said to him: He, exalted, made the world for something of what you asked about? If it is said: And what is the proof of that? It is said: The proof of it is that disturbing inducements, ideas, and purposes only occur and are permissible for one who has need, for whom it is valid to attract benefits and repel harms. And that is a matter that is only permissible for one upon whom pains and pleasures, natural inclination and aversion are possible. And all of that is evidence of the temporality of one described by it and his need for it. And that is negated of the Eternal, exalted. Likewise, disturbing causes, movers, and motivators to actions only move the heedless, awaken the ignorant, and occur to the fearful and hopeful who fears harm from leaving actions and hopes for benefit by performing them. And Allah is exalted above that, because He knows what will be before it is, and the consequences of matters, and He knows the secret and what is more hidden. And it is not permissible for one who...
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The questioner asks whether God created the world due to some inducement, motive, purpose, etc., or for no reason at all. Baqillani responds that God created it for something of what the questioner mentioned (this may be a rhetorical concession to set up the proof). Then the actual proof: such motives and purposes only apply to a being with needs, one who can attract benefits and repel harms, which involves pain, pleasure, inclination, aversion—all signs of temporality and need. Since God is Eternal, He is free from these. Additionally, motives only affect the heedless, ignorant, or those who fear/hope for outcomes. God, being all-knowing of everything before it occurs, does not require motives. The passage cuts off, but the intended conclusion is clearly that God does not act for a purpose.

Key Terms
غرض
داع
حاجة
قديم
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people assume that God acts for a purpose, just as humans do.
Correction: Baqillani explains that purposes imply need and temporality, which are incompatible with God's eternity and self-sufficiency.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani argue that God does not act for a purpose?
A) Because He is all-powerful
B) Because purposes indicate need and temporality
C) Because He has free will
D) Because the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
God's creation is not for a purpose because purposes arise from need and deficiency, which are absent in the all-knowing, self-sufficient Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself assuming that God's actions must fit into your understanding of purpose? How does Baqillani's argument challenge your tendency to measure God's actions by human standards? Are you prepared to submit to a God whose ways are beyond your comprehension of 'why'?
?
When you face trials, your heart may secretly demand to know the purpose. How can you train yourself to trust that God's wisdom is sufficient, even when you cannot discern a reason? What specific step will you take today to surrender your need for explanations?
?
When you seek to do good deeds, do you sometimes do them for a purpose – to feel good, to gain reward, to avoid punishment? Baqillani argues that purposes arise from need. How does this reflect on your own motives? Can you perform an act of worship this week with no conscious purpose, purely out of love and submission to the Self-Sufficient?

Setting Up the Objection: Is God's Creation Purposeful?
Baqillani presents the objection and initial affirmation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hypothetical objection that God's creation must have a motive or purpose, and Baqillani's initial response that God did create for something.

Core Idea
The objector assumes that creation must be driven by a motive or purpose.

Baqillani begins by presenting a hypothetical objector who questions whether the Maker of the world created it due to a motive, mover, or purpose. The initial answer is surprising: Baqillani affirms that God created for something. This sets the stage for the deeper argument that such purposes are actually impossible for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice the list of terms: motive (dā‘ī), mover (muḥarrik), sender (bā‘ith), purpose (gharaḍ), thought (khāṭir). The objector asks if any of these drove creation.
2
Identify the initial answer
The response says: 'He made the world for something of what you asked about.' This seems to concede a purpose, but it's a setup for the refutation.
The next lesson will show the evidence that this is impossible.
3
Understand the objector's demand for evidence
The objector asks for evidence, which Baqillani will provide. The lesson ends with that demand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في أن صنع الله للعالم ليس لغرض
فإن قال قائل : فهل تقولون إن صانع العالم صنعه بعد أن لم يصنعه لداع دعاه إلى فعله ومحرّك حرَّكه وباعث بعثه وغرض أزعجه وخاطر اقتضى وجود الحوادث منه أم صنعه لا لشيء مما سألت عنه؟ قيل له : إنه تعالى صنع العالم لشيء مما سألت عنه فإن قيل وما الدليل على ذلك؟ قيل : الدليل عليه أن الدواعي المزعجات والخواطر والأغراض إنما تكون وتجوز على ذي الحاجة الذي يصح منه اجتلابُ المنافع ودفع المضارّ وذلك أمر لا يجوز إلا على من جازت عليه الآلام واللذاتُ وميل الطبع والنفورُ وكل ذلك دليل على حَدَثِ من وصف به وحاجته إليه وهو مُنتَفٍ عن القديم تعالى، وكذلك الأسباب المزعجة المحركة الباعثة على الأفعال إنما تحرك الغافل وتنبه الجاهل وتخطر للخائف والراجي الذي يخاف الاستضرار بترك الأفعال بإيقاعها الصلاحَ والانتفاع والله يتعالى عن ذلك لأنه عالم بما يكون قبل أن يكون وبما تؤول إليه عواقب الأمور ويعلم السر وأخفى، ولا يجوز على من هذه صفته خطور الأمر بباله كالذي لم يكن عالماً به ولا أن تبعثه الدواعي والبواعتُ على أفعاله ؛ وذلك انا إذا قلنا لفاعلنا : ما الذي دعاك إلى الفعل وحرّكك على إيقاعه وما الغرض فيه دون غيره وفي فعله دون تركه والانصراف عنه ؟ فإنما نأله ليخبرنا أقل بذلك اجتلابَ منفعة أو دفع مضرة أم لا؛ فإن قال : فَعَلَه لا لاجتلاب منفعة ولا لدفع مَضَرَّة مع علمه بوقوعه وجب القضاء على تسفيهه لأنه ممن يحتاج إلى جرّ المنافع ودفع المضار وهو مأمور بذلك وإيقاعه الفعل عارياً من القصد إلى ذلك والتصدي له سَفَه وخلافٌ لما وجب عليه والقديم تعالى ليس بذي حاجة ولا ممن يلزمه الانقياد والطاعة؛ فلم يجز أن يقاس على فاعلنا.
Chapter: That God's making of the world is not for a purpose.
If someone says: Do you say that the Maker of the world made it after not making it due to a motive that called Him to His act, a mover that moved Him, a sender that sent Him, a purpose that disturbed Him, and a thought that necessitated the occurrence of originated things from Him, or did He make it not for any of what I asked about? It is said to him: He, exalted, made the world for something of what you asked about. If it is said: What is the evidence for that? It is said: The evidence for it is that disturbing motives, thoughts, and purposes only occur and are permissible for one who has need, from whom it is possible to seek benefits and repel harms. That is a matter only permissible for one whom pains and pleasures, natural inclination and aversion can befall; all of that is evidence for the originatedness of the one described by it and his need for it, and it is negated of the Eternal, exalted. Likewise, disturbing, moving, sending causes for actions only move the heedless, awaken the ignorant, and occur to the fearful and hopeful who fear harm from leaving actions by bringing about benefit and advantage. And God is exalted above that because He knows what will be before it is and what outcomes of matters will end up, and He knows the secret and what is more hidden. It is not permissible for one with this description that a matter should occur to his mind like someone who was not knowledgeable of it, nor that motives and senders should impel him to his actions. And that is because when we say to one who acts among us: What called you to the action and moved you to perform it, and what is the purpose in it rather than another, and in doing it rather than leaving it and turning away from it? We only ask him to tell us: Did you intend by that to bring a benefit or repel a harm, or not? If he said: I did it neither for gaining benefit nor for repelling harm, while knowing its occurrence, judgment must be passed on his foolishness, because he is one who needs to draw benefits and repel harms and is commanded to do so, and his performing the action devoid of intention for that and pursuit of it is foolishness and contrary to what is obligatory upon him. But the Eternal, exalted, is not a being of need nor one for whom compliance and obedience are obligatory. So it is not permissible that He be compared to one who acts among us.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a theological refutation of the claim that God created the world for a purpose (gharaḍ). Baqillani structures it as an objection-and-answer. The objector asks whether God created due to a motive (dā‘ī), mover (muḥarrik), sender (bā‘ith), purpose (gharaḍ), or thought (khāṭir). The answer is that He did create for something of those, but then the objector demands evidence. The evidence is that motives, thoughts, and purposes only apply to beings with need (dhū ḥāja) — beings who can seek benefit (manfa‘a) and repel harm (ḍarar). Such beings are subject to pain, pleasure, inclination, and aversion, all of which indicate originatedness (ḥudūth) and need. These are negated of the Eternal. Moreover, such causes only affect the heedless (ghāfil), ignorant (jāhil), fearful (khā’if), and hopeful (rājī). God, however, is knowing of all things before they occur and is not subject to these. When we ask a human agent why he acted, we are asking whether he sought benefit or avoided harm. If he did an act without any such purpose, he would be considered foolish (safīh) because he is in need of benefits and commanded to pursue them. But God has no such need and is not commanded. Therefore, God cannot be compared to human agents in this regard.

Key Terms
غرض
داعي
باعث
محرك
خاطر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Baqillani agrees that God acts for a purpose like humans do.
Correction: Baqillani's initial affirmation is a rhetorical device; he immediately provides evidence that such purposes are impossible for God.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani initially answer when asked if God created due to a motive, mover, purpose, or thought?
A) He created without any of those.
B) He created for something of what was asked.
C) He created for no reason at all.
D) He refused to answer.

Key Takeaway
The objection sets up the issue: if creation is not for a purpose, why do we speak of purpose in God's actions?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah did this for a purpose,' are you unconsciously imagining Allah needing something from His creation? What does this reveal about the limits of your own understanding? Will you commit to reminding yourself that Allah is Self-Sufficient (al-Ghanī) whenever you think of divine purpose?
?
The objector assumes that any action must have a motive like a human. Which of your own actions are you most likely to judge as pointless or purposeless? Are you willing to trust that Allah's wisdom is at work even when you see no motive? What would change in your life if you fully believed that?
?
Baqillani's response is deliberately vague—he says 'for something' without defining it. Are you comfortable with not knowing the full reasons behind Allah's decrees? Or does this discomfort reveal a desire to control God? Take a moment to repent from any attempt to confine Allah to your understanding.

5 min
Why Purpose Implies Need and Originatedness
Baqillani provides the evidence that purposes imply need and originatedness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that motives and purposes can only apply to beings with needs, which is incompatible with God's eternal nature.

Core Idea
Purpose requires need, and need requires originatedness.

In this lesson, Baqillani provides the evidence that the objector requested. He argues that disturbing motives only affect those who need to seek benefit and repel harm, which in turn requires being subject to pain, pleasure, and inclination. These are all signs of originatedness (ḥudūth) and need. Since God is eternal, He cannot have such attributes, so He cannot have a purpose in the human sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the evidence passage
Focus on the starting point: 'الدليل عليه أن الدواعي المزعجات...' and trace the argument through to the conclusion that these are negated of the Eternal.
2
Identify the connection between purpose and need
The text states that motives and purposes only occur for one who has need (dhū ḥāja) and can seek benefit and avoid harm.
3
Note the signs of originatedness
Pains, pleasures, inclination, aversion – these indicate that the being is originated and in need.
This is a key principle in Kalām: any attribute of need indicates contingency.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since God is Eternal (qadīm), He is free from all such attributes. Therefore, He cannot have motives or purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في أن صنع الله للعالم ليس لغرض
فإن قال قائل : فهل تقولون إن صانع العالم صنعه بعد أن لم يصنعه لداع دعاه إلى فعله ومحرّك حرَّكه وباعث بعثه وغرض أزعجه وخاطر اقتضى وجود الحوادث منه أم صنعه لا لشيء مما سألت عنه؟ قيل له : إنه تعالى صنع العالم لشيء مما سألت عنه فإن قيل وما الدليل على ذلك؟ قيل : الدليل عليه أن الدواعي المزعجات والخواطر والأغراض إنما تكون وتجوز على ذي الحاجة الذي يصح منه اجتلابُ المنافع ودفع المضارّ وذلك أمر لا يجوز إلا على من جازت عليه الآلام واللذاتُ وميل الطبع والنفورُ وكل ذلك دليل على حَدَثِ من وصف به وحاجته إليه وهو مُنتَفٍ عن القديم تعالى، وكذلك الأسباب المزعجة المحركة الباعثة على الأفعال إنما تحرك الغافل وتنبه الجاهل وتخطر للخائف والراجي الذي يخاف الاستضرار بترك الأفعال بإيقاعها الصلاحَ والانتفاع والله يتعالى عن ذلك لأنه عالم بما يكون قبل أن يكون وبما تؤول إليه عواقب الأمور ويعلم السر وأخفى، ولا يجوز على من هذه صفته خطور الأمر بباله كالذي لم يكن عالماً به ولا أن تبعثه الدواعي والبواعتُ على أفعاله ؛ وذلك انا إذا قلنا لفاعلنا : ما الذي دعاك إلى الفعل وحرّكك على إيقاعه وما الغرض فيه دون غيره وفي فعله دون تركه والانصراف عنه ؟ فإنما نأله ليخبرنا أقل بذلك اجتلابَ منفعة أو دفع مضرة أم لا؛ فإن قال : فَعَلَه لا لاجتلاب منفعة ولا لدفع مَضَرَّة مع علمه بوقوعه وجب القضاء على تسفيهه لأنه ممن يحتاج إلى جرّ المنافع ودفع المضار وهو مأمور بذلك وإيقاعه الفعل عارياً من القصد إلى ذلك والتصدي له سَفَه وخلافٌ لما وجب عليه والقديم تعالى ليس بذي حاجة ولا ممن يلزمه الانقياد والطاعة؛ فلم يجز أن يقاس على فاعلنا.
Chapter: That God's making of the world is not for a purpose.
If someone says: Do you say that the Maker of the world made it after not making it due to a motive that called Him to His act, a mover that moved Him, a sender that sent Him, a purpose that disturbed Him, and a thought that necessitated the occurrence of originated things from Him, or did He make it not for any of what I asked about? It is said to him: He, exalted, made the world for something of what you asked about. If it is said: What is the evidence for that? It is said: The evidence for it is that disturbing motives, thoughts, and purposes only occur and are permissible for one who has need, from whom it is possible to seek benefits and repel harms. That is a matter only permissible for one whom pains and pleasures, natural inclination and aversion can befall; all of that is evidence for the originatedness of the one described by it and his need for it, and it is negated of the Eternal, exalted. Likewise, disturbing, moving, sending causes for actions only move the heedless, awaken the ignorant, and occur to the fearful and hopeful who fear harm from leaving actions by bringing about benefit and advantage. And God is exalted above that because He knows what will be before it is and what outcomes of matters will end up, and He knows the secret and what is more hidden. It is not permissible for one with this description that a matter should occur to his mind like someone who was not knowledgeable of it, nor that motives and senders should impel him to his actions. And that is because when we say to one who acts among us: What called you to the action and moved you to perform it, and what is the purpose in it rather than another, and in doing it rather than leaving it and turning away from it? We only ask him to tell us: Did you intend by that to bring a benefit or repel a harm, or not? If he said: I did it neither for gaining benefit nor for repelling harm, while knowing its occurrence, judgment must be passed on his foolishness, because he is one who needs to draw benefits and repel harms and is commanded to do so, and his performing the action devoid of intention for that and pursuit of it is foolishness and contrary to what is obligatory upon him. But the Eternal, exalted, is not a being of need nor one for whom compliance and obedience are obligatory. So it is not permissible that He be compared to one who acts among us.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a theological refutation of the claim that God created the world for a purpose (gharaḍ). Baqillani structures it as an objection-and-answer. The objector asks whether God created due to a motive (dā‘ī), mover (muḥarrik), sender (bā‘ith), purpose (gharaḍ), or thought (khāṭir). The answer is that He did create for something of those, but then the objector demands evidence. The evidence is that motives, thoughts, and purposes only apply to beings with need (dhū ḥāja) — beings who can seek benefit (manfa‘a) and repel harm (ḍarar). Such beings are subject to pain, pleasure, inclination, and aversion, all of which indicate originatedness (ḥudūth) and need. These are negated of the Eternal. Moreover, such causes only affect the heedless (ghāfil), ignorant (jāhil), fearful (khā’if), and hopeful (rājī). God, however, is knowing of all things before they occur and is not subject to these. When we ask a human agent why he acted, we are asking whether he sought benefit or avoided harm. If he did an act without any such purpose, he would be considered foolish (safīh) because he is in need of benefits and commanded to pursue them. But God has no such need and is not commanded. Therefore, God cannot be compared to human agents in this regard.

Key Terms
حاجة
منفعة
مضرة
قديم
حادث
ملذة
ألم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's purpose is to benefit creation, implying that God needs to create to gain something.
Correction: Baqillani shows that any purpose implies need, which is impossible for God. God is self-sufficient and does not create for a benefit.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do disturbances (like motives and purposes) indicate about the being who experiences them?
A) That the being is powerful.
B) That the being is eternal.
C) That the being is originated and in need.
D) That the being is knowledgeable.

Key Takeaway
Any being that acts for a purpose must be in need and originated, which is impossible for the Eternal God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plead to Allah for help, do you subconsciously think He needs your worship or is moved by your desperation? How does this lesson challenge you to purify your understanding of Allah's self-sufficiency?
?
You often fear the consequences of neglecting religious duties. What does this passage say about the nature of such fear in relation to Allah? Does it reveal a hidden assumption that Allah is like a human who responds to fear and hope? How must your taqwa change?
?
If Allah's actions are not driven by any need or motive, why do you sometimes feel that your sins 'disappoint' Him or that your good deeds 'benefit' Him? What is the practical implication of this lesson for your daily relationship with Allah?

The Human Analogy and the Conclusion
Baqillani uses a human example to conclude that God cannot be compared to creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the analogy of human agents to show that a purposeless action is foolish for humans, but this does not apply to God.

Core Idea
A purposeless human act is foolish; God's act is beyond comparison.

Baqillani now draws an analogy with human agents. When we ask a person why they acted, we expect them to mention a benefit or harm avoidance. If they acted without any such purpose, we judge them as foolish. But God is not like humans: He has no needs, and He is not commanded to seek benefits. Therefore, the argument that God must have a purpose is invalid when applied to Him. This concludes the proof that God's creation is not for a purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the human analogy
Focus on the scenario: when we ask a human agent why they acted, we are asking about benefit or harm. If they say no purpose, they are foolish.
2
Understand the condition for humans
Humans need to seek benefits and avoid harms; they are commanded to do so. Acting without purpose is a violation.
3
Contrast with God
God has no need and is not under any command. Therefore, the analogy does not apply to Him.
4
Grasp the conclusion
It is not permissible to compare God to human agents in this regard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

The Logical Fallacy of Astrology
Baqillani denies any evidential link between celestial movements and earthly events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that astrology cannot provide genuine evidence for future events.

Core Idea
Astrology's claims lack rational evidence; celestial movements have no proven connection to earthly events.

Baqillani begins by attacking the very foundation of astrological claims. He points out that while some proofs have clear connections to their conclusions, the movements of stars and planets have no such link to events like rain, prices, or war. This lesson shows his logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify astrological claims
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note what events astrologers claim to predict.
Pay attention to the list: rains, growth, decay, prices, bloodshed, etc.
2
Analyze Baqillani's argument
Notice Baqillani's assertion that there is 'no way by which it is known' (لا وجه من قبله يُعْلَم) connecting celestial motions to these events.
This is a direct denial of any evidential link.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider what it means for a claim to have no known evidence. How does this undermine astrology?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على صدق صاحبها وأمثال ذلك مما قد عُرف جهة تعلق الدليل فيه بمدلوله ولا وجه من قبله يُعْلَم لِدَلالة كون هذه الأفلاك في البروج وسيرها وحركاتها على حدوث ما يحدث من الأمطار والنماء والنقصان وغلاء الأسعار وسفك الدماء وسكون الهيج والفساد وعلى ما يَسْتَسِر الناسُ بعلمه وما ينطوون عليه . وقد أخبر الله تعالى عن كذب مُدَّعى علم ذلك، وأنه تعالى المستبد بعلم ما كان ويكون فقال : «وأنبئكم بما تَأْكُلُونَ وَمَا تَدْخِرُونَ فِي بيوتكم» فجعل ذلك من دليل النبوة وما لا يطلع عليه إلا من أوجي به إليه وقال : إِنَّ اللَّهَ عِنْدَهُ عِلْمُ السَّاعَةِ ويُنَزِّلُ الغَيْثَ وَيَعْلَمُ مَا فِي الأَرْحَامِ وَمَا تَدْرِي نَفْسٌ ماذا تَكْسِبُ غَداً وَمَا تَدْرِي نَفْسٌ بِأَيِّ أَرْضِ تَمُوتُ ) وقال : ( عالم الغيب فلا يظهر على غيبه أحداً إلا ارتضى من رسول ) وفي نظائر هذه الآيات ما يدل على أن علم ما يكون لا يدركه إلا علام الغيوب أو من أطْلَعَهُ على ذلك ; فكيف يُدْرَك ذلك بقطع الأفلاك وسير النجوم؟ وكيف يجتمع في قلب مؤمن تصديق الرسل وتصحيح الآيات اعتقاد تصحيح أحكام المنجمين واعتقاد كون سير الأفلاك أدلة على علم ما كان ويكون ؟ وقد روي عن النبي، أنه قال : ( من أتى كاهناً أو عرّافاً ـ وفي بعضها : أو منجماً - فقد كفر بما أنزل على قلب محمد ) صلَّى الله عليه في أمثال لهذه الرواية يطول ذكرها
…upon the truthfulness of its possessor and the like of that by which the aspect of the connection of the evidence to its signification is known, and there is no way by which it is known from the indication (dalāla) of the existence of these spheres in the constellations and their course and movements upon the occurrence of what occurs of rains and growth and decay and rise of prices and shedding of blood and cessation of strife and corruption, and upon what people conceal by its knowledge and what they hide. And Allah the Exalted has informed of the lie of the claimant of knowledge of that, and that He the Exalted alone has knowledge of what was and will be, and He said: “And I will inform you of what you eat and what you store up in your houses.” So He made that a proof of prophethood and what none is informed of except one to whom revelation is given. And He said: “Indeed Allah, with Him is knowledge of the Hour, and He sends down the rain, and knows what is in the wombs, and no soul knows what it will earn tomorrow, and no soul knows in what land it will die.” And He said: “Knower of the unseen, and He does not disclose His unseen to anyone except whom He approves of as a messenger.” And in similar verses there is indication that knowledge of what will be is not attained except by the Knower of the unseen (‛allām al-ghuyūb) or one whom He has informed of that. So how can that be attained by the positions of the spheres and the stars? And how can there come together in the heart of a believer belief in the messengers and confirmation of the verses with the belief in confirming the judgments of astrologers and the belief that the course of the spheres are evidences for knowledge of what was and will be? And it has been narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: “Whoever goes to a soothsayer or fortune-teller—and in some versions: or an astrologer—has disbelieved in what was revealed to Muhammad,” peace be upon him, and similar narrations of which the mention would be lengthy.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of astrology and an affirmation that knowledge of the unseen belongs solely to Allah. The author begins by referencing evidence whose connection to its conclusion is known, then describes the claim that celestial movements indicate events like rain, prices, war, etc. Allah has declared such claimants liars, reserving knowledge of past and future for Himself. Three Quranic verses are cited: from Surah Al-Imran (49) where Jesus says he can inform people of their food and storage as a proof of prophethood; from Surah Luqman (34) listing five keys of the unseen; and from Surah Al-Jinn (26-27) that Allah discloses His unseen only to chosen messengers. These prove that such knowledge is exclusive to Allah or those He informs. Baqillani then asks rhetorically how one can attain this through astrology, and how a believer can reconcile faith in the prophets with belief in astrological judgments. Finally, he cites a hadith that visiting a soothsayer or astrologer constitutes disbelief, emphasizing the severity.

Key Terms
دليل
المنجمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Astrology is a valid science that can predict events.
Correction: Baqillani argues there is no known rational connection between celestial movements and earthly occurrences.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani say about the connection between celestial spheres and events like rains and prices?
A) There is a known connection
B) There is no way to know the connection
C) The connection is confirmed by revelation
D) The connection is known only to prophets

Key Takeaway
Astrology lacks any rational evidence linking stars to events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever made a decision—big or small—based on a horoscope or astrological sign? How does Baqillani's argument that 'there is no way by which it is known' challenge the foundation of that choice?
?
In what areas of your daily life do you subconsciously rely on omens or superstitions (e.g., 'Mercury in retrograde') instead of trusting Allah's wisdom? Will you consciously reject such unproven links after this lesson?
?
When a friend says 'the stars are aligned' for success or failure, what specific remark from Baqillani's refutation can you use to gently correct them? Commit to saying it the next time the occasion arises.

Quranic Verses on Knowledge of the Unseen
Baqillani cites Quran to prove unseen knowledge belongs to Allah alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran reserves knowledge of the unseen exclusively to Allah, except for prophets He chooses.

Core Idea
Only Allah knows the unseen; prophets only know what Allah reveals.

After refuting astrology rationally, Baqillani turns to divine revelation. He cites three Quranic verses that emphasize Allah's exclusive knowledge of the unseen, including future events. This lesson examines those verses and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three Quranic verses
Read the passage and locate the three distinct Quranic citations. Note the content of each.
They are from Surat Al-Imran, Luqman, and Al-Jinn.
2
Analyze the first verse
The verse from Al-Imran (49) where Jesus says he can inform people of their food and storage. Baqillani says this is a proof of prophethood.
3
Analyze the second and third verses
Surah Luqman (34) lists five keys of the unseen. Surah Al-Jinn (26-27) says Allah reveals unseen only to chosen messengers.
These verses clearly show exclusive knowledge.
4
Understand the conclusion
Baqillani concludes that such knowledge is only from Allah or those He informs. This directly refutes astrology.

Source Text

…upon the truthfulness of its possessor and the like of that by which the aspect of the connection of the evidence to its signification is known, and there is no way by which it is known from the indication (dalāla) of the existence of these spheres in the constellations and their course and movements upon the occurrence of what occurs of rains and growth and decay and rise of prices and shedding of blood and cessation of strife and corruption, and upon what people conceal by its knowledge and what they hide. And Allah the Exalted has informed of the lie of the claimant of knowledge of that, and that He the Exalted alone has knowledge of what was and will be, and He said: “And I will inform you of what you eat and what you store up in your houses.” So He made that a proof of prophethood and what none is informed of except one to whom revelation is given. And He said: “Indeed Allah, with Him is knowledge of the Hour, and He sends down the rain, and knows what is in the wombs, and no soul knows what it will earn tomorrow, and no soul knows in what land it will die.” And He said: “Knower of the unseen, and He does not disclose His unseen to anyone except whom He approves of as a messenger.” And in similar verses there is indication that knowledge of what will be is not attained except by the Knower of the unseen (‛allām al-ghuyūb) or one whom He has informed of that. So how can that be attained by the positions of the spheres and the stars? And how can there come together in the heart of a believer belief in the messengers and confirmation of the verses with the belief in confirming the judgments of astrologers and the belief that the course of the spheres are evidences for knowledge of what was and will be? And it has been narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: “Whoever goes to a soothsayer or fortune-teller—and in some versions: or an astrologer—has disbelieved in what was revealed to Muhammad,” peace be upon him, and similar narrations of which the mention would be lengthy.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of astrology and an affirmation that knowledge of the unseen belongs solely to Allah. The author begins by referencing evidence whose connection to its conclusion is known, then describes the claim that celestial movements indicate events like rain, prices, war, etc. Allah has declared such claimants liars, reserving knowledge of past and future for Himself. Three Quranic verses are cited: from Surah Al-Imran (49) where Jesus says he can inform people of their food and storage as a proof of prophethood; from Surah Luqman (34) listing five keys of the unseen; and from Surah Al-Jinn (26-27) that Allah discloses His unseen only to chosen messengers. These prove that such knowledge is exclusive to Allah or those He informs. Baqillani then asks rhetorically how one can attain this through astrology, and how a believer can reconcile faith in the prophets with belief in astrological judgments. Finally, he cites a hadith that visiting a soothsayer or astrologer constitutes disbelief, emphasizing the severity.

The Incompatibility of Faith and Astrology
Baqillani asserts that faith and astrology cannot coexist and cites a hadith on kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why believing in astrology contradicts faith in the prophets and the Quran, and the severe warning given by the Prophet.

Core Idea
Believing in astrology is incompatible with being a believer; it amounts to disbelief.

Baqillani presents a powerful rhetorical challenge: how can a heart that believes in the messengers and the Quran also accept astrological judgments? He then cites the hadith that visiting a soothsayer or astrologer is equivalent to disbelief. This lesson shows the ultimate incompatibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the rhetorical questions

The questions are: 'How can that be attained?' and 'How can there come together...'
2
Examine the hadith
Read the hadith carefully. Note the different versions mentioned (soothsayer, fortune-teller, astrologer).
The hadith declares that such actions constitute disbelief.
3
Understand the severity
Baqillani uses this hadith to show that astrology is not a minor sin but a matter of kufr.
This is the strongest possible condemnation.

Why Thought and Doubt Cannot Apply to God
Refutes the claim that the devil originated from God's thought, doubt, or punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why thought and doubt are impossible for God and how this refutes claims about the origin of the devil.

Core Idea
Thought and doubt imply deficiency, so they are impossible for the Eternal God.

This passage addresses a claim from dualists that the devil originated from God's thought, doubt, or punishment. Imam Baqillani refutes this by establishing that thought and doubt are defects that cannot be ascribed to God, just as ignorance and death cannot. The argument shows that if God were to think or doubt, He would not be able to know or create perfect actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: claim, objection, answer.
The passage is a concise theological argument.
2
Identify the claim and objection
Find the dualist claim that the devil comes from God's thought, doubt, or punishment. Then see the question posed to the theologian.
3
Understand the proof against thought and doubt
The author says thought and doubt are impossible for God just like ignorance, death, and other defects. Reflect on why these are considered defects.
Defects indicate deficiency and temporality.
4
See the consequence of the conditional argument
If God were thinking or doubting, He could not know or produce perfect actions. Since perfect actions exist, the premise is false.
This is a logical proof by contradiction.
5
Connect to divine transcendence
This passages upholds God's transcendence above any imperfections. Consider how this defends tawhid against dualist misconceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مِنْ عُقُوبَةٍ عَاقَبَ اللهُ بِهَا سُبْحَانَهُ وَتَعَالَى هُوَ الشَّيْطَانُ. إِنْ قَالَ مِنْهُمْ قَائِلٌ: لِمَ أَنْكَرْتُمْ أَنْ يُحْدِثَ فِعْلُ اللهِ مِنْ أَوْ غَيْرِهِ مِنْ فِكْرَةٍ فَكَّرَهَا أَوْ شَكَّةٍ شَكَّهَا أَوْ عُقُوبَةٍ عَاقَبَ بِهَا؟ قِيلَ لَهُ: لِقِيَامِ الدَّلِيلِ عَلَى اسْتِحَالَةِ الْفِكْرِ وَالشَّكِّ عَلَى الْقَدِيمِ كَمَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ الْجَهْلُ وَالْمَوْتُ وَالْغَفْلَةُ وَالنَّوْمُ وَغَيْرُ ذَلِكَ مِنَ الْآفَاتِ الدَّالَّةِ عَلَى نَقْصِ مَنْ جَازَتْ عَلَيْهِ وَحُدُوثِهِ، وَلَأَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ سُبْحَانَهُ فِي أَزَلِهِ مُفَكِّراً مُرْتَاباً شَاكّاً لَاسْتَحَالَ أَنْ يَعْلَمَ وَأَنْ تَقَعَ مِنْهُ الْأَفْعَالُ الْمُحْكَمَةُ الدَّالَّةُ عَلَى الْعِلْمِ وَالْقَصْدِ، وَذَلِكَ بَاطِلٌ بِمَا قَدَّمْنَاهُ.
From a punishment with which God, exalted be He, punished, that is the devil. If one of them says: Why do you deny that the action of God — or something else from a thought that He thought or a doubt that He doubted or a punishment with which He punished — produces? It is said to him: Because the evidence stands for the impossibility of thought and doubt for the Eternal, just as ignorance, death, heedlessness, sleep, and other defects that indicate the deficiency and temporality of the one to whom they occur are impossible for Him, and because if He, exalted be He, were in His pre-eternity thinking, doubting, or skeptical, it would be impossible for Him to know and for perfected actions indicating knowledge and intention to proceed from Him, and that is false by what we have already presented.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist claim that the devil originated from God's punishment, thought, or doubt. The author begins by stating the claim: the devil comes from a punishment of God. Then he presents the objection: why do you (theologians) deny that God's action — or something else like a thought, doubt, or punishment — could produce the devil? The answer is a proof: the impossibility of thought and doubt for the Eternal (al-Qadim). The author argues that just as ignorance, death, heedlessness, sleep, and other defects are impossible for God — because they indicate deficiency and temporality in the one who experiences them — similarly, thought and doubt are impossible for Him. Moreover, if God were thinking, doubting, or skeptical in His pre-eternity (azal), it would be impossible for Him to know and to produce the perfectly crafted actions that indicate knowledge and intention. Such actions are undeniable in creation, so the premise is false. This concludes the argument.

Key Terms
الشَّكُّ
الفِكْرُ
القَدِيمُ
الحُدُوثُ
الآفَاتُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the devil originated from God's thought, doubt, or punishment.
Correction: Thought and doubt are defects incompatible with God's perfection and eternality, so He cannot be subject to them. The existence of perfect actions from God proves He knows without needing thought.

Checkpoint
Why is thought and doubt impossible for God according to the author?
A) Because God is too great to think.
B) Because they imply deficiency and temporality, like ignorance and death.
C) Because thought and doubt are created things.
D) Because God is eternal, and thought is temporal.

Key Takeaway
Thought and doubt are defects that indicate deficiency and temporality, so they are impossible for the perfect, eternal God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself doubting God's wisdom in your life, do you realize that such doubt is a defect that cannot be attributed to God? Yet you allow it to fester in your heart. Will you repent from this and affirm His perfect knowledge, even when you don't understand?
?
The passage argues that thought and doubt would make God unable to produce perfected actions. Look at the intricate creation around you—does that not silence every doubt about God's perfection? Question: When you see the order in the universe, do you still entertain doubts about God's attributes? What concrete step will you take to train your mind to reject such thoughts?
?
The devil is said to originate from a punishment, but you might be tempted to think God's actions are arbitrary. This passage reminds you that God's actions are perfectly wise and intentional. So, when you face trials, do you trust that they are from a God who never thinks or doubts, but acts with perfect knowledge? Or do you complain? Examine your heart: do you truly believe that God's actions are devoid of any deficiency? If not, what will you change today?

Divine Transcendence

God's Transcendence over Deficiencies
Bāqillānī argues that death, sleep, doubt, etc. prove deficiency and cannot apply to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Bāqillānī argues that attributes like death, sleep, and doubt cannot apply to God, and how this establishes His perfection.

Core Idea
Imperfections like death and doubt prove contingency, impossible for the Necessary Being.

In this passage, Imam al-Bāqillānī presents a concise argument for God's transcendence over all forms of deficiency. He lists common human afflictions—death, sleep, heedlessness—and shows that they indicate a being's contingency and imperfection. He then extends the logic to mental states like doubt, demonstrating that God's perfect knowledge and perfectly measured actions preclude any such deficiencies. This short but powerful argument grounds God's transcendence in rational necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the original passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the concise, sequential structure: first a list of deficiencies, then a conditional argument, then the conclusion.
The reference 'بما قدمناه' points to earlier proofs; for now, focus on the logic presented here.
2
Identify the first proof: deficiencies
Find the words for death, heedlessness, sleep. Understand why Bāqillānī says they indicate نقص (deficiency) and حدوث (contingency). Ask yourself: why do these attributes necessarily imply imperfection?
Deficiencies are incompatible with the necessary, eternal nature of God.
3
Analyze the conditional argument
Examine the phrase 'لو كان سبحانه في أوله متفكرا مرتابا شاكا'. Bāqillānī argues that if God were ever thinking, doubting, or uncertain, then it would be impossible for Him to have certain knowledge and to produce perfectly measured actions. Why? Because doubt is incompatible with certain knowledge.
The conditional shows that doubt would undermine the very attributes that prove God's existence and perfection.
4
Connect to God's actions
Note the phrase 'الأفعال المحكمة الدالة على العلم والقصد'. Bāqillānī points to the observable world: perfectly ordered actions (like creation) are proof of knowledge and intent. If God were deficient, He could not produce such perfection. Therefore, deficiencies are impossible for Him.
This is a simple but powerful cosmological argument linking God's attributes to His effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والموت والغفلة والنوم وغير ذلك من الآفات الدالة على نقص من جازت عليه وحدوثه ولأنه لو كان سبحانه في أوله متفكرا مرتابا شاكا لاستحال أن يعلم وأن تقع منه الأفعال المحكمة الدالة على العلم والقصد وذلك باطل بما قدمناه
And death, heedlessness, sleep, and other such afflictions indicate deficiency and temporality of the one to whom they occur. And because if He, exalted, were in His beginning thinking, doubting, or uncertain, it would be impossible for Him to know and for perfectly measured actions indicating knowledge and intent to occur from Him. And that is invalid by what we have already presented.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by Bāqillānī that God cannot be characterized by deficiencies like death, sleep, or heedlessness, because such attributes indicate that the one possessing them is contingent and imperfect. He then extends the argument: if God were ever subject to thinking, doubt, or uncertainty (even at the beginning of His existence, as some might claim), then He could not have certain knowledge, and His actions would not be perfectly measured and indicative of knowledge and intent. Since we know God's actions are perfectly measured, such deficiencies are impossible for Him. Bāqillānī refers to previous arguments (بما قدمناه) for the impossibility of these states in God.

Key Terms
الآفات
نقص
حدوث
مفكر
مرتاب
شاك
أفعال محكمة
العلم
القصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God could experience doubt or uncertainty before creating the universe, since He was 'alone' and had not yet acted.
Correction: Bāqillānī shows that doubt is incompatible with knowledge and perfect action. If God ever doubted, He could not be all-knowing, and His actions would not be perfectly measured. Since His actions are perfect, doubt is impossible for Him at any time.

Checkpoint
What does Bāqillānī argue about death, sleep, and heedlessness in relation to God?
A) They prove that God is beyond all deficiency.
B) They show that God could have experienced them in the beginning.
C) They indicate that God is subject to time.
D) They are irrelevant to God's nature.

Key Takeaway
Bāqillānī shows that any deficiency, whether physical (death, sleep) or intellectual (doubt), is incompatible with God's necessary perfection and is disproven by the perfect order of His actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage declares that doubt is incompatible with God's perfection. When you face doubts about your faith, do you see them as a reflection of your own limited understanding, rather than a flaw in God? What concrete step will you take this week to anchor yourself in certainty about God's attributes?
?
Bāqillānī points to the perfect order of the universe as proof of God's knowledge and intent. Look at the areas of your life that feel chaotic or unjust. Can you still trust that there is a wise purpose behind them? How will you remind yourself of God's perfect plan when your circumstances seem disordered?
?
The lesson teaches that even subtle defects like sleep indicate contingency and are impossible for God. Every night you sleep reminds you of your own weakness and need. Does this awareness of your contingency drive you to depend more on the Necessary Being? What is one routine you can change to actively acknowledge your reliance on Him?

Refuting Three Claims About the Origin of Doubt
Baqillani refutes three claims about the origin of shakk (doubt).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses logical dilemmas to refute three false claims about the origin of doubt, thought, and punishment, and why each claim leads to absurdity or contradiction.

Core Idea
Three wrong answers about the origin of doubt all collapse under rational scrutiny.

Baqillani presents a logical dilemma to opponents who claim that doubt (shakk), thought, and punishment are originated. He forces them to choose between three possible origins: no originator, shaytan, or God. Then he demolishes each option with a clear rational argument, showing that only the correct Islamic understanding of God as the sole Creator remains consistent. This passage demonstrates the power of dialectical reasoning (jadal) in classical kalam.

Guided Path
1
Follow the dialectical flow
Read the passage carefully, noting how Baqillani presents a dilemma with three branches. Understand that he starts from the opponents' claim and then asks a key question that forces a choice.
The structure is: if you say doubt is originated, then is it from an originator or not?
2
Identify the three refuted claims
Find the three possible answers: (1) no originator, (2) shaytan, (3) God. Notice how each is refuted in turn.
The refutations are sequential and each builds on the previous.
3
Examine the first refutation
If they say 'no originator,' Baqillani says this leads to ta'til (suspension of causality) and negating the Maker entirely. This shows the absurdity of denying an originator.
Ta'til is the view that events occur without any cause.
4
Examine the second refutation
If they say 'shaytan,' Baqillani points out that shaytan existed before the doubt and thought he supposedly caused. This is a temporal contradiction: the effect cannot precede its cause.
This refutation relies on the principle that a cause must exist before its effect.
5
Examine the third refutation
If they say 'God,' Baqillani asks whether doubt is good or evil. Both answers lead to contradictions: if good, how can evil (shaytan) come from it? If evil, then God created evil, which the opponents deny, yet they cannot provide a consistent distinction.
This refutation destroys dualist claims that good and evil come from different sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا : إن الشَّكَ مُحْدَثٌ وكذلك العقوبة والفكر عند القائل بكل واحد منهما قيل لهم : أفمن محدثٍ حدث الشك أم لا من محدث؟ فإن قالوا : لا من محدث، قيل لهم : فما يؤمنكم أن يكون سائر الأفعال والحوادث كائنة لا من محدث؟ وفي ذلك التعطيل وإبطال الصانع . وإن قالوا من محدث حدثت هذه الأمور، قيل لهم فمن محدثها؟ فإن قالوا الشيطان تجاهلوا وقيل لهم : فقد كان الشيطان قبل الفكر والشك اللذين كان عنهما ؛ فكيف يكون الشيء قبل أصله وسببه الذي عنه كان ووجد ؟ . وإن قالوا : الله أحدث الشك والفكرة قيل لهم : فخبرونا عن الشك والفكرة أشر هما أم خير؛ فإن قالوا خير قيل لهم : فكيف كان عنهما الشيطان الذي هو شرٌّ؟ وما أنكرتم، إن جاز ذلك، أن يفكر الشيطان الذي هو شر، فكراً هو شر يتولد عنه ويقع الخير؟ وإن جاز ذلك جاز وقوع التبريد عن النار والتسخين عن الثلج ؛ وهذا نقض قولهم، وإن قالوا بأن الشك شرّ ؛ لأنه ولّد الشيطان الذي هو شرّ . قيل لهم : فقد فعل الله الخير الشكَّ الذي هو شر من الشر، وهو أصل الشيطان ؛ وإن جاز ذلك ، فلِمَ لا يجوز أن يفعل سائر الشرور وجميع الأشخاص الضارة من السباع والعقارب والحيات والهموم والأحزان وسائر الشرور؟ وما الفصل في ذلك؟ ولا فصل فيه .
And if they say: 'Doubt (al-shakk) is originated (muhdath), and likewise punishment (al-uquba) and thought (al-fikr) according to the one who holds each of them,' it is said to them: 'Is doubt originated from an originator (muhdith) or not from an originator?' If they say: 'Not from an originator,' it is said to them: 'What prevents you that all actions and events happen not from an originator? In that is ta'til (suspension of causality) and negation of the Maker (ibṭāl al-ṣāniʿ).' And if they say: 'From an originator these things originated,' it is said to them: 'Who originated them?' If they say: 'Shaytan,' they are being ignorant, and it is said to them: 'But shaytan existed before the thought and doubt from which he originated; so how can a thing be before its origin and cause from which it was and existed?' And if they say: 'God originated doubt and thought,' it is said to them: 'Inform us about doubt and thought: are they evil (sharr) or good (khayr)?' If they say: 'Good,' it is said to them: 'How then from them came shaytan who is evil? And what prevents, if that is allowed, that shaytan who is evil thinks an evil thought which generates evil, and good occurs? And if that is allowed, then cooling from fire and heating from ice would be allowed; and this invalidates their statement.' And if they say that doubt is evil because it generated shaytan who is evil, it is said to them: 'Then God did good (khayr) by creating doubt which is evil from evil, and it is the origin of shaytan. And if that is allowed, why is it not allowed that He creates all evils and all harmful entities like predators, scorpions, snakes, sorrows, griefs, and all evils? And what distinction is there in that? There is no distinction in it.'
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of various claims about the origin of doubt (shakk), thought (fikr), and punishment ('uquba). Baqillani presents a dilemma with three possible answers: the opponent claims these are originated (muhdath). Then he asks: are they from an originator or not? If they say not from an originator, that leads to ta'til (suspending agency) and negating the Maker altogether. If they say from an originator, then who? If they say shaytan, Baqillani points out that shaytan existed before the doubt and thought, so how can the effect precede its cause? If they say God, then he asks whether doubt and thought are good or evil. If they say good, how can shaytan (evil) come from good? And if good can come from evil, then cooling could come from fire, which is absurd. If they say doubt is evil because it produced shaytan, then God created evil to produce evil, which would also imply God creates all evil, but they deny that. So there is no consistent distinction. The argument refutes dualist views that separate good and evil creator.

Key Terms
الشك (al-shakk: doubt)
الشيطان (al-shaytan: the devil)
محدث (muhdath: originated)
محدث (muhdith: originator)
التعطيل (al-ta'til: suspension of causality)
إبطال الصانع (ibṭāl al-ṣāniʿ: negation of the Maker)
التبريد (al-tabrid: cooling)
التسخين (al-taskhin: heating)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Evil requires a separate creator (dualist view) because good cannot come from the same source as evil.
Correction: Baqillani shows that attributing evil to God does not create a logical contradiction; the opponent cannot consistently deny that God could create evil while accepting that He created doubt which is evil.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the opponent says doubt originates from no originator?
A) It leads to affirming God as creator.
B) It leads to ta'til (suspension of causality) and negation of the Maker.
C) It leads to affirming that shaytan is the originator.
D) It leads to affirming that doubt is good.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's dialectical argument refutes all three possible origins for doubt, demonstrating that only the Islamic tawhidic view of God as the sole Creator remains logically consistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience doubt about your faith, do you immediately blame shaytan or external factors? This lesson shows that such an explanation is logically incoherent. How will you now take personal responsibility for your doubts instead of externalizing them?
?
Baqillani's argument forces you to confront the possibility that your own thoughts—even evil ones—ultimately stem from Allah. Does this bring you humility or discomfort? How will you respond to your own sinful thoughts now, knowing they don't have an independent source?
?
If you truly believe that Allah is the sole creator of all things, including the evil that befalls you, how must your reaction to trials change? Will you still complain, or will you submit completely? Commit to a specific action the next time you face a hardship.

Proof that God is neither light nor darkness
Baqillani concludes a proof against dualists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God is neither light nor darkness, and how the contingency of light leads to this conclusion.

Core Idea
Light is created; the Creator cannot be light.

Imam Baqillani here presents the conclusion of a powerful argument against the dualist claim that God is light. By using the dualists' own premise—that light is an observable event—he demonstrates that light is created, and therefore cannot be the uncreated Creator. This short passage encapsulates a key principle of Islamic theology: God's transcendence beyond physical attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and its literal translation. Notice the compact style of the author.
The passage is only two lines but contains a full argument.
2
Identify the argument's structure
Look for what the author claims he demonstrated ('we showed them') and the conclusion drawn from it.
The key phrase is 'we showed them... the creation of light'.
3
Understand the reasoning
The author uses light itself and something stronger to prove light is created. Why does he need a stronger proof? Possibly because the dualists might accept certain evidence about light.
The 'stronger' evidence could be the sun or another clear contingency.
4
Apply the conclusion
Reflect on why this matters: if God were light, He would be created, which is impossible. So God transcends all physical elements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أريناهم به وبما هو أقوى منه حَدَث النور، وبينا بذلك أن الله تعالى ليس بنور ولا ظلام .
We showed them by it and by what is stronger than it the creation of light, and we made clear by that that God Almighty is not light nor darkness.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the conclusion of a proof against the Zoroastrian dualists (Majus) who claim that God is light and that darkness is an opposite principle. The author states that they demonstrated to them by means of light itself—and by something even more evident than light—that light is created (hadath al-nur). From this, they deduced that God, being the Creator, cannot be light or darkness, since both are contingent beings. The argument relies on a premise acceptable to the dualists: that light is an event (hadith) in the observed world. Thus, if light is created, it cannot be the uncreated God. The same applies to darkness. The passage is succinctly self-contained: it states the method and the conclusion.

Key Terms
حدث (creation/event)
النور (light)
الظلام (darkness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the author demonstrate about light?
A) That it is eternal and uncreated.
B) That it is created (hadath).
C) That it is part of God's essence.
D) That it is darkness in disguise.

Key Takeaway
God is neither light nor darkness because both are created, and the Creator cannot be among His creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is neither light nor darkness, what mental images or attributes have you been subconsciously associating with Allah that are actually from creation? Are you willing to let go of those images and worship Him as He truly is, beyond your imagination?
?
The dualists believed in a god who is material light. Have you ever reduced Allah to a concept or feeling that you prefer, rather than submitting to the unseen Lord who is absolutely transcendent? Confess any such tendency and commit to purifying your tawheed today.
?
This lesson shows that even something as ethereal as light is created. What other 'good' things in your life do you mistakenly think are independent of Allah or possess divine-like power? Repent of attributing any part of Allah's dominion to creation.
