The Inconsistency of Analogy from the Witnessed World
Baqillani presents two reductio arguments against analogical reasoning for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani refutes the claim that God can be understood by analogy to the witnessed world through two reductio arguments.

Core Idea
Analogy from the temporal world either makes God temporal or reduces Him to a substance.

Imam al-Baqillani confronts those who apply qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy from the unseen to the witnessed) to God. He shows that if consistently applied, such analogy forces God to be temporal or a substance with accidents — both absurd. This passage presents two powerful reductio arguments that demolish the claim of anthropomorphists who rely on analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and its translation. Notice how Baqillani uses a question-and-response structure.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two arguments
The first argument focuses on temporal existence; the second on the nature of substance. Trace each step.
3
Understand the reductio
For each argument, see how Baqillani forces his opponent into a dilemma: either accept the absurd conclusion (God is temporal or material) or reject their own method.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Baqillani ends by asserting that God is neither substance nor accident, nor like witnessed existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشاهد ثم يقال لهم أفليس قد اتفقنا على أنه لا موجود معلوم في والمعقول إلا محدث موجود عن عدم فإذا قالوا أجل قيل لهم فيجب أن يكون صانع العالم جل ذكره موجودا محدثا قياسا على الشاهد فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا مذهبهم وإن أبوه نقضوا دليلهم ثم يقال لهم فهل وجدتم جوهرا في الشاهد إلا متحيزا قابلا للأعراض من جنس هذه الجواهر المعقولة فإذا قالوا بلى قيل لهم فيجب عليكم إذا كان القديم تعالى جوهرا أن يكون كالجواهر المعقولة ومن جنسها وقابلا للأعراض كقبولها فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا دينهم وإن أبوه قيل لهم فما أنكرتم أن يكون القديم سبحانه موجودا ليس بجوهر ولا عرض ولا كالموجودات في الشاهد كما أنه ليس كالجواهر ولا فصل في ذلك أبدا
The witnessed. Then it is said to them: Did we not agree that there is no known existent in the witnessed and the intellectual except a temporal existent from non-existence? So if they say 'Yes', it is said to them: Then it is necessary that the Maker of the world, may His mention be exalted, be a temporal existent, by analogy to the witnessed. If they pass over that, they abandon their doctrine, and if they reject it, they invalidate their proof. Then it is said to them: Did you find any substance in the witnessed except that it is spatially extended, receptive of accidents, of the genus of these intellectual substances? So if they say 'Yes', it is said to them: Then it is incumbent upon you, if the Eternal, exalted is He, is a substance, that He be like the intellectual substances and of their genus and receptive of accidents as they are receptive. If they pass over that, they abandon their religion, and if they reject it, it is said to them: Why do you not deny that the Eternal, glorified is He, is an existent not a substance, not an accident, and not like the existents in the witnessed, just as He is not like substances? And there is no distinction in that ever.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by reductio ad absurdum of those who claim that God is a substance by analogy with the witnessed world (shahid). The author presents two arguments. First: He asks if they agree that every known existent in the witnessed and intellectual realms is temporal (created from non-existence). If they agree, then by their own method of analogy (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid), the Creator would also have to be temporal. If they accept this, they abandon their doctrine (which holds God eternal); if they reject it, they invalidate their own proof. Second: He asks if they have found any substance in the witnessed except that it is spatially extended (mutahayyiz) and receptive of accidents. If they agree, then if God were a substance, He would necessarily be like those substances, implying temporality. If they accept this, they abandon their religion; if they reject it, they are asked: Why then do you not admit that God is an existent that is neither substance nor accident, nor like the witnessed existents, just as He is not like substances? The author concludes that there is no distinction (fasl) that would prevent this.

Key Terms
الشاهد
المحدث
الجوهر
العرض
القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage endorses analogy from the witnessed world to God.
Correction: Baqillani shows that applying analogy leads to absurdities, thus disproving its validity.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, if all witnessed existents are temporal, what follows by analogy?
A) The Creator must be eternal
B) The Creator must be temporal
C) The Creator is not a substance
D) The Creator is not an accident

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that applying analogy from the witnessed world to God leads to absurd conclusions, thereby preserving divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining God in human terms—like a powerful king on a throne, or a father with human emotions? After reading Baqillani's argument, do you see the danger in such analogies? Will you consciously reject those images and affirm that God is unlike anything you know?
?
When you pray to Allah, do you feel a distance because you cannot picture Him? Or do you fill that gap with mental images that reduce Him? What uncomfortable change must you make in your heart to truly worship a God you cannot compare to anything?
?
Baqillani says there is no distinction that allows analogy to hold. Is your soul satisfied with a God who is wholly other? Or do you secretly wish for a deity you can relate to through human terms? If you are honest, what do you need to let go of to embrace pure tanzih?

Error in Division and the Body Objection
Correction of division and presentation of the body objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opponents' division between body and substance is flawed and how they argue that God cannot be a body based on the assumed link between body and temporality.

Core Idea
Not every self-subsistent thing is a simple substance; some are composite bodies.

After establishing the basic rational judgments, Sanusi now turns to correct a common error in dividing existents. He points out that among self-subsistent, noble, active things, there are two types: composite bodies and simple substances. He then presents the opponents' objection against God being a body, which sets the stage for a deeper discussion of divine transcendence. This passage sharpens the conceptual framework for understanding what can and cannot be said about the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: first a correction, then a challenge, then the opponents' response.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the error being corrected
The author says the opponents erred because they overlooked that self-subsistent things include both composite bodies and non-composite substances. Find the two categories mentioned.
The terms 'jism mu'allaf' (composite body) and 'jawhar' (substance) are key.
3
Understand the body objection
The author asks: why deny that God is a body? The opponents reply that every body they know is disparate, composite, and formed — all signs of temporality (hadath). Since God is eternal, He cannot be a body.
This objection relies on an assumption about the nature of bodies.
4
Reflect on the assumption
Notice that the opponents assume 'body' necessarily implies composition and form. The author will later challenge this assumption. For now, simply identify it.
This assumption is central to the debate on divine embodiment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم إنكم قد أخطأتم لأن منها الفعال الشريف القائم بنفسه الذي هو الجسم المؤلف وليس بشيء واحد ومنها الشريف القائم بنفسه الذي هو الجوهر الذي ليس بمؤلف فما أنكرتم أن يكون الباري سبحانه جسما فإن قالوا لأننا لم نعقل جسما إلا متغايرا مؤتلفا مصورا وهذه الأمور من صفات الحدث والباري سبحانه لا يجوز عليه ذلك فبطل أن يكون جسما
Then it is said to them: You have indeed erred, because among [the things] is the active, noble, self-subsistent which is the composite body (al-jism al-mu'allaf), and it is not one thing; and among them is the noble, self-subsistent which is the substance (al-jawhar) that is not composite. So why do you deny that the Creator, glorified is He, is a body? If they say: Because we have not conceived a body except as disparate, composite, and formed; and these are attributes of temporality; and the Creator, glorified is He, cannot be described with that; so it is invalid that He be a body.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage corrects the earlier division and introduces the body objection. The author points out that the opponents erred because among self-subsistent, noble, active things, there are two types: (1) the composite body (jism mu'allaf), which is not a single thing (i.e., composed of parts), and (2) the substance (jawhar) that is not composite. The author then asks: why do you deny that God is a body? The opponents respond: we do not know a body except as something disparate (mutaghayyir), composite (mu'talif), and formed (musawwar); these are attributes of temporality (hadath). Since God cannot have such attributes, it is invalid that He be a body.

Key Terms
جسم
جوهر
مؤلف
متصور
حدث
متغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God cannot be a body because all bodies are necessarily composite, formed, and temporal.
Correction: The passage presents this as the opponents' view, but the author will later challenge whether 'body' must always entail these temporal attributes, especially when referring to the Creator.

Checkpoint
Why do the opponents deny that God is a body in this passage?
A) Because bodies are temporal
B) Because bodies are active and noble
C) Because bodies are composite and formed
D) Because bodies are not self-subsistent

Key Takeaway
The opponents' denial of divine embodiment is based on an assumed link between body and temporality, but this assumption will be challenged later in the text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you judge God based on your limited experience of created things? This passage shows the opponents denied God's possible nature because they had never seen a body without composition. Is there any attribute or action of God that you reject simply because it doesn't fit your human categories? Write down one such belief you hold and ask yourself: is it based on revelation or on my own limited conception?
?
The opponents assumed that if God were a body, He would be like created bodies. In your heart, do you sometimes imagine God in human terms? This lesson calls you to purify your conception of God. What specific images or assumptions about God do you need to repent from today?
?
The passage ends with the opponents' conclusion that God cannot be a body because of temporality. But the author hints that this assumption will be challenged. How does your own assurance about what God 'cannot' be stem from a lack of trust in His transcendence? Are you willing to let God define Himself, even if it contradicts your mental categories? Commit to one concrete way you will seek to know God as He reveals Himself, not as you assume Him.

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
6 min
Refuting God as a Substance
Using the same logic to show God cannot be a substance, with final rebuttal about noble bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the opponents' own classification of substances and bodies to refute the claim that God is a substance.

Core Idea
The same logic used against divine bodies works against divine substances.

In this passage, Baqillani delivers the final rebuttal to those who assert that God is a substance. He first demonstrates that every known substance is temporal, and when the opponents try to salvage their position by distinguishing noble substances, he turns the tables by applying the same distinction to bodies, forcing a symmetrical contradiction. This lesson walks through each step of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the back-and-forth structure: argument, objection, counter, conclusion.
The argument is concise; note the key terms 'sharif' and 'khasis'.
2
Identify the two parallel claims
Baqillani first argues that substances are temporal, so God cannot be a substance. The opponents then claim substances are of two types: noble and base. Identify these two moves.
3
See how Baqillani mirrors the objection
Baqillani turns the opponents' own distinction against them by dividing bodies similarly into noble and base, concluding that God, being noble, is not a base body—but this empty classification does not solve the problem.
This is the key rhetorical move: the opponents' division is symmetrical and thus fails to protect their claim.
4
Understand the final conclusion
Baqillani concludes that the opponents have no answer. Notice the rhetorical force of 'wa la jawaba lahum 'an shay'in min dhalik' (there is no answer for them concerning any of that).
This seals the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال لهم أيضا فما أنكرتم من استحالة كونه جوهرا لأننا لم نعقل جوهرا إلا شاغلا متحيزا قابلا للحوادث من جنس هذه الجواهر وهذه الأمور دالة على حدث من جازت عليه فلما لم يجز أن يكون القديم سبحانه محدثا لم يجز أن يكون جوهرا فإن قالوا الجواهر ضربان شريف وخسيس فالخسيس هو القابل منها للأعراض الذي يتميز ويشغل المكان والشريف هو ما لا يجوز ذلك عليه منها فوجب أن يكون غير متميز ولا قابل للأعراض قيل لهم ما أنكرتم أيضا أن تكون الأجسام على ضربين جسم خسيس وهو المتميز القابل لصورة والتأليف والحوادث وضرب شريف لا يقبل شيئا من ذلك ولا يجوز عليه والقديم سبحانه شريف فوجب أنه ليس بذي صورة ولا مكان ولا قابلا للأعراض ولا جواب لهم عن شيء من ذلك
It is also said to them: Why do you deny the impossibility of His being a substance? Because we have not conceived a substance except as occupying space (shaghlan), spatially extended (mutahayyizan), receptive of temporal events from the genus of these substances; and these things indicate temporality for the one to whom they apply. So when it is not permissible that the Eternal, glorified is He, be temporal, it is not permissible that He be a substance. If they say: Substances are of two types: noble and base; the base is the one among them receptive of accidents, which is distinguished and occupies space; and the noble is that to which that does not apply, so it must be undifferentiated and not receptive of accidents. It is said to them: Why do you also deny that bodies are of two types: a base body which is the distinguished, receptive of form, composition, and temporal events; and a noble body which does not accept any of that and to which it does not apply; and the Eternal, glorified is He, is noble, so it is necessary that He is not possessed of form, place, or receptive of accidents. And there is no answer for them concerning any of that.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is the final rebuttal. The author turns the opponents' own argument against them. He first states: if they deny God is a body because bodies are composite, they should also deny He is a substance because every known substance occupies space, is spatially extended, and receptive to temporal events—all indicators of temporality. Since God is eternal, He cannot be a substance. The opponents then counter that substances are of two kinds: base (khasis) which is receptive to accidents and occupies space, and noble (sharif) which is not so. Therefore God, being noble, is not receptive to accidents. The author replies by dividing bodies similarly: base bodies are receptive to form, composition, and temporal events, while noble bodies are not. Since God is noble, He is not possessed of form, place, or receptivity to accidents. The author concludes that the opponents have no answer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dividing substances into noble and base allows God to be a substance without temporality.
Correction: The same division applies to bodies, so it does not exclude God from being a body; it only creates a parallel empty category.

Checkpoint
How do the opponents try to avoid the conclusion that God cannot be a substance?
A) By denying that substances exist
B) By dividing substances into noble and base
C) By claiming God is not a body
D) By admitting God is temporal

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the claim that God is a substance by turning the opponents' own distinction between noble and base substances against them, showing that it applies equally to bodies and resolves nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that every concept of 'substance' carries implications of temporality. In what areas of your life do you still try to fit God into human categories, expecting Him to behave like a created thing? How does that affect your worship and trust in His transcendence?
?
Baqillani uses the opponents' own logic against them. Are there beliefs or assumptions you hold about God that, when examined carefully, lead to conclusions you would reject? What would it take to surrender those assumptions to the pure tawhid taught by the Salaf?
?
The opponents' distinction between noble and base substances seemed to offer a way out, but it collapsed under scrutiny. What 'noble' categories do you create in your mind to distance God from imperfection, that actually still limit Him? How can you replace that with affirmation of His absolute incomparability?

Refuting Corporealism Through Mutual Analogy
Baqillani refutes the claim that God is a body or substance through mutual analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani uses mutual analogy (qiyas al-mu'awada) to refute those who claim that Allah is a body or substance, and how he turns their own reasoning against them.

Core Idea
If your reasoning makes God a body, it also makes Him a substance—so why stop there?

This passage is a masterclass in dialectical theology. Imam Baqillani takes the argument of those who claim God is a body and shows that the same logic would force them to also accept that God is a substance—which they deny. He then counters their attempt to distinguish between noble and base substances by applying the same distinction to bodies, leaving them with no reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice how Baqillani uses the opponent's own terms.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent denies God is a body because all bodies they know are composite and contingent. Baqillani then asks: why not deny He is a substance for the same reason?
This is the first move of mutual analogy.
3
Follow the opponent's distinction
The opponent distinguishes noble from base substances. Baqillani applies the same distinction to bodies, showing that a noble body would not have contingent attributes.
The opponent's own distinction backfires.
4
See the conclusion
Baqillani concludes that the opponent has no answer, thereby refuting both claims that God is a body or substance in the sense they mean.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن منها الفعال الشريف القائم بنفسه الذي هو الجسم المؤلف، وليس بشيء واحد ; ومنها الشريف القائم بنفسه الذي هو الجوهر الذي ليس بمؤلف ; فما أنكرتم أن يكون الباري سبحانه جسما؟ فإن قالوا : لأننا لم نعقل جسما إلا متغايرا مؤتلفا مصورا؛ وهذه الأمور من صفات الحدث ; والباري سبحانه لا يجوز عليه ذلك ; فبطل أن يكون جسما، يقال لهم أيضا : فما أنكرتم من استحالة كونه جوهرا؟ لأننا لم نعقل جوهرا إلا شاغلا متحيزا قابلا للحوادث من جنس هذه الجواهر ; وهذه الأمور دالة على حدث من جازت عليه فلما لم يجز أن يكون القديم سبحانه محدثا، لم يجز أن يكون جوهرا.
فإن قالوا الجواهر ضربان شريف وخسيس; فالخسيس هو القابل منها للأعراض الذي يتميز ويشغل المكان ; والشريف هو ما لا يجوز ذلك عليه منها فوجب أن يكون غير متميز ولا قابل للأعراض، قيل لهم ما أنكرتم أيضا أن تكون الأجسام على ضربين: جسم خسيس وهو المتميز القابل للصورة والتأليف والحوادث ; وضرب شريف لا يقبل شيئا من ذلك ولا يجوز عليه ; والقديم سبحانه شريف فوجب أنه ليس بذي صورة ولا مكان ولا قابلا للأعراض ; ولا جواب لهم عن شيء من ذلك .
Because among them is the noble, self-subsistent, effective one which is the composite body, and it is not one thing; and among them is the noble, self-subsistent one which is the substance that is not composite. So why do you deny that the Creator, glorified be He, is a body? If they say: Because we do not conceive a body except as diverse, composite, and formed; and these are attributes of contingency; and the Creator, glorified be He, does not allow that; so it is invalid that He be a body. It is said to them also: So why do you deny the impossibility of His being a substance? Because we do not conceive a substance except as occupying, situated, receptive of accidents of the kind of these substances; and these are indications of contingency for whomever they occur; so when it is not permissible that the Eternal, glorified be He, be contingent, it is not permissible that He be a substance. If they say: Substances are of two types: noble and base. The base is that which receives accidents, is distinguished, and occupies space; and the noble is that which does not allow that, so it must be that it is non-distinguished and not receptive of accidents. It is said to them: So why do you not also allow that bodies are of two types: a base body which is distinguished, receptive of form, composition, and contingencies; and a noble type which does not accept anything of that and it is not permissible for it; and the Eternal, glorified be He, is noble, so it must be that He is not possessed of form, nor place, nor receptive of accidents. And they have no answer to any of that.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument against those who claim God is a body or substance. The author begins by noting that a body is composed of parts, but some bodies are noble and self-subsistent, and some substances are noble and non-composite. Then he asks why the opponent denies that God is a body, given that they accept that God is a substance? The opponent replies that all bodies are composite and subject to change, which are signs of contingency. The author then turns the argument around: the same reasoning applies to substances, since we only know substances that occupy space and receive accidents. The opponent then makes a distinction: substances are of two types: base (which receives accidents) and noble (which does not). The author responds by making a parallel distinction for bodies: there are base bodies (composite) and noble bodies (non-composite). Therefore, God, being noble, is not a base body but a noble one, not subject to change. The opponent has no answer.

شريف

قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this passage denies that God can be described as a body in any sense, including metaphorical.
Correction: The passage refutes the corporealist claim that God is a body like other bodies (composite, contingent), but leaves open the possibility of a 'noble' body not subject to contingency—though the overall aim is to show the inconsistency of the opponent's reasoning, not to assert that God is a body.

Checkpoint

B) Eternal and unchanging
C) Noble and self-subsistent
D) Non-composite and simple

Key Takeaway
Imam Baqillani refutes claims of God being a body or substance by showing that the same logic applies to both, and the opponent's attempts to distinguish noble from base categories fail when consistently applied.

?

The Absurdities of Believing in Divine Indwelling in a Body
Baqillani shows that indwelling leads to spatial attributes for the eternal, an absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a logical reductio to show that the Christian doctrine of indwelling (ḥulūl) implies spatial and physical attributes for the eternal, which is absurd.

Core Idea
If the eternal can indwell a body, it can touch, move, and be spatial—so why not all bodies be eternal?

Imam al-Bāqillānī tackles the Christian claim that the divine Word (al-Kalima) indwells the body of Christ. He does not directly deny the premise; instead, he shows that if you allow indwelling, you must allow a cascade of spatial attributes that contradict divine transcendence. This lesson walks through his devastating reductio.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Christian premise
The passage begins by defining the view: union (ittihad) is indwelling, mixing, and mingling of the Word in a human body.
This is the starting point of the argument.
2
Follow the logical consequences
Baqillani then asks: if the eternal Word can dwell in a body, why can't it touch, be adjacent, move, occupy space? He lists these consequences as absurd.
Each consequence is more problematic than the last.
3
See the final reductio
He concludes that if something with these attributes can be eternal, then any body could be eternal—undermining the Christian claim.
The argument ends with an incomplete question, but the direction is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما من قال إن الاتحاد إنما هو حلول الكلمة في المتحد به واختلاطها به وممازجتها له، فإنه يقال له: إذا جاز على الكلمة الحلول في الجسد المخلوق وممازجتها له واختلاطها به، وهي مع ذلك قديمة، فما أنكرتم من اجتماعها مع الجسد ومماستها له؟ وإذا جاز على القديم سبحانه المماسة والمجاورة والمخالطة للمحدث والممازجة له فلِمَ لا يجوز عليه مقابلة المحدث ومحاذاته؟ ولم لا يجوز عليه الظهور والكمون والحركة والبعد والقرب والشغل، والتفريغ والتصوير والتركيب؟ فإن راموا في ذلك فصلاً لم يجدوه وإن مروا على ذلك قيل لهم: فإذا جاز أن يكون ما هذه صفته قديماً وقد كان في القدم غير مماس ولا ممازج ولا مخالط، فما أنكرتم أن يكون سائر الأجسام المماسة المختلطة المتحركة الساكنة قديمة؟ وما الذي جعل الكلمة التي هذه صفتها بالقدم أولى منها بالحدث؟ وما
As for whoever said that union is only the indwelling of the Word in that with which it is united, and its mixing with it and its mingling with it, it is said to him: If it is permissible for the Word to indwell the created body and to mingle with it and mix with it, while it is eternal, why do you deny its coming together with the body and its touching it? And if it is permissible for the Eternal – exalted be He – touching, adjacency, mixing with the originated, and mingling with him, then why is it not permissible for Him to face the originated and be adjacent to him? And why is it not permissible for Him to appear, to hide, to move, to be distant, to be near, to occupy space, to be emptied, to be shaped, and to be composed? If they seek a distinction in that, they will not find it. And if they pass over that, they are told: If it is permissible for that which has this description to be eternal while it was in pre‑eternity not touching, not mingling, not mixing, then why do you deny that all other bodies that touch, mix, move, and are stationary could be eternal? And what makes the Word which has this description more worthy of pre‑eternity than of origination? And what...
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage refutes the view that the union (ittihad) consists in indwelling (hulul), mixing, and mingling of the Word with the body. Baqillani argues from the Christian premise that the Word is eternal yet can dwell in a created body and mix with it. If so, he asks, why not allow the Word to touch, be adjacent, mix with the body? Then if such spatial and physical attributes are allowed for the Eternal, why not allow appearing, hiding, moving, distance, nearness, occupancy, shaping, and composition? Christians cannot find a distinction to rule these out. The reductio continues: if something with these attributes can be eternal despite previously not having them, why not allow that ordinary bodies with similar contact and motion are also eternal? The final, incomplete sentence asks what makes the Word more deserving of pre‑eternity than origination. The passage follows a logical escalation: from a conceded premise to increasingly absurd consequences, showing that the incarnation claim undermines divine transcendence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Indwelling does not imply spatial or physical attributes for the divine.
Correction: Baqillani demonstrates that if indwelling occurs, then the eternal must be capable of touching, moving, and occupying space, which contradicts divine transcendence.

Checkpoint

B) The Word can touch, move, and occupy space, so all bodies could be eternal.
C) Only the Word is eternal, not other bodies.

Key Takeaway
Allowing divine indwelling in a body logically leads to spatial and physical attributes for the Eternal, an impossibility that refutes the doctrine.

Divine Wisdom in Unequal Creation
Baqillani refutes the objection that unequal creation contradicts justice by citing divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the objectors' own reasoning about divine wisdom to defend the justice of unequal abilities.

Core Idea
Inequality in creation is not injustice; it serves a higher wisdom.

This passage presents a central objection against divine justice: if God is just, why does He create some with knowledge and health, and others without? Baqillani shows that even the objectors admit that such diversity serves a purpose—benefit and a path to great good. By accepting that, they lose their basis to object to other acts of God, like sending prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the list of opposites: knowledge/ignorance, health/affliction.
The objectors claim this is favoritism.
2
Identify the objectors' concession
In the middle of the passage, the objectors admit that the distribution is a benefit and a path to great good. This is key.
By admitting benefit, they open the door to Baqillani's reply.
3
See Baqillani's turn
Baqillani uses their own reason: if this is beneficial, then why can't sending prophets also be beneficial?
The argument is cut off but the logic is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم أيضاً ما أنكرتم من أنه لا يجوز في عدل القديم سبحانه وحكمته على موضوع دليلكم، أن يخلق في بعض عباده الجهل وفي بعضهم العلم وفي بعضهم العمى والبَكَمَ والخرَسَ والزَّمانة وفي بعضهم القوة والتمكين وصحة الآلة وكمال العقل والنحيزة الطبيعية لأن ذلك تفضيل بعض الجنس على بعض . فإن قالوا : عطيّته العلم والحياة وكمال العقل والحواس لبعضهم ومنعه لغيرهم مصلحة للمعطى والممنوع وسبيل لهم إلى نفع عظيم هو سبحانه أعلم به ، قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أن يكون إرساله بعض الخلق
And it is also said to them: 'What prevents you from recognizing that it is not incompatible with the justice of the Eternal, Glorified, and His wisdom, based on your own argument, that He creates ignorance in some of His servants, knowledge in others, blindness, muteness, deafness, and decrepitude in some, and in others strength, ability, sound organs, perfection of intellect, and natural disposition? Because that is a preference of some of the species over others.' If they say: 'His giving knowledge, life, perfection of intellect, and senses to some and withholding them from others is a benefit for the giver and the withheld, and a path for them to a great good which He, Glorified, knows best.' It is said to them: 'What prevents you from recognizing that His sending some of His creatures...'
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection and the beginning of its answer. The objectors claim that if God creates unequal abilities (knowledge, health, etc.), that would be unjust favoritism. Baqillani turns their own reasoning against them: if they argue that such diversity serves a purpose (benefit and a path to great good), then why cannot the sending of prophets also serve a similar purpose? The text cuts off but the argument is that the same logic they use to justify unequal abilities can be used to justify the sending of prophets. Thus the objection fails on its own grounds.

The Dilemma of Analogical Reasoning: Refuting Anthropomorphism
Baqillani refutes the claim that sending messengers is invalid based on analogy from the witnessed world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani refutes the objection against prophethood by exposing the self-defeating nature of analogical reasoning from the witnessed world.

Core Idea
Analogy from creation to Creator leads to absurdity, not to denial of prophethood.

This lesson presents a powerful refutation by Imam al-Baqillani against those who argue that sending messengers is impossible because in our experience, agents only act for benefit or to repel harm. Baqillani shows that if you push this analogy to its logical conclusion, you end up attributing to God the limitations of created beings—which is absurd. Moreover, you would undermine your own proof for the world's origination. This dilemma demolishes the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: it begins with a conditional statement and then presents two possible outcomes.
The passage is a single argument with a dilemma structure.
2
Understand the opponent's claim
Identify what the opponents are arguing: that sending a messenger is impermissible because in the witnessed world, agents only act for benefit or to repel harm. They analogize from created agents to God.
This is an application of 'qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid' (inference from the unseen to the witnessed).
3
Analyze the two horns of the dilemma
Baqillani presents a dilemma: if the opponents accept the consequences of their analogy (attribute compositeness, location, etc. to God), they invalidate the proof of origination (huduth). If they reject those consequences, they abandon their own analogical reasoning. Trace how each horn leads to a contradiction.
The dilemma shows that the opponents' argument is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم : إن بنيتم الأمر على قبح ذلك في الشاهد بزعمكم، فيجب أن تقضوا على أن الفاعل للعالم لا يفعله إلا لاجتلاب منفعة أو دفع مضرة وداع دعاه إلى الفعل وبعثه عليه؛ وأنه تعالى مؤلّفٌ ذو حَيِّز وقبول للأعراض وفي مكان دون مكان لأنكم لم تعقلوا فاعلا في الشاهد إلا كذلك فإن مروا على هذا أبطلوا الحدوث والمحدث وسيقت عليهم مطالبات الدهرية وإن أبوه، نقضوا استدلالهم بمجرد الشاهد والوجود.
And it is said to them: If you base the matter on the ugliness of that in the witnessed [world] according to your claim, then you must judge that the agent of the world does not do it except to attract a benefit or repel a harm and a motive that calls him to the act and impels him to it; and that He, exalted, is composite, possessing location, subject to accidents, and in a place not another place, because you have not known an agent in the witnessed [world] except like that. If they proceed upon this, they invalidate [the argument for] origination and the originated, and the arguments of the materialists would be brought against them; and if they reject it, they contradict their inference based solely on analogy from the witnessed [world] and existence.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against sending prophets. The objectors argue that sending a messenger is impossible because in the witnessed world, such an act is only done for benefit or to repel harm, implying imperfection in God. Imam al-Baqillani responds by posing a dilemma: if they insist on this analogy, they must also attribute to God the characteristics of created agents—being composite, occupying space, subject to accidents—which is absurd. If they accept these consequences, they undermine their own proof for the world's origination (huduth) and fall into the position of the materialists (dahriyya). If they reject the consequences, they abandon their reliance on analogy from the witnessed world. Thus, their objection is self-defeating.

Key Terms
الشاهد
الفاعل
الحدوث
الدهرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Analogical reasoning from the witnessed world to God is always valid and can be used to deny prophethood.
Correction: Baqillani shows that such reasoning leads to absurd anthropomorphic conclusions or self-contradiction, thus is invalid.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, if the opponents accept the consequences of their analogy from the witnessed world, what do they invalidate?
A) The existence of the Creator
B) The argument for origination (al-huduth)
C) The possibility of prophethood
D) The attributes of God

Key Takeaway
Analogical reasoning from the witnessed world to God is invalid because it leads to self-contradiction and undermines theological proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Are there areas in your life where you assume God operates like a human agent—needing a reason, benefit, or motive? How does Baqillani's dilemma force you to reconsider that assumption, and what will you do to submit to a God whose ways are beyond your analogies?
?
What would it cost you to surrender your need to make God's actions 'make sense' according to human logic? Can you commit to trusting and obeying a God whose wisdom is infinitely higher than your own, even when His commands seem to contradict your reasoning?

Refuting the Genus Objection: The First Proof
Baqillani presents the objection and the first part of his refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Baqillani refutes the objection that God must be of the same genus as creation in order to send a messenger, by showing that the same logic would prevent God from guiding through intellect and command.

Core Idea
If the messenger must share genus, then God cannot even argue through reason.

Imam al-Baqillani tackles a key objection against prophethood: that God cannot send a messenger because a messenger must be of the same genus as the recipient. In this passage, he shows that this objection backfires—it would also mean God cannot communicate through the intellect or issue commands, which would lead to abandoning tawhid. Let's see how he argues this.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection from the objectors: they claim that in the witnessed world, a messenger is always of the same genus as the one sent to, so God cannot send a messenger since He is not of creation's genus.
2
Understand the underlying principle
Identify the principle: the messenger must be of the same genus as the recipient. This is what Baqillani will challenge.
3
Follow Baqillani's first reductio
See how Baqillani applies the same principle to divine guidance through intellect and command. If the principle holds, then God cannot be an arguer or commander, since in the witnessed world the arguer/commander is of the same genus as the one argued/commanded.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Recognize the dilemma
Notice the two options: if they accept the consequence, they abandon tawhid; if they reject it, they contradict their original inference. Either way, the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : الدليل على أنه لا يجوز أن يُرْسِلُ الله تعالى رسولاً إلى خَلقه أنا وجدنا الرسول في الشاهد والمعقول من جنس المرسل فلما لم يَجُزْ أن يكون القديم من جنس المخلوقات بذاته ثبت أنه لا يجوز أن يرسل رسولاً إلى خلقه فيقال لهم : فيجب على اعتلالكم هذا ألا يكون الله سبحانه مُحْتَجّاً على الخلق بعقولهم ولا أمراً لهم بما وضعه فيها عندكم من وجوب فعل الْحَسَنِ وترك القبيح واستعمال النظر وفعــل التوحيد الله والمعرفة به والشكر لنعمه لأن المُحْتَج الأمر في الشاهد المأمور المُحْتَجٌ عليه فإن مرُّوا على ذلك تركوا التوحيد ولحقوا بأهل التعطيل، وإن أبوه وراموا فصلاً نقضوا استدلالهم . ويقال لهم : فيجب على موضوعكم ألا يكون القديم سبحانه شيئاً ولا فاعلا ولا عالماً حياً قادراً لأن ذلك يوجب أن يكون من جنس الأشياء المعقولة لأن الشيء في الشاهد والوجود لا ينفك من أن يكون جسماً أو جوهراً أو عرضاً والحي العالم القادر لا يكون إلا جسماً وجواهر مجتمعة والفاعل منا لا يفعل إلا في نفسه أو في غيره بسبب يُحْدِثُهُ في نفسه فإن لم يجب هذا أجمع، سقط ما تعلقتم به.
For they say: The evidence that it is not permissible for God to send a messenger to His creation is that we find the messenger in the witnessed [world] and in reason to be of the same genus as the one sent to. So since it is not permissible for the Eternal to be of the same genus as created things in His essence, it is established that it is not permissible for Him to send a messenger to His creation. So it is said to them: According to this reasoning of yours, it would be necessary that God not be an argument against creation through their intellects, nor command them with what He has placed in them according to you of the obligation to do good, avoid evil, employ reflection, perform tawhid for God, know Him, and thank Him for His blessings, because the arguer and commander in the witnessed [world] is the one against whom the argument is brought. If they go along with that, they abandon tawhid and join the people of negation; and if they reject it and seek a distinction, they contradict their inference. And it is said to them: According to your principle, it would be necessary that the Eternal not be a thing, nor an agent, nor knowing, living, powerful, because that would require that He be of the same genus as intelligible things, since a thing in the witnessed [world] and existence is not free from being a body, substance, or accident, and the living, knowing, powerful being is only a body and assembled substances, and the agent among us does not act except in himself or in another due to an event he produces in himself. If all this is not necessary, then your attachment falls.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific objection against prophethood: that a messenger must be of the same genus (jins) as the one to whom he is sent, so since God is not of the same genus as creation, He cannot send a messenger. Imam al-Baqillani offers a two-part refutation. First, if this principle were sound, it would also mean that God cannot be an 'argument' (muḥtajj) against creation through their intellects or command them through reason, because the one who argues or commands in the witnessed world is of the same genus as the recipient. This would lead to abandoning tawhid and joining the people of negation (ahl al-ta‘ṭīl). Second, the principle would force the absurd conclusion that God cannot be described as a 'thing', agent, knowing, living, or powerful, since in the witnessed world all such descriptions apply only to bodies or composites. Since these conclusions are false, the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
الرسول
المرسل
المحتج
التوحيد
التعطيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must be of the same genus as humans to send a messenger
Correction: Baqillani shows that this principle would also negate God's guidance through intellect and command, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, if the messenger must be of the same genus as the one sent to, then what would also be impossible for God?
A) Sending messengers
B) Arguing or commanding through intellect
C) Being eternal
D) Creating the world

Key Takeaway
The objection that God must share genus to send a messenger is self-refuting because it would also prevent God from guiding through reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth from God because it didn't fit your limited human reasoning? How does Baqillani's argument reveal the self-defeating nature of such objections?
?
If you truly believe God can guide through your intellect, then why do you sometimes ignore the clear obligations He has placed in your mind—like seeking truth and avoiding falsehood? What would it cost you to follow that guidance fully?
?
This lesson shows that rejecting a divine command on the basis of a flawed analogy leads to abandoning tawhid. Is there an area of your life where you are using a similar excuse to avoid submitting to God's commands? Will you let that excuse stand, or will you break it today?

Refuting the Genus Objection: The Second Proof – Divine Attributes
Baqillani presents the second refutation focusing on divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Baqillani further refutes the genus objection by showing that it would strip God of essential attributes like being a thing, knowing, living, and powerful.

Core Idea
If genus is required, God cannot even be described as knowing or living.

In this second part of his refutation, Imam al-Baqillani drives the point home: if the objectors' principle were applied consistently, God could not be called a thing, agent, knowing, living, or powerful, since in the witnessed world these descriptions only apply to bodies or composites. Since Muslims affirm these attributes, the objection collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the second refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. This is a direct continuation of the previous argument.
2
Notice the list of attributes negated
Identify that Baqillani says if the principle holds, God would not be a thing, agent, knowing, living, or powerful.
These are all attributes Muslims affirm for God.
3
Understand the reasoning
In the witnessed world, 'thing' always refers to a body, substance, or accident. Living/knowing/powerful beings are always bodies. Agents only act in themselves or by causing events in themselves. So if God were described with these terms, He would be considered of the same genus as creation—an unacceptable consequence.
4
Conclude the refutation
Since Muslims affirm these attributes for God, the objectors' principle cannot be valid. The objection collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Creator's Absolute Sovereignty
Baqillani presents a rational argument for God's absolute sovereignty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's rational argument that the Creator's absolute ownership entails unrestricted authority over all creation.

Core Idea
The Creator's absolute ownership entails absolute authority over His creation.

Baqillani now presents a third argument, one grounded in reason itself. He challenges the objectors to accept the logical implications of God's unique status as the sole Owner of all existence. This passage distills the core of divine sovereignty: because there is no higher authority, God may do as He wills with His creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the confident closing statement: 'they will find no way to refute that.'
2
Identify key terms
Look for the terms 'خالق الأعيان' (Creator of entities), 'مالك الذوات' (Owner of essences), and 'زاجر' (restrainer). These are central to the argument.
3
Understand the logical structure
The argument is: because God is the sole Owner and Creator, there is no superior to forbid Him. Therefore, He can do as He pleases with His creation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does Baqillani claim the opponents 'will find no way to refute' this? Consider the philosophical implications of absolute ownership.
5
Address a common misunderstanding
Some might think God is bound by human notions of justice. Baqillani asserts that God's sovereignty transcends such limitations.
This argument is foundational for divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: ما أنكرتم أن يكون العقل قاضياً على أن لخالق الأعيان ومالك الذوات أن يتلفها ويؤلمها وأن يبيح ذلك فيها وأن يبتدئها باللذات بدلاً من الآلام وبالآلام بدلاً من اللذات لأنه لا مالك فوقه ولا زاجر يجدد له؟ فلا يجدون إلى دفع ذلك سبيلا
Then it is said to them: What prevents you from [accepting] that reason rules that the Creator of entities and the Owner of essences has the right to destroy them and cause them pain, and to permit that in them, and to begin them with pleasures instead of pains and with pains instead of pleasures, because there is no owner above Him and no restrainer that could forbid Him? So they will find no way to refute that.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This is a third argument, appealing to reason itself. Baqillani argues that reason itself judges that the Creator—who is the sole Owner of all beings—has absolute authority to inflict destruction and pain, and to interchange pleasures and pains as He pleases. The justification is that there is no superior authority over Him and no restraining force to prohibit Him. The conclusion is that the opponents cannot logically deny this sovereignty. The passage ends with a confident assertion: 'they will find no way to refute that.'

Key Terms
خالق الأعيان
مالك الذوات
يتلف
يؤلم
زاجر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God is constrained by human notions of justice or fairness, so He cannot arbitrarily cause pain.
Correction: Baqillani asserts that because there is no higher authority over God, His sovereignty is absolute and not subject to external moral constraints.

Checkpoint
Why, according to Baqillani, can the Creator inflict pain or pleasure on animals as He wishes?
A) Because animals have no rights.
B) Because there is no owner above Him and no restrainer to forbid Him.
C) Because reason always permits causing pain.
D) Because the prophets have granted Him permission.

Key Takeaway
The Creator's absolute ownership over all beings implies He has unrestricted authority to cause pain, pleasure, life, and death as He pleases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you suffer a loss or pain, do you truly submit to the fact that the Owner of your soul has the right to cause that pain, or do you find yourself feeling entitled to an explanation from Him?
?
If there is no restrainer above God, what is stopping you from surrendering every aspect of your life to His will? Is it fear of people, attachment to comfort, or a hidden belief that you have rights against Him?
?
What would change in your daily life if you fully internalized that God's absolute ownership means He can begin you with pleasures instead of pains and pains instead of pleasures—and that this is perfectly just because He is the Owner?

Refuting the Claim of Temporary Incapacitation
Refutes the claim that the Quran's inimitability was only temporary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the permanent inability of the Arabs to produce a text like the Quran is the foundation for its miraculous nature, and how Imam al-Baqillani refutes the claim that it was only a temporary divine intervention.

Core Idea
The Arabs' permanent inability proves the Quran's miracle, not just a temporary divine act.

Imam al-Baqillani addresses a key objection: were the Arabs only prevented from matching the Quran during the Prophet's lifetime? He examines two alternative views and demonstrates that even if the prevention were temporary, it would still constitute a miracle. Ultimately, he argues that the permanent nature of the inability is the true sign of the Quran's uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the two alternative views presented.
2
Identify the two alternative views
The passage mentions two claims: one says Allah only prevented the Arabs during the challenge, another says they were able before but prevented at the time. Note how each is phrased.
These are the views the author refutes.
3
Analyze the analogy
The author gives an analogy of a prophet challenging his people to move their hands. Understand how this illustrates that even temporary prevention is a miracle.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the objection falls away because the permanent inability is established, and even if temporary, it would still be a miracle.
This solidifies the Quran's permanent inimitability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعَرُوضُه؟ قيل له: لعلمنا بأنه ليس بمثل له ولا من جنس نظمه؛ فإن قدر على ذلك قادر فليأت به لنريه أنه خلاف له ونعلم ذلك بعجز العرب عن معارضة القرآن مع العلم بأنهم أفصح الفصحاء وأبلغ البلغاء وأشعرُ وأخطبُ من على وجه الأرض ممن تكلم بلسانهم بعدهم. فتعلم بذلك تعذر معارضته على من بعدهم. وعلى أن من الناس من يزعم أن الله سبحانه إنما أعجز العرب أيضاً عن معارضته وقت التحدي بالإتيان بمثله لكي يخرق بذلك العادة لصاحبه ويدل على صدقه، وقد يجوز أن يُقدِرَهم بعد موت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم على مثله. ومنهم أيضاً من يقول: قد كانت العرب قادرة قبل التحدي على الإتيان بمثله وإنما أعجزهم الله سبحانه عن ذلك وقت تحدي الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ونقض عادتهم ليدل على صدقه. ولعمري إن ذلك لو كان كذلك لكان آية عظيمة وخرقاً للعادة كما أن نبياً لو تحدى قومه بتحريك أيديهم والخروج عن أماكنهم إلى أقرب المواضع إليها فمنعوا القدرة على ذلك وقد اعتادوا الاقتدار عليه، ثم أقدروا عليه ثانية بعد تقضي تحديه لكان خرق العادة بإيجاد القدرة على ذلك وإعدامها على خلاف المتعالم المألوف آية عظيمة وحجة بينة. فإذا كان ذلك كذلك سقط ما سألوا عنه.
And its objection? It was said to him: Because we know that it is not similar to it nor of the same kind as its composition; for if someone is capable of that, let him produce it so that we may show him that it is contrary to it, and we know that by the inability of the Arabs to oppose the Quran, while knowing that they are the most eloquent of the eloquent and the most fluent of the fluent, and the most poetic and oratorical of those on the face of the earth who spoke with their language after them. So you know by that the impossibility of opposing it for those after them. And among people are those who claim that Allah the Exalted only incapacitated the Arabs also from opposing it at the time of the challenge to produce something like it, in order to break the custom for its owner and to indicate his truthfulness, and it might be possible that He empowers them after the death of the Prophet (peace be upon him) to produce something like it. And among them also are those who say: The Arabs were able before the challenge to produce something like it, but Allah the Exalted only incapacitated them from that at the time of the challenge of the Messenger (peace be upon him) and broke their custom to indicate his truthfulness. By my life, if that were the case, it would be a great sign and a breach of custom, just as if a prophet challenged his people to move their hands and leave their places to the nearest locations, and they were prevented from the ability to do that while they were accustomed to having the ability, then they were given ability again after the conclusion of his challenge, that would be a breach of custom by creating ability for that and removing it contrary to what is customary and familiar, a great sign and a clear proof. So if that is the case, what they asked about falls away.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument defending the permanent inimitability of the Quran. It begins by anticipating an objection: how do we know the Quran is unlike anything else? The answer is that we know because the Arabs, despite their eloquence, could not produce anything similar. The author then refutes two contrary views. First, some claim that Allah only prevented the Arabs during the Prophet's lifetime as a temporary miracle. Second, others claim that the Arabs were able before the challenge but were prevented only at the time of the challenge. The author argues that even if it were temporary, it would still be a breach of custom and a miracle. He gives an analogy: if a prophet challenged his people to move their hands and then prevented them, and later empowered them again, that would still be a miracle. Therefore, the objection that the inimitability was only temporary fails. The permanent inability of the Arabs proves the Quran's miraculous nature.

Key Terms
إعجاز
تحدي
خرق العادة
معارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Arabs' inability to produce a text like the Quran was only a temporary divine intervention during the Prophet's lifetime, and that they could have done so after his death.
Correction: The author argues that even if it were temporary, it would still be a miracle, but the permanent inability of the Arabs—both during and after the Prophet's time—is the actual proof of the Quran's unique nature.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the main proof that the Quran is inimitable?
A) The Arabs were only prevented during the Prophet's time.
B) The Arabs, despite their eloquence, have been unable to produce anything like it at any time.
C) Allah broke their custom only at the challenge.
D) The Quran contains prophecies.

Key Takeaway
The permanent inability of the Arabs to produce a text like the Quran is the foundation of its miraculous nature, and even if temporary, it would still be a miracle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that even a temporary miracle is still a clear proof. Think of a time you doubted Allah's signs because they seemed 'temporary' or 'small'. How does this lesson expose the weakness in your reasoning? What will you do today to train your heart to accept any sign from Allah as fully convincing?
?
The permanent inability of the Arabs to match the Quran is a standing miracle. Do you truly live with the conviction that the Quran is beyond human capacity, or do you sometimes feel it could be imitated? If doubt lingers, what concrete step will you take this week to strengthen your certainty in the Quran's divine origin?

Counter-Argument: God's Actions and Badā'
Baqillani presents a reductio ad absurdum against the badā' objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a reductio ad absurdum to defend against the badā' objection to naskh.

Core Idea
If all divine changes imply badā', then the objection proves too much.

Imam Baqillani now turns the tables on the objectors who claim that abrogation (naskh) implies badā' (change of mind) in God. He presents a powerful counter-argument: if a change in ruling implies badā', then every instance of God changing a creature's state—from life to death, health to sickness—would also imply badā'. Since the objectors do not affirm badā' in those cases, they must also reject it in abrogation. This forces logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice the rhetorical question format.
The passage is a single sentence with several examples.
2
Identify the logical structure
Recognize that Baqillani is using a reductio ad absurdum: he takes the objectors' premise and applies it to other divine actions to show an absurd conclusion.
The key is the phrase 'ما أنكرتم أن يكون... فهذا بدا له' — 'Do you deny that... then this would be badā'?'
3
Analyze the examples
List the examples: causing death after life, sickness after health, pain after pleasure, changing from a state. See how each involves a change from one state to another.
4
Understand the force of the argument
Ask: If the objectors accept that these changes are not badā', then why would they claim naskh is badā'? Consistency demands they either accept badā' in all changes or reject it in naskh.
This is the heart of the counter-argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم في اعتلالهم في البداء ما أنكرتم أن يكون الله سبحانه إذا أمات الجسم بعد حياته وأسقمه بعد صحته وآلمه بعد إلذاذه وغيره عن حالته فهذا بدا له وعلم أن ما كان فعله مفسدة ليس بمصلحة
And it is said to them regarding their argument about badā': Do you deny that if God (سبحانه) causes the body to die after its life, and makes it sick after its health, and causes it pain after its pleasure, and changes it from its state, then this would be a badā' for Him, and He would know that what He did was a harm, not a benefit?
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a rhetorical counter-argument. Baqillani turns the objection against the objectors: if they claim that naskh implies badā', then by the same logic, every instance of God changing a state—such as causing death after life, sickness after health, pain after pleasure—would also imply badā'. Since they do not accept that God has badā' in these changes, they must also reject that naskh implies badā'. The argument forces consistency.

Key Terms
البداء (badā')
الإماتة (imātah)
الإسقام (isqām)
الإيلام (īlām)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objectors believe that naskh implies badā' (a change of mind due to ignorance) in God.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if that were true, then all divine changes (like death after life) would also imply badā', which the objectors do not accept. Therefore, their premise is inconsistent.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of Baqillani's counter-argument using God's acts of causing death and sickness?
A) To prove that God changes His mind.
B) To show that the objectors' own logic would force them to affirm badā' in God's daily acts.
C) To deny that God causes death and sickness.
D) To argue that naskh is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's counter-argument shows that the badā' objection is self-defeating because it would apply to all divine changes, which the objectors do not accept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a sudden loss—health, comfort, or a loved one—does your heart truly believe that God's knowledge is perfect and that He knew this from eternity, or do you find yourself accusing Him of ignorance? Will you repent of that accusation today?
?
This passage shows that God's changes are never based on new information. Are you living as if God already knows every detail of your future, or do you act as if He doesn't? What decision are you avoiding because you fear change?
?
If you were to apply the badā' logic to your own circumstances, you would despair at every loss. Can you instead accept that every change from God is based on eternal wisdom, and leave your complaints behind? What complaint will you silence today?

Refuting Badā' from Changes in Creation
Baqillani refutes the objection that changes in creation imply badā'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that changes in creation do not imply badā' (a change in God's knowledge), and that changes in divine actions are based on shifting wisdom and benefit.

Core Idea
Changes in creation reflect changing wisdom, not changing knowledge in God.

In this passage, Baqillani takes on a common objection: that when God causes death after life, sickness after health, or pain after pleasure, this seems to indicate badā'—a change in God's knowledge. He shows that such changes can be explained by the differing benefits of pleasure and punishment, and that the same logic applies to divine commands and prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the full argument. Note the flow: objection, response, counter, and parallel.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the objectors' claim
Find where Baqillani states the objectors' reasoning: when God changes a state, they claim this is badā'. Also note how they might try to justify it as recompense.
The objectors' justification is highlighted in the translation.
3
Trace Baqillani's counter-argument
Baqillani asks whether the initial act of bestowing pleasure also implies badā' if later replaced by pain. He then presents a dilemma: if they say yes, they contradict their religion; if no, then the change is based on benefit.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Understand the parallel with commands
Baqillani extends the argument to commands and prohibitions: just as actions can change based on benefit, so can commands. This shows that change does not entail ignorance.
This strengthens the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم في اعتلالهم في البداء ما أنكرتم أن يكون الله سبحانه إذا أمات الجسم بعد حياته وأسْقَمَه بعد صحته وآلَمَه بعد إلذاذه وغيره عن حالته فهذا بدا له وعلم أن ما كان فعله مفسدة ليس بمصلحة فإن قالوا : الآلام والعقاب إنما توقع بعد التفضل باللذات على سبيل الجزاء والانتقام وكان ذلك هو الصلاح لهم، لأنه أزجر لهم عن المعصية وأدعى إلى الطاعة قيل لهم : اعملوا على أن ذلك كما ادعيتم أليس قد كان الله تعالى ابتدأهم بالتفضل باللذة فلما عصوه أبدلهم بها ألماً وسقماً على سبيل النقمة فهل بدا له من فعل اللذة ومثلها؟ فإن قالوا أجل تركوا دينهم وإن قالوا لا ولكن التفضل في وقت باللذات أصلح لهم والانتقام بعد ذلك بالآلام على الإجرام أصلح لهم من اللذات قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم من مثل ذلك في الأمر لهم بالشيء في وقت والنهي عن مثله، فيكون كفعل الشيء في وقت وترك مثله بعده؟
And it is said to them regarding their reasoning about badā': What prevents you from [saying] that God, may He be exalted, when He causes the body to die after its life, and makes it sick after its health, and pains it after pleasing it, and changes it from its state, then this is badā' for Him, and He knows that what He did was corruption not benefit? Then if they say: The pains and punishment are only inflicted after the bestowal of pleasures by way of recompense and vengeance, and that was beneficial for them, because it is more deterrent from disobedience and more conducive to obedience, it is said to them: Consider that as you claim, did not God Almighty at the beginning bestow pleasure upon them, and when they disobeyed Him, He replaced it with pain and sickness by way of revenge? So did badā' occur to Him from the act of pleasure and the like? If they say: Yes, they have abandoned their religion; and if they say: No, but the bestowal of pleasure at one time is more beneficial for them, and the revenge after that with pains for offenses is more beneficial for them than pleasures, it is said to them: So what prevents you from the like of that regarding the command for them of something at one time and the prohibition of its like, so that it is like doing a thing at one time and leaving its like afterwards?
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the accusation that changes in creation imply badā' for God. The author presents the objectors' claim: when God causes death after life, sickness after health, pain after pleasure, and changes a state, the objectors say this indicates badā'—a change in God's knowledge. The author then anticipates a response: the objectors might say that pains and punishments are only inflicted after bestowing pleasures as recompense and vengeance, and that this is beneficial because it deters from sin and encourages obedience. The author counters by asking whether God's initial bestowal of pleasure and subsequent replacement with pain and sickness upon disobedience also implies badā'. If they say yes, they have abandoned their religion (i.e., contradicted their own beliefs). If they say no, and claim that the bestowal of pleasure at one time is more beneficial and revenge with pain later is also beneficial, then the author applies the same logic to commands: why can't God command something at one time and prohibit its like later, without implying badā'? The point is that changes in both actions and commands can be based on shifting wisdom and benefit, not on new knowledge.

Key Terms
بداء (badā')
إماتة (imāta)
إسقام (isqām)
إيلام (īlām)
تفضل (tafaḍḍul)
جزاء (jazā')
انتقام (intiqām)
صلاح (ṣalāḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any change in divine action implies a change in God's knowledge (badā').
Correction: Changes in actions are based on shifting wisdom and benefit, not on new knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the objectors' initial explanation for pain and punishment after pleasure?
A) It is arbitrary and without reason.
B) It is on account of recompense and vengeance for disobedience.
C) It is a test of faith.
D) It is a sign of badā'.

Key Takeaway
Changes in divine actions are based on wisdom and benefit, not on new knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a painful reversal after a period of ease, do you ever question whether God's plan is inconsistent? How does this passage challenge you to trust that His wisdom is perfect even when His actions change?
?

?

Refuting Badā' from the Pain of Innocents and Changes in Objects
Baqillani refutes badā' from pain of children/animals and changes in objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the examples of suffering children, animals, and changing inanimate objects to refute badā' without attributing vengeance or change in God.

Core Idea
Pain of innocents and changes in objects do not imply badā'—they reflect wise administration.

After establishing that variation in commands does not indicate badā', Baqillani turns to another potential objection: the apparent suffering of innocent beings and the alteration of inanimate objects. Critics might claim these changes suggest a change in God's attitude. In this passage, Baqillani systematically refutes this, showing that any explanation—vengeance or benefit—either leads to heretical doctrines like reincarnation or falls under the same logic of changing circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument flow before you interpret.
Notice the rhetorical questions: 'What do you say about...'
2
Identify the two options for pain of innocents
The passage presents two possible explanations for the suffering of children and animals: vengeance or benefit. See what each leads to.
Vengeance leads to reincarnation (tanāsukh), which is heretical.
3
Extend the argument to inanimate objects
Baqillani then lists many changes in inanimate objects (composition, shape, moisture, etc.) and implies the same logic applies: change does not mean badā'.
This reinforces that all change is for benefit, not due to new knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : ما تقولون أيضاً في إيلام الأطفال والبهائم والمجانين بعد الذاذهم ونقض بنيتهم بعد صحتها وتقطع جوارحهم بعد سلامتهم والذهاب بأسماعهم وأبصارهم؟ أتقولون إن ذلك على سبيل الانتقام منهم؟ فإن قالوا : أجل تركوا قولهم ولحقوا بأهل التناسخ الذين يقولون إن هذه الأرواح المحبوسة في البهائم والأطفال قد غضب الله عليهم ونقلهم في الأكوان والأدوار ؛ وذلك ترك قولهم ؛ وإن قالوا: ليس تغير حالات الأطفال في هذه الأمور انتقاماً ولكن ذلك على سبيل المصالح فقط قيل لهم مثله في الأمر بالشيء في وقت والنهي عن مثله بعده . وكذلك يُسألون عن نقض الجماد بعد تأليفه وتربيعه بعد تسديسه وترطيبه بعد تيبيسه وتحريكه بعد تسكينه وتسويده بعد تبييضه وغير ذلك من تغير الحالات
Then it is said to them: What do you also say about inflicting pain on children, animals, and the insane after their pleasures, and destroying their structure after their health, and cutting their limbs after their safety, and taking away their hearing and sight? Do you say that is by way of vengeance upon them? If they say: Yes, they have abandoned their statement and joined the people of reincarnation who say that these souls imprisoned in animals and children, God has become angry with them and transferred them in the spheres and cycles; and that is abandoning their statement; and if they say: The changing of the states of children in these matters is not vengeance, but that is only by way of benefits, it is said to them: The like of that in the command of something at one time and the prohibition of its like afterwards. And likewise they are asked about the ruining of inanimate objects after their composition, and their squaring after their hexagon, and their moistening after their drying, and their moving after their stillness, and their blackening after their whitening, and other changes of states.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of badā' by pressing the objectors on cases where change seems punitive but cannot be explained by vengeance against creatures who are not morally responsible—infants, animals, and the insane. The author asks: if these beings suffer pain after pleasure, destruction after health, loss of limbs and senses, do you claim it is vengeance? If they say yes, they would be forced into the doctrine of reincarnation (tanāsukh), which is unacceptable. If they say no, and that these changes are purely for benefit (masāliḥ), then the same logic applies to commands: God can command something at one time and prohibit its like later for benefit, without implying badā'. The author further extends the argument to inanimate objects: they too undergo changes (composition to ruin, square from hexagon, moist from dry, etc.), which are not attributed to badā'. The overall point is that change in states or commands does not indicate a change in divine knowledge; rather, it reflects wise administration based on shifting benefits.

Key Terms
إيلام (īlām)
أطفال (aṭfāl)
بهائم (bahā'im)
مجانين (majānīn)
تناسخ (tanāsukh)
جماد (jamād)
تأليف (ta'līf)
تربيع (tarbī')
تسديس (tasdīs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The pain of innocent children and animals must be either punishment or evidence of badā'.
Correction: Baqillani shows that such pain is for benefit, like changes in inanimate objects, and does not imply any change in God's knowledge or will.

Checkpoint
What do the objectors have to accept if they claim that the pain of children and animals is vengeance?
A) That God is unjust.
B) The doctrine of reincarnation (tanāsukh).
C) That there is no afterlife.
D) That God changes His mind.

Key Takeaway
Changes in states of created beings—whether suffering infants or dissolving objects—do not indicate badā' in God, but rather reflect His wise administration of benefits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

When your spiritual condition shifts, do you attribute that to God's wise guidance or to chance? Will you now commit to seeing every shift in your life as from His wise hand?
