Refuting Bada' from God's Changing Pleasure to Punishment
Baqillani refutes the claim that God's changing pleasure to punishment implies bada'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the claim that God's changing pleasure to punishment implies a change in His knowledge (bada').

Core Idea
God's actions vary for benefit without implying change in His eternal will.

This passage presents a powerful refutation. The opponent argues that God's inflicting pain after pleasure as retribution suggests a change in divine plan. Baqillani turns the argument back, showing that if they deny bada', they must accept that divine actions can vary for beneficial reasons without affecting God's eternal knowledge. He then extends this to commands and prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the interrogative style Baqillani uses.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find the claim that pains are inflicted after pleasures as retribution. Understand their reasoning that this is beneficial.
The opponent uses this to argue against divine consistency.
3
Analyze the two possible responses
Baqillani asks directly: does this imply bada'? If yes, they abandon religion; if no, they must accept variation for benefit. Trace both branches.
This is a crucial logical fork.
4
Understand the analogy to commands
Baqillani extends the argument: commands and prohibitions can also vary without implying bada'. This refutes the opponent's objection against abrogation.
5
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider how this argument preserves God's eternal knowledge and will while allowing for variable actions in time.
This connects to broader Aqeedah principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بهم بمصلحة فإن قالوا : الآلام والعقاب إنما توقع بعد التفضل باللذات على سبيل الجزاء والانتقام وكان ذلك هو الصلاح لهم، لأنه أزجر لهم عن المعصية وأدعى إلى الطاعة قيل لهم : اعملوا على أن ذلك كما ادعيتم أليس قد كان الله تعالى ابتدأهم بالتفضل باللذة فلما عَصَوْهُ أبدلهم بها ألماً وسقماً على سبيل النقمة فهل بدا له من فعل اللذة ومثلها؟ فإن قالوا أجل تركوا دينهم وإن قالوا لا ولكن التفضل في وقت باللذات أصلح لهم والانتقام بعد ذلك بالآلام على الإجرام أصلح لهم من اللذات قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم من مثل ذلك في الأمر لهم بالشيء في وقت والنهي عن مثله، فيكون كفعل الشيء في وقت وترك مثله بعده؟
By them with benefit. If they say: Pains and punishment are only inflicted after bestowal of pleasures by way of recompense and retribution, and that was the benefit for them because it is more deterring for them from disobedience and more conducive to obedience. It is said to them: Act upon that. As you claimed, has not God, exalted, begun them with the bestowal of pleasure, and when they disobeyed Him, He replaced it with pain and sickness by way of vengeance? So did bada' (change of mind) occur for Him from the act of pleasure and its like? If they say 'yes', they abandon their religion. If they say 'no, but the bestowal of pleasures at a time is beneficial for them, and the retribution after that with pains for crimes is more beneficial for them than pleasures.' It is said to them: So what do you deny of the like of that in commanding them to do something at a time and prohibiting its like afterward, so that it would be like doing something at a time and leaving its like afterward?
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer argument. The opponent claims that God inflicts pain and punishment after bestowing pleasure as retribution, and that this is beneficial because it deters from sin. The author refutes by asking whether this implies bada' (change in God's knowledge or will). If the opponent admits bada', they abandon their religion (since God's knowledge is eternal). If they deny bada', they must accept that God's actions vary according to time and situation. The author then extends this analogy to commands and prohibitions: if God can vary His actions for benefit, He can also command something at one time and prohibit it at another without implying bada'. This refutes the opponent's objection against abrogation or varying divine commands.

Key Terms
البداء (bada')
التفضل (bestowal of grace)
الانتقام (retribution)
المصلحة (benefit)
الأمر والنهي (command and prohibition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's changing punishments implies a change in His knowledge or will (bada').
Correction: Baqillani argues that such changes are only in created states, not in God's eternal knowledge; they are for benefit without implying change in God.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the opponent admits that God's changing pleasure to punishment implies bada'?
A) They accept abrogation
B) They abandon their religion
C) They affirm God's foreknowledge
D) They deny retribution

Key Takeaway
God's actions vary according to wisdom and benefit without any change in His eternal knowledge or will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a sudden shift from ease to hardship, do you ever doubt that God's plan is consistent? How does Baqillani's argument that God's actions vary for unchanging wisdom challenge that doubt? Commit today to seeing every change as a sign of His perfect plan, not a flaw.
?
Think of a time you struggled to accept a change in Islamic rulings or personal circumstances. Did you subconsciously attribute that change to God's indecision? How does this lesson's refutation of bada' reshape your understanding of God's eternal will? Resolve to submit to His wisdom without reservation.
?
The analogy between God's varying actions and varying commands shows that change is for benefit. Can you identify a 'change' in your own obedience—a time you shifted from a good habit to a better one—that actually grew your faith? How will you actively seek such beneficial changes in your spiritual life starting this week?

Refuting Bada' from Afflictions on the Innocent
Baqillani refutes the claim that affliction of innocents implies bada'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the claim that God's infliction of pain on children, animals, and the insane implies bada' (change in divine will).

Core Idea
Affliction of the innocent does not imply bada'; it serves a benefit, just as varied commands do.

In this passage, Baqillani presents a second argument against those who claim that God's changing commands implies bada'. He addresses the objection that God afflicts innocent beings with pain and harm. The opponent must either accept retribution (which leads to metempsychosis) or acknowledge benefit (which parallels the variation in commands). This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation; notice the two conditional arguments the author presents to the opponent.
The argument is structured as a dilemma.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The opponent is given two options: either afflictions are retribution or they are purely for benefit. See what each entails.
Retribution leads to metempsychosis; benefit leads to analogy with commands.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author shows that both alternatives fail to prove bada'. The benefit option directly parallels the variation of commands, which the opponent rejects as implying bada'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : ما تقولون أيضاً في إيلام الأطفال والبهائم والمجانين بعد الذاذهم ونقض بنيتهم بعد صحتها وتقطع جوارحهم بعد سلامتهم والذهاب بأسماعهم وأبصارهم؟ أتقولون إن ذلك على سبيل الانتقام منهم؟ فإن قالوا أجل تركوا قولهم ولحقوا بأهل التناسخ الذين يقولون إن هذه الأرواح المحبوسة في البهائم والأطفال قد غضب الله عليهم ونقلهم في الأكوان والأدوار وذلك ترك قولهم وإن قالوا ليس تغير حالات الأطفال في هذه الأمور انتقاماً ولكن ذلك على سبيل المصالح فقط قيل لهم مثله في الأمر بالشيء في وقت والنهي عن مثله بعده.
Then it is said to them: What do you also say about inflicting pain on children, animals, and the insane after their pleasures, and breaking their bodies after their health, and cutting their limbs after their safety, and taking away their hearing and sight? Do you say that is by way of retribution against them? If they say 'yes', they abandon their statement and join the people of metempsychosis (tanasukh) who say that these souls imprisoned in animals and children have been angered by God and He transferred them in the existences and cycles. And that is abandoning their statement. If they say: 'The change of states of children in these matters is not retribution, but only by way of benefits.' It is said to them: The like of that in commanding something at a time and prohibiting its like afterward.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This second objection-and-answer addresses the issue of innocent beings (children, animals, the insane) suffering pain and bodily harm. The opponent might claim that such suffering is retribution for previous sins. The author argues that if they say it is retribution, they are forced to accept metempsychosis (transmigration of souls), which is contrary to Islamic teaching. If they deny retribution and say it is purely for benefit, then the author again uses the analogy of commanding and prohibiting at different times: if God can cause benefit through varying physical states, He can also vary His commands without implying bada'.

Key Terms
الإيلام (inflicting pain)
التناسخ (metempsychosis)
المصلحة (benefit)
الأمر والنهي (command and prohibition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The affliction of innocent beings implies bada' or injustice on God's part.
Correction: Baqillani shows that such afflictions are either retribution (leading to absurd metempsychosis) or for benefit (which refutes bada' by analogy with commands).

Checkpoint
What consequences do the opponents face if they claim that afflictions on children are retribution?
A) They must affirm bada'
B) They must accept metempsychosis
C) They must deny God's mercy
D) They must reject divine wisdom

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes bada' by showing that afflictions on the innocent are for benefit, not retribution, and thus analogous to varied divine commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or hardship, do you ever secretly believe that God has changed His mind about you or is punishing you without cause? How does this lesson's argument that all afflictions are for benefit challenge that assumption?
?
Think of a command from God that you find difficult to understand or that seems to change with circumstances. Are you willing to trust that there is a wisdom and benefit behind it, even when you cannot see it?
?
If you were in the position of the opponent, what would it take for you to admit that your reasoning was flawed? This lesson forces you to confront the logic of your own objections to God's actions – will you let it reshape your heart?

Refutation of Bada' from Changes in Inanimate Objects
Baqillani refutes the claim that God's changes in inanimate objects imply bada'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the example of opposite changes in inanimate objects to refute the doctrine of bada' (change of mind in God).

Core Idea
God's changes in creation reflect wisdom, not bada'.

This lesson continues Baqillani's cumulative argument against bada'. He now turns to the natural world, listing a series of opposite transformations that occur in inanimate objects—such as breaking after composing, or moistening after drying. He then forces the opponent into a dilemma: either these changes imply bada' (which is heretical) or they are for benefit at different times, which then supports the possibility of abrogation in divine commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the list of opposite changes (e.g., breaking after composing, making square after hexagonal) and the question that follows.
Pay attention to the rhythm of the questioning—it is meant to corner the opponent.
2
Identify the dilemma
Baqillani presents an either-or question: does God's performing these opposite actions imply bada' or not? Recognize that this is a forced choice with no neutral ground.
3
Analyze the opponent's potential escape
The opponent says 'no bada'' but claims the changes are for benefit at different times. Understand that this is an attempt to avoid the first horn of the dilemma.
Baqillani counters that the same logic applies to commands: God can command one thing at one time and prohibit it at another for the same reason.
4
See how Baqillani closes the argument
Notice that Baqillani declares the opponent has no answer to this analogy. This completes his proof that varying commands do not imply bada'.
This lesson ties together the previous arguments about abrogation and human commands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يُسألون عن نقض الجماد بعد تأليفه وتربيعه بعد تسديسه وترطيبه بعد تيبيسه وتحريكه بعد تسكينه وتسويده بعد تبييضه وغير ذلك من تغير أحواله وأوصافه فيقال لهم، البداء من الله تعالى واستدراك علم غير ما فعله ونقض ما ألفه وفرق ما جمعه وحرك ما سكنه وأحيـا مـا أمـاتـه أم لبداء؟ فإن قالوا لبَداء تركوا دينهم واعتلالهم وإن قالوا لا البداء، ولكن لأن النقض في وقت مصلحة للمكلفين والتأليف مصلحة في غيره وكذلك التحريك والتسكين قيل لهم مثله في الأمر بالشيء وقت والنهي عن مثله في غيره ولا جواب لهم عن شيء من ذلك.
And similarly they are asked about breaking a solid object after composing it, and making it square after making it hexagonal, and moistening it after drying it, and moving it after stilling it, and blackening it after whitening it, and other changes of its states and attributes. So it is said to them: Is there bada' (change of mind) from God, exalted, and acquisition of knowledge of other than what He did, and breaking what He composed, and separating what He gathered, and moving what He stilled, and reviving what He caused to die, or is there no bada'? If they say 'there is bada'', they abandon their religion and their argument. If they say 'no bada', but because the breaking at a time is a benefit for the morally responsible, and the composing is a benefit at another time, and likewise moving and stilling.' It is said to them: The like of that in commanding something at a time and prohibiting its like at another time, and there is no answer for them about any of that.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This third argument extends the refutation to inanimate objects. The author lists examples of opposite changes in physical objects (e.g., breaking after joining, squaring after hexagon-ing, etc.) and asks whether such changes imply bada' in God. If the opponents answer yes, they leave their religion. If they answer no, and claim these changes are beneficial at different times, then the author applies the same analogy: if God can change physical states for benefit, He can also command one thing at one time and prohibit it at another, and they have no valid objection. This closes the cumulative proof that varying divine commands do not imply bada'.

Key Terms
الجماد
التأليف والنقض
التربيع والتسديس
البداء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if God changes the state of an object, it implies He changed His mind (bada').
Correction: Baqillani shows that these changes are part of a preordained plan for the benefit of creation, not a correction of knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the opponent says that God's changing inanimate objects implies bada'?
A) They must accept abrogation
B) They abandon their religion and their argument
C) They deny divine power
D) They admit ignorance

Key Takeaway
God's actions in creation, including changing inanimate objects, are consistent with His wisdom and do not imply bada'—just as abrogation of commands is for benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever, when your plans suddenly change, silently accuse God of changing His mind or being uncertain? How would you answer yourself using the argument in this passage?
?
When you see tragedy or hardship in creation, do you first assume God's wisdom or do you doubt His consistency? What would it take for you to trust that every change is for your benefit, even if you don't see it?
?
Can you recall a time when you resisted a change in your life because you thought the previous situation was better? Did you consider that God's wisdom might require that very change for your ultimate good? Commit to reminding yourself of this lesson when change comes.

First Objection: Why God Cannot Be a Body
Baqillani answers the objection that the Eternal could be a body by defining body as composite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical kalam theologians argued that God cannot be a body, based on the definition of body as essentially composed and aggregated.

Core Idea
If body means composite, then the simple Eternal cannot be a body.

Baqillani responds to an objection from the Anthropomorphists (al-Mujassima) who claimed that the Eternal could be a body. He does not simply deny; he provides a linguistic and conceptual argument. First, he establishes the true definition of 'body' (jism) as that which is composed and aggregated. Then, since God is one and not composed, He cannot be a body. This lesson examines that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation. Notice the structure: objection, answer with definition, linguistic proof, conclusion.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Understand the objection
The objector asks: Why do you deny that the Eternal could be a body? This reflects the belief of the Mujassima that God has a body. Baqillani takes it seriously.
The objection is not dismissed; it is answered with evidence.
3
Analyze the definition of body
Baqillani claims that the true reality (haqiqa) of body is that it is composed (mu'allaf) and aggregated (mujtami'). He supports this with linguistic evidence: the words 'jaseem' and 'ajsam' refer to composition, not other attributes.
Focus on the phrase 'حقيقة الجسم أنه مؤلَّفٌ مُجتمع'.
4
Connect definition to conclusion
If body is defined by composition, then anything that is not composed cannot be a body. Since the Eternal is one and not composed (not aggregated), He cannot be a body.
The conclusion follows necessarily from the definition.
5
Reflect on the method
Notice Baqillani uses linguistic analysis to establish a theological truth. This is a distinctive method of kalam: using language to define terms before arguing.
Consider how this differs from simply citing a scriptural text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام على المجسمة. إن قال قائل : لم أنكرتم أن يكون القديم سبحانه جسماً؟ قيل له : لِمَا قَدّمناه مِنْ قَبْلُ وهو أن حقيقة الجسم أنه مؤلَّفٌ مُجتمع بدليل قولهم : رجل جسيم وزيد أجسم من عمرو وعلماً بأنهم يقصُرون هذه المبالغة على ضرب من ضروب التأليف في جهة العرض والطول ولا يوقعونها بزيادة شيء من صفات الجسم سوى التأليف فلما لم يجز يكون القديم مجتمعاً مؤتلفاً وكان شيئاً واحداً ثبت أنه تعالى ليس بجسم.
Chapter on the discussion concerning the Anthropomorphists. If someone says: Why do you deny that the Eternal, glorified be He, could be a body? It is said to him: Because of what we have previously established, which is that the reality of body is that it is composed and aggregated, by evidence of their saying: a man is corpulent and Zayd is more corporeal than Amr, and by knowledge that they restrict this exaggeration to a type of composition in terms of width and length, and they do not use it with an increase of any attribute of body other than composition. So when it is not permissible for the Eternal to be aggregated and composed, and He was one thing, it is established that He, exalted be He, is not a body.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector asks: Why do you (theologians) deny that God (the Eternal) could be a body? The answer begins with a previously established principle: the reality (haqiqa) of 'body' (jism) is that it is composed (mu'allaf) and aggregated (mujtami'). The proof for this definition is linguistic: Arabs say 'rajul jaseem' (a bulky man) and 'Zayd ajsam min Amr' (Zayd is more corporeal than Amr). The superlative form 'ajsam' indicates a degree of composition, not an increase in other bodily attributes like color or weight. Thus, 'body' essentially means something composed of parts. Since the Eternal is one (single, not composed), He cannot be a body. The argument follows: if composition is the defining characteristic of body, and God is not composite, then God is not a body.

Key Terms
المجسمة
الجسم
مؤلَّف
مجتمع
حقيقة الجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Baqillani simply asserts that God is not a body without proof.
Correction: Baqillani provides a careful linguistic argument: he defines body as essentially composed, then shows that the non-composite Eternal cannot be a body.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the defining characteristic of a body (jism)?
A) Having width and length
B) Being composed or aggregated
C) Being visible
D) Being capable of movement

Key Takeaway
Baqillani argues that the defining characteristic of a body is composition, so the non-composite Eternal cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God is not composed of any parts, does your mind ever still picture Him as having edges or boundaries? What would it take to truly purify your mental image of God from any hint of composition?
?
If the essence of body is composition, and God is not a body, then what does that mean for how you approach Him in your daily life? Are there ways you treat God as though He were limited or physical?
?
Baqillani uses linguistic evidence to clarify God's nature. How often do you let the precise meanings of words about God shape your understanding? Will you commit to investigating one term about God's attributes this week to deepen your conviction?

Why the Eternal Cannot Be Composite: First Reason
Baqillani gives the first reason why the Eternal cannot be composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's first argument that if God were composed of parts, He would necessarily have a location and occupy space, which is incompatible with divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Composition implies location, and location is impossible for the Eternal.

The objectors ask why the Eternal cannot be made of parts. Baqillani begins his answer with several reasons. The passage we have gives the first reason: composition would entail that God has a place and occupies space, which implies limitation. This lesson explores that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection, then the answer begins with 'from several aspects' (min wujuh).
The answer is incomplete in this passage, but the first aspect is fully given.
2
Identify the key claim
The first reason is that composition would necessitate God being 'possessor of a place' (dha hayyiz) and 'occupation in existence' (shughl). Understand why these imply limitation.
Place and occupation are properties of physical objects, not the Creator.
3
Analyze the argument about mutual touching
Baqillani adds that if God were composed, each part would touch adjacent parts, but it would be impossible for a part to touch two different things from the same direction. This shows composition imposes restrictions.
This is based on the principle of mutual contact (tamass).
4
Reflect on why this matters
Think about how this argument preserves divine transcendence (tanzih). Why did Baqillani present this as the first of several reasons?
This connects to the broader theme of God's uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: ومن أين استحال أن يكون القديم مجتمعاً مؤتلفاً؟ قيل لهم : من وجوه : أحدها أن ذلك لو جاز عليه لوجب أن يكون ذا حيز وشغل في الوجود وأن يستحيل أن يماس كل بعض من أبعاضه وجزء من أجزائه ما ماسه الأبعاض وأجزاء الجواهر أيضاً من جهة ما هما متماسان لأن الشيء المماس لغيره لا يجوز أن يماسه ويماس غيره من جهة واحدة
If they say: From where is it impossible that the Eternal be aggregated and composed? It is said to them: From several aspects: One of them is that if that were permissible for Him, it would necessitate that He be possessor of location and occupation in existence, and that it would be impossible that every part of His parts and every component of His components touch what the parts and components of substances also touch, from the aspect that they are both mutually touching, because the thing touching something else is not allowed to touch it and also touch something else from the same direction.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a follow-up objection and the beginning of its answer. The objectors ask: How do you know that it is impossible for the Eternal to be aggregated and composed? Baqillani begins his answer by noting there are several aspects (wujuh). He then gives the first aspect: if composition were possible for God, He would necessarily have a place (hayyiz) and occupy space (shughl) in existence. This implies physical location. Furthermore, in a composite being, each part touches adjacent parts. But if God had parts, it would be impossible for a given part to touch multiple other parts from the same direction simultaneously. The argument uses the principle of mutual contact (tamass): a thing that touches another cannot also touch a third thing from the same direction. This would be a limitation, and thus incompatible with divine perfection. The answer is incomplete here, but the first reason is given. The text likely continues with more reasons.

Key Terms
مجتمعاً مؤتلفاً
حيز
شغل
متماسان
المماس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is purely physical and does not apply to a spiritual being.
Correction: The argument is logical: composition inherently involves spatiality and limitation, which are incompatible with the divine nature.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani argue would necessarily follow if the Eternal were composite?
A) He would have color and shape.
B) He would have a location and occupy space.
C) He would be divisible into infinite parts.
D) He would be subject to change.

Key Takeaway
The first reason Baqillani gives against God being composite is that composition would require God to have a location and occupy space, which contradicts divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking of Allah as having parts or being located somewhere, like in the sky? How does this argument challenge that assumption, and what can you do to purify your concept of Tawhid?
?
If Allah were composite, He would be limited and dependent on His parts. How does realizing His absolute simplicity change the way you rely on Him in your daily struggles?
?
You believe Allah is the Greatest, but do your thoughts about His nature truly reflect that? Consider one area where you might be inadvertently limiting your understanding of His perfection, and commit to learning more about His attributes.

What is a Body? The Linguistic Argument
Objection and definitional refutation via language

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani defines a body as composed and aggregated, and how he uses linguistic evidence to argue that God is not a body.

Core Idea
A body is defined by composition; God is one, so He cannot be a body.

In this passage, Baqillani begins his refutation of the anthropomorphists by establishing a clear definition of what a body is. He argues from language itself that a body is inherently composed and aggregated. Since God is said in revelation to be one and not composed, the claim that He is a body is self-contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Notice how Baqillani frames the argument: an opponent asks why God cannot be a body, and Baqillani gives a direct answer based on definition.
The objection is typical of the anthropomorphist position.
2
Examine the linguistic evidence
Baqillani uses the Arabic words 'jaseem' and 'ajsam' to show that body (jism) implies composition (tālīf) in dimensions.
Linguistic analysis is a key method in kalam.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since God is one (shay’ wāhid) and not composed, He cannot be a body. Write this logical step in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام على المجسمة. إن قال قائل: لم أنكرتم أن يكون القديم سبحانه جسماً؟ قيل له: لما قدمناه من قبل وهو أن حقيقة الجسم أنه مؤلف مجتمع بدليل قولهم: رجل جسيم وزيد أجسم من عمرو، وعلماً بأنهم يقصرون هذه المبالغة على ضرب من ضروب التأليف في جهة العرض والطول ولا يوقعونها بزيادة شيء من صفات الجسم سوى التأليف، فلما لم يجز يكون القديم مجتمعاً مؤتلفاً وكان شيئاً واحداً ثبت أنه تعالى ليس بجسم. فإن قالوا: ومن أين استحال أن يكون القديم مجتمعاً مؤتلفاً؟ قيل لهم: من وجوه: أحدها أن ذلك لو جاز عليه لوجب أن يكون ذا حيز وشغل في الوجود وأن يستحيل أن يماس كل بعض من أبعاضه وجزء من أجزائه ما ماشه الأبعاض وأجزاء الجواهر أيضاً من جهة ما هما متماسان، لأن الشيء المماس لغيره لا يجوز أن يماسه ويماس غيره من جهة واحدة. وليس يقع هذا التمانع من المماسة إلا للتحيز والشغل، ألا ترى أن العرض الموجود بالمكان إذا لم يكن له حيز وشغل لم يمنع وجوده من وجود غيره من الأعراض في موضعه؟ وإذا ثبت ذلك، وجب أن تكون سائر الأبعاض المجتمعة ذا حيز وشغل، وما هذه سبيله فلا بد أن يكون حاملاً للأعراض ومن جنس الجواهر والأجسام. فلما لم يجز أن يكون القديم سبحانه من جنس شيء من المخلوقات لأنه لو كان كذلك لسد مسد المخلوق وناب منابه واستحق من الوصف لنفسه ما يستحقه ما هو مثله لنفسه، فلما لم يجب أن يكون القديم سبحانه محدثاً والمحدث قديماً ثبت أنه لا يجوز أن يكون القديم سبحانه مؤتلفاً مجتمعاً. ويدل على ذلك أيضاً أنه لو كان القديم سبحانه ذا أبعاض مجتمعة لوجب أن تكون أبعاضه قائمة بأنفسها ومحتملة للصفات ولم يخل كل بعض ولا عالم ولا قادر، فإن كان أن يكون عالماً قادراً حياً أو غير حي، وواحد منها فقط هو الحي العالم القادر دون سائرها وجب أن يكون ذلك البعض منه هو الإله المعبود المستوجب للشكر دون غيره، وهذا يوجب أن تكون العبادة والشكر واجبين لبعض القديم دون جميعه، وهذا كفر من قول الأمة كافة. وإن كانت سائر أبعاضه عالمة حية قادرة وجب جواز تفرُّد كل شيء منها بفعل غير فعل صاحبه وأن يكون كل واحد منها...
Chapter on discourse against the anthropomorphists. If a sayer says: Why do you deny that the Eternal (سبحانه) is a body? It is said to him: For what we have presented previously, which is that the reality of a body is that it is composed and aggregated, evidenced by their saying: a man is jaseem (large-bodied) and Zayd is ajsam (more large-bodied) than Amr, and knowledge that they restrict this hyperbole to a type of composition in the dimension of width and length, and they do not apply it by adding any of the attributes of a body besides composition. So when it is not permissible for the Eternal to be aggregated and composed, and He is one thing, it is established that He (تعالى) is not a body. Then if they say: From where is it impossible that the Eternal be aggregated and composed? It is said to them: From aspects: One of them is that if that were allowable for Him, it would necessitate that He possess spatial location and occupation in existence, and that it be impossible for each part of His parts and portion of His portions to touch what touches the parts and portions of atoms also, from the aspect of both being mutually touching, because the thing touching another is not allowed to touch it and touch another from one aspect. And this mutual exclusion from touching occurs only due to spatial location and occupation. Do you not see that an accident existing in a place, if it has no spatial location and occupation, does not prevent its existence from the existence of other accidents in its location? And when that is established, it necessitates that all the aggregated parts possess spatial location and occupation, and whatever is this way must necessarily be a bearer of accidents and of the genus of atoms and bodies. So when it is not permissible for the Eternal (سبحانه) to be of the genus of any created thing—because if He were like that, He would take the place of the created and stand in its stead and deserve for Himself the description that what is like Him deserves for itself—and when it is not necessary that the Eternal (سبحانه) be originated and the originated be eternal, it is established that it is not permissible for the Eternal (سبحانه) to be composed and aggregated. And that is also indicated by the fact that if the Eternal (سبحانه) had aggregated parts, it would necessitate that His parts be self-subsistent and capable of attributes, and each part would not be devoid of being knowledgeable and powerful. So if it is that it is knowledgeable, powerful, living, or not living, and only one of them is the living, knowledgeable, powerful one apart from the others, it would necessitate that that part of Him is the deity worshipped, deserving of gratitude apart from others, and this would necessitate that worship and gratitude be obligatory for a part of the Eternal apart from all of Him, and this is disbelief according to the consensus of the community. And if the rest of His parts were knowledgeable, living, powerful, it would necessitate the permissibility of each of them acting independently with an act different from the act of its companion, and that each one of them be...
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the anthropomorphists (al-mujassima) who claim that God is a body. It begins with an objection: 'Why do you deny that God is a body?' The author answers by referring to a previous definition: a body is composed (mu'allaf) and aggregated (mujtama'), as shown by language (e.g., 'jaseem' for large-bodied). Since God is one and not composed, He is not a body. The opponent then asks why composition is impossible for God. The author gives multiple aspects (wujuh). The first aspect is a detailed argument: if God were composed, He would have spatial location (hayyiz) and occupation (shughl), and each part would touch others. This mutual exclusion is only possible for things with spatial location, unlike accidents which can coexist in the same place. Therefore, composed things are bearers of accidents and of the genus of atoms and bodies. God cannot be of the genus of creatures, because that would make Him like them, which is impossible. Thus, God cannot be composed. The author then adds a second argument (incomplete in the OCR): if God had parts, each part would be self-subsistent. A dilemma arises: either only one part is the living, knowing, powerful deity (making worship due to that part alone, which is kufr), or all parts are such (leading to multiple independent deities). The argument is cut off before its conclusion.

Key Terms
جسم
مؤلف
مجتمع
جسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument about 'jaseem' means that God is not huge or large.
Correction: The point is not about size but composition: 'jaseem' refers to increased composition in dimensions, not mere volume.

Checkpoint
What linguistic evidence does Baqillani use to define a body?
A) The words 'hayyiz' and 'shughl'
B) The words 'jaseem' and 'ajsam'
C) The words 'muallaf' and 'mujtama'
D) The words 'qadim' and 'muhdath'

Key Takeaway
A body is defined as composed and aggregated; since God is one, He is not a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah in a physical form, even subtly? What does the definition of 'body' as necessarily composed reveal about the danger of such imaginings? Commit to replacing any mental image with the truth of His oneness.
?
When you declare 'Allahu Ahad' (God is One), do you truly internalize that His oneness excludes all composition and division? How would your worship change if you let this reality penetrate your heart? Resolve to ponder this before your next prayer.
?
The imams used precise language to protect tawheed. In your own understanding of faith, where might you be accepting vague or imprecise ideas about Allah? What step will you take today to replace that vagueness with clear, scriptural knowledge?

Why Composition is Impossible for God
Aspect-based argument against composition of God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that if God were composed, He would have spatial location and be like created bodies, which is impossible.

Core Idea
If God were composed, He would have parts that touch and occupy space, making Him like creation.

After the initial definition, Baqillani addresses the deeper question: why can't God be composed? He presents a logical argument based on the necessary properties of composed things—namely, spatial location and mutual exclusion. This argument shows that composition implies being a bearer of accidents and belonging to the genus of bodies, which cannot apply to the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question
The opponent asks why composition is impossible for God. Baqillani responds with 'from aspects' (wujuh) and gives the first aspect.
The first aspect is a detailed reductio ad absurdum.
2
Follow the conditional logic
Trace the conditional: if God were composed, He would have spatial location and mutual exclusion among parts. This leads to being a bearer of accidents.
Note the contrast with accidents, which do not occupy space.
3
Understand the impossibility of likeness to creation
Since composed things are of the genus of created bodies, God cannot be composed because that would make Him equal to creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام على المجسمة. إن قال قائل: لم أنكرتم أن يكون القديم سبحانه جسماً؟ قيل له: لما قدمناه من قبل وهو أن حقيقة الجسم أنه مؤلف مجتمع بدليل قولهم: رجل جسيم وزيد أجسم من عمرو، وعلماً بأنهم يقصرون هذه المبالغة على ضرب من ضروب التأليف في جهة العرض والطول ولا يوقعونها بزيادة شيء من صفات الجسم سوى التأليف، فلما لم يجز يكون القديم مجتمعاً مؤتلفاً وكان شيئاً واحداً ثبت أنه تعالى ليس بجسم. فإن قالوا: ومن أين استحال أن يكون القديم مجتمعاً مؤتلفاً؟ قيل لهم: من وجوه: أحدها أن ذلك لو جاز عليه لوجب أن يكون ذا حيز وشغل في الوجود وأن يستحيل أن يماس كل بعض من أبعاضه وجزء من أجزائه ما ماشه الأبعاض وأجزاء الجواهر أيضاً من جهة ما هما متماسان، لأن الشيء المماس لغيره لا يجوز أن يماسه ويماس غيره من جهة واحدة. وليس يقع هذا التمانع من المماسة إلا للتحيز والشغل، ألا ترى أن العرض الموجود بالمكان إذا لم يكن له حيز وشغل لم يمنع وجوده من وجود غيره من الأعراض في موضعه؟ وإذا ثبت ذلك، وجب أن تكون سائر الأبعاض المجتمعة ذا حيز وشغل، وما هذه سبيله فلا بد أن يكون حاملاً للأعراض ومن جنس الجواهر والأجسام. فلما لم يجز أن يكون القديم سبحانه من جنس شيء من المخلوقات لأنه لو كان كذلك لسد مسد المخلوق وناب منابه واستحق من الوصف لنفسه ما يستحقه ما هو مثله لنفسه، فلما لم يجب أن يكون القديم سبحانه محدثاً والمحدث قديماً ثبت أنه لا يجوز أن يكون القديم سبحانه مؤتلفاً مجتمعاً. ويدل على ذلك أيضاً أنه لو كان القديم سبحانه ذا أبعاض مجتمعة لوجب أن تكون أبعاضه قائمة بأنفسها ومحتملة للصفات ولم يخل كل بعض ولا عالم ولا قادر، فإن كان أن يكون عالماً قادراً حياً أو غير حي، وواحد منها فقط هو الحي العالم القادر دون سائرها وجب أن يكون ذلك البعض منه هو الإله المعبود المستوجب للشكر دون غيره، وهذا يوجب أن تكون العبادة والشكر واجبين لبعض القديم دون جميعه، وهذا كفر من قول الأمة كافة. وإن كانت سائر أبعاضه عالمة حية قادرة وجب جواز تفرُّد كل شيء منها بفعل غير فعل صاحبه وأن يكون كل واحد منها...

The Dilemma of Divine Parts
Second argument: the problem of parts leading to contradictory conclusions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's second argument that if God had parts, it would lead to either worshipping a part or multiple gods, both absurd.

Core Idea
If God had parts, only one part would be the true deity, or all parts would be separate gods—both impossible.

Baqillani presents a second argument against God being a body: if He had parts (a'bad), each part would be self-subsistent and capable of attributes. This leads to a dilemma: either only one part is the living, knowing, powerful deity (making worship due to that part alone, which is kufr) or all parts are such (leading to multiple independent deities). The argument is incomplete in the manuscript but the logic is clear.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
If God had parts, each part would be self-subsistent and would have to be either knowing/powerful/living or not.
The premise comes from the nature of body parts.
2
Explore the first horn
If only one part is the true deity, then worship is only for that part, not the whole God—this is disbelief.
Baqillani calls this 'kufr' by consensus.
3
Explore the second horn
If all parts are divine, they could act independently, implying multiple gods.
The argument is cut off, but the implication is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes God actually has parts; it's a hypothetical dilemma.

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
5 min
Refuting the Corporealist Analogy
Baqillani refutes the corporealist analogy with a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani refutes the claim that God must be a body by showing that the analogical argument from witnessed agents would force unacceptable attributes onto God, thus proving too much.

Core Idea
Analogy from the created to the Creator proves too much—it proves nothing.

How can we know that God is not a body when every agent we see in the world is embodied? The opponents of tawhid use this very observation to argue that the Creator must also be a body. In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani turns their own reasoning against them, exposing its fatal flaw. Read carefully to see how a simple logical twist dismantles the corporealist argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing for analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a question, the opponents' claim, and Baqillani's rebuttal.
The Arabic uses a conditional structure: 'if they say... then it is said to them...'.
2
Identify the opponents' analogy
The opponents argue: all agents we observe (shahid) are bodies, so the unseen agent (gha'ib) must also be a body. Find this in the text and see how it is presented.
3
Understand Baqillani's counter
Baqillani shows that if the analogy is applied consistently, it forces God to have all the attributes of created agents: being composed, originated, shaped, etc. He then presents the dilemma: accept these or reject the analogy.
The key move is extending the analogy to its logical conclusion.
4
Grasp the dilemma
The opponents face a choice: if they persist, they abandon tawhid; if they refuse the additional attributes, they undermine their own inference. This makes the argument self-defeating.
This type of argument is called reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة ويقال لهم : ما الدليل على أن صانع العالم جسم؟ فإن قالوا : لأننا لم نجد في الشاهد والمعقول فاعلاً إلا جسماً فوجب القضاء بذلك على الغائب قيل لهم : فيجب على موضوع استدلالكم هذا أن يكون القديم سبحانه مُؤلَّفاً مُحدثاً مُصَوَّراً ذا حَيّز وقَبُول للأعراض لأنكم لم تجدوا في الشاهد وتعقلوا فاعلا إلا كذلك فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا قولهم وفارقوا التوحيد وإن أبوا نقضوا استدلالهم.
A question: And it is said to them: 'What is the evidence that the Maker of the world is a body?' If they say: 'Because in the witnessed world and in reason we have not found any agent except a body, so judgment must be passed accordingly upon the unseen,' it is said to them: 'Then according to the basis of your inference, it would be necessary that the Pre-Eternal (glorified be He) be composed, originated, shaped, possessing location and receptivity to accidents, because you have not found in the witnessed world and you do not comprehend an agent except likewise. If they persist in that, they abandon their own doctrine and depart from monotheism; and if they refuse, they invalidate their own inference.'
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a proof for God's corporeality. The opponents argue by analogy: every agent we observe in this world (shahid) is a body, so the unseen agent (gha'ib) — the Maker of the world — must also be a body. Baqillani turns the analogy back on them: if the analogy is applied consistently, then the Pre-Eternal must also be composed, originated, shaped, located, and receptive to accidents, because all witnessed agents have those properties. If the opponents accept that conclusion, they abandon tawhid (monotheism). If they reject those attributes, they break their own analogical reasoning. Thus the argument is self-defeating.

غائب
فاعل

محدث
مؤلف
مصور
حيز
قبول للأعراض
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Baqillani denies God is an agent; rather, he denies that an agent must be a body.
Correction: Baqillani affirms God's agency but rejects the corporealist notion that agency requires a body, as the analogy would impose limitations unbecoming of the Creator.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani refute the claim that God must be a body because all witnessed agents are bodies?
A) He argues that God is not an agent in the same sense.
B) He shows that the analogy would force God to have all other properties of created agents, which is unacceptable.
C) He denies that all witnessed agents are bodies.
D) He brings a proof from revelation that God is not a body.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that the analogical argument for God's corporeality is self-defeating because it would require God to possess the very imperfections that define created bodies.

?
In your prayer or contemplation, do you catch yourself visualizing God in a certain way? What concrete steps can you take today to purify your understanding of God from any trace of corporealism?
?

Why Shared Names Do Not Imply Essential Similarity
Baqillani responds to an objection: shared names do not necessitate shared essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that shared names between God and creation do not necessitate similarity in essence or attributes.

Core Idea
Sharing a name does not make two things essentially alike.

This passage addresses a common objection: if God and humans share attributes like 'living', does that imply similarity? Baqillani explains that essential likeness depends on full interchangeability in all judgments, not on shared names. He clarifies that the proof of homogeneity comes from evidence, not vocabulary.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: an objector poses a question, and Baqillani responds. Pay attention to the key terms like 'ishtirak' (sharing) and 'tajanus' (homogeneity).
The objector's assumption is that shared names imply shared essences.
2
Identify the key principle
Baqillani states that homogeneity of two things is due to their essences, not their names. Identify the condition he gives for true similarity (mithlayn): each takes the place of the other in all judgments.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Apply to Creator and creation
See how Baqillani applies this principle to the case of the Eternal (al-Qadim) and the originated (al-muhdath). He argues that sharing names like 'living' or 'knowing' does not necessitate similarity, because the Creator is incomparable.
4
Understand the role of proof
Baqillani uses examples (two blacknesses, two substances) to show that homogeneity is established by proof (dalil) of interchangeability, not by shared names. Reflect on how this reinforces the argument against anthropomorphism.
Derived names also do not imply likeness of the source meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل: فهل في الأسماء والصفات النفسية والمعنوية ما يوجب الاشتراك فيما جانسها من المسميات؟ قيل له: ليس في الأسماء شيء يوجب الاشتراك فيه تجانساً وتماثلاً، وإنما يجب تجانس الشيئين لأنفسهما. فوجب، إذا كشفت من حالهما أن كل واحد منهما يسدّ مسدّ الآخر وينوب منابه في جميع أحكامه وأوصافه، أن يكونا مثلين. وليس يجب ذلك لهما لأجل اشتراكهما في شيء من الأسماء والصفات. فكذلك ما لم تجب مشابهة المحدث للقديم سبحانه إذا شاركه في كونه شيئاً موجوداً وفي كونه حياً عالماً قادراً سميعاً بصيراً حكيماً وغير ذلك من الأسماء. ولم يجب اختلاف المحدثين، إذا افترقا في هذه الأسماء والصفات. وليس الذي أوجب تجانس السوادين والجوهرين اشتراكهما في هذين الاسمين والوصفين المستحقين للنفس، ولكن لأجل قيام الدليل على أن كل واحد منهما يسد مسد الآخر وينوب منابه. وكذلك اشتراك الشيئين في الاسمين المشتقين لا يوجب تشابه ما اشتقا منه معنيين. فلذلك لم يجب اشتباه صفات القديم سبحانه وصفاتنا، وإن كانت توجب الاشتقاق على وجه واحد. وبالله التوفيق.
If a questioner says: 'Are there among the names and the essential (nafsiyyah) and significatory (ma'nawiyyah) attributes something that necessitates sharing (ishtirak) in what is similar to them (jānasahā) among the named things?' It is said to him: There is nothing in names that necessitates sharing in homogenousness (tajāfus) and similarity (tamāthul); rather, the homogenousness of two things is necessary for their essences (li-anfusihimā). So when it is disclosed from their state that each one of them takes the place of the other and substitutes for it in all its judgments and attributes, then it is necessary that they be similar (mithlayn). And that is not necessary for them due to their sharing in any name or attribute. Similarly, it is not necessary that the originated (al-muhdath) be similar to the Eternal (al-qadīm) subḥānahu when it shares with Him in being an existing thing (shay' mawjūd) and in being living, knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing, wise, and other names. Nor is it necessary that the originated things differ when they differ in these names and attributes. And that which necessitated the homogenousness of two blacknesses and two substances is not their sharing in these two names and descriptions that are deserved for the self (mustahiqqayni lil-nafs), but rather because the proof (al-dalīl) has been established that each one of them takes the place of the other and substitutes for it. Similarly, the sharing of two things in derived names (al-ismayni al-mushtaqqayn) does not necessitate the likeness of that from which they are derived (ishtaqqā minhu) in meaning (ma'ānīn). Therefore, it is not necessary that the attributes of the Eternal (al-qadīm) subḥānahu and our attributes be similar, even if they necessitate derivation (ishtiqāq) in one manner. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer. The objector asks whether names and attributes (especially the essential and significatory ones) necessitate that things sharing names be essentially similar (ishtirak). The answer is no; likeness is not due to names but due to the essences themselves. If two things are proven to be equivalent in all judgments, they are similar (mithl). But sharing a name like 'living' does not make the created similar to the Creator, because the proof shows the Creator is incomparable. Likewise, two created things may differ despite sharing names. The proof of homogeneity is based on evidence of interchangeability, not on shared names. Derived names do not imply that the source meanings are alike. Thus, divine attributes are not like human attributes despite shared names.

التجانس
التماثل
المثل
القديم
المحدث
الاشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God and humans share names like 'living' or 'knowing', then God must be similar to humans in those respects.
Correction: Shared names do not imply shared essences; similarity requires full interchangeability in all judgments, which does not hold between Creator and creation.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the only reason two things can be considered similar (mithlayn)?
A) They share the same name.
B) They share the same attribute.
C) Each takes the place of the other in all judgments and attributes.
D) They are both created.

Key Takeaway
Sharing names does not make things similar; only proven interchangeability in all judgments establishes essential likeness.

Abu Hudhayl's Denial of Divine Transcendence
Baqillani refutes Abu Hudhayl's claim that God is not unlike creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Hudhayl's claim that God is not different from creation is a denial of divine transcendence and why Baqillani refutes it.

Core Idea
To deny God's difference from creation is to reduce Him to a body.

Baqillani introduces a dangerous claim from Abu Hudhayl, the leader of the Mu'tazila. Abu Hudhayl argued that God cannot be described as 'different' from creation because difference only applies to bodies. This passage exposes the error: equating God with creation undermines transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Abu Hudhayl uses a logical argument with a premise and conclusion.
2
Identify Abu Hudhayl's reasoning
Find the claim (that God is not different) and the reason (difference only applies to bodies). Understand the logical steps: difference is a property of bodies, so if God were different, He would be a body; since He is not a body, He cannot be different.
This argument implicitly denies God's transcendence.
3
Understand the refutation
Baqillani records this only to refute it. Consider why this claim is unacceptable: it makes God comparable to creation and denies His absolute uniqueness. The correct belief is that God is utterly unlike anything in creation, yet He is not a body.
The key is to distinguish between 'different' as incomparability and 'different' as a property of bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم أبو الهذيل شيخ المعتزلة والمقدم فيها أن الله سبحانه ليس بمخالف لخلقه لأن المخالف ليس ما حلّه الخلاف، وذلك لا يجوز إلا على الأجسام.
And Abu Hudhayl, the shaykh of the Mu'tazila and their foremost, claimed that Allah, exalted be He, is not different from His creation, because the different is not what the difference inhabits, and that is only permissible for bodies.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Hudhayl's argument that God is not different (مخالف) from His creation. He reasons that 'different' applies only to what is subject to difference (حلّه الخلاف), which he claims is only true for bodies. Baqillani records this to refute it: it denies God's transcendence and makes Him comparable to creation. The author implies this is a false analogy.

Key Terms
مخالف
الخلاف
أجسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is not different from His creation because difference only applies to bodies.
Correction: Difference (مخالفة) is an attribute of God's uniqueness, but He is not a body. The term 'different' in the context of God means He is absolutely unlike creation, not that He shares properties with bodies.

Checkpoint

A) God is unlike anything
B) God is not different from His creation
C) God is a body
D) God has no attributes

Key Takeaway
Abu Hudhayl's denial that God is different from creation leads to a denial of divine transcendence and is refuted by Baqillani.

Refutation of Anthropomorphic Interpretation: The Case of Iblis
Refutes anthropomorphic interpretation using the case of Iblis and logical analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the literal interpretation of God's attributes like 'hand' and 'face' as limbs is invalid, based on the argument from Iblis and the logical principle that God's attributes are not bound by created limitations.

Core Idea
God's attributes are real but not physical; we do not judge Him by our limited experience.

Imam Baqillani presents a powerful argument against anthropomorphism by turning to the Qur'anic story of Iblis. If 'hand' were a literal limb, Iblis could have objected that he too was created by God's hand, but he did not—proving that 'hand' means power. The passage then addresses a deeper objection about how attributes can exist without being physical, establishing a key principle of Islamic theology: we do not apply the limitations of creation to the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without trying to analyse every detail. Let the flow of the argument register before you break it down.
2
Identify the Iblis argument
Notice how the author uses Iblis's silence as evidence: if 'hand' were literal, Iblis would have used it as an excuse. Since he did not, the literal interpretation is false.
3
Understand the objection and the answer
The imaginary objector asks: how can we deny that God's attributes are limbs if we have no experience of non-physical attributes? The answer is that we do not limit God to what we experience in creation. This is a key principle of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدل على فساد تأويلهم أيضاً أنه لو كان الأمر على ما قالوه، لم يقل ذلك إبليس وعن أن يقول: وأيّ فضل لآدم علي يقتضي أن أسجد له، وأنا أيضاً بيدك خلقتني، التي هي قدرتك، وبنعمتك خلقتني؟ وفي العلم بأن الله تعالى فضل آدم عليه بخلقه بيديه دليل على فساد ما قالوه. فإن قال قائل: فما أنكرتم أن يكون وجهه ويده جارحة، إذ كنتم لم تعقلوا يد صفة ووجه صفة لا جارحة؟ يقال له: لا يجب ذلك، كما لا يجب، إذا لم نعقل حياً عالماً قادراً إلا جسماً، أن نقضي نحن وأنتم على الله تعالى بذلك. وكما لا يجب، متى كان قائماً بذاته، أن يكون جوهراً أو جسماً، لأنا وإياكم لم نجد قائماً بنفسه في شاهدنا إلا كذلك. وكذلك الجواب لهم إن قالوا: فيجب أن يكون علمه وحياته وكلامه وسائر صفاته لذاته أعراضاً أو أجناساً أو حوادث أو أغياراً له أو حالة فيه أو محتاجة له إلى قلب، واعتلوا بالوجود.
And it also indicates the corruption of their interpretation that if the matter were as they said, Iblis would not have said that, and why would he not say: 'What virtue does Adam have over me that necessitates that I prostrate to him, while I also was created by Your hand, which is Your power, and by Your grace You created me?' And in the knowledge that God Almighty preferred Adam over him by creating him with His two hands is evidence of the corruption of what they said. So if someone says: 'Why do you deny that His face and hand are limbs, since you have not conceived of a hand that is an attribute and a face that is an attribute without being a limb?' It is said to him: That is not necessary, just as it is not necessary, when we have not conceived of a living, knowing, powerful being except as a body, that we and you judge God Almighty by that. And just as it is not necessary, when something is self-subsisting, that it be a substance or a body, because we and you have not found anything self-subsisting in our witnessed world except thus. And likewise the answer to them if they say: 'Then His knowledge, life, speech, and all His attributes must be accidents or genera or originated things or other-than-Him or states in Him or needing a heart?' And they argued by existence.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage refutes the anthropomorphic interpretation of God's attributes by employing a logical analogy. The author begins by pointing out that if the attributes of 'hand' and 'face' were literal limbs, then Iblis, when ordered to prostrate to Adam, could have objected that he too was created by God's hand, implying equal status. Since Iblis did not make this argument, it shows that 'hand' here means God's power, not a physical limb. The author then addresses an objection: those who deny limbs must explain how attributes can exist without physical form. The answer is that we do not judge God by our limited experience—just as we do not require God to be a body because we only know living beings as bodies, we do not require His attributes to be limbs. The same logic applies to other attributes: if someone claims that God's knowledge, life, and speech must be accidents or originated because we only know such qualities as accidents, the author replies that this is not necessary, because God's attributes are not like created things. The passage concludes by noting that the objectors resort to arguing from existence (i.e., what is known in the created world), but that is an invalid analogy.

يد
وجه
صفة
جارحة
قدرة

جوهر
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that denying literal limbs means denying the attributes themselves or that attributes are mere metaphors.
Correction: The passage affirms that attributes like 'hand' and 'face' are real divine attributes but not physical limbs. They are meaningful and indicate God's power and presence without corporeality.

Checkpoint
Why does the author use the example of Iblis to refute anthropomorphism?
A) To show that Iblis was more knowledgeable than others.
B) To demonstrate that Iblis had a valid argument for not prostrating.
C) To prove that if 'hand' were literal, Iblis would have used it as an excuse, but he did not.
D) To establish that Adam was created without God's hand.

Key Takeaway
The attributes of God are real and meaningful but not physical; we must not measure Him by the standards of creation.

The Meaning of 'What is He?' About God
Baqillani enumerates five interpretations of 'what is He?' and provides answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the question 'What is God?' can be interpreted in five different ways, and how Baqillani responds to each interpretation, safeguarding divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The question 'What is God?' has multiple meanings, each requiring a distinct answer.

When someone asks 'What is God?', they might mean several different things. Imam Baqillani considers five possible intentions behind this question and answers each one carefully. This approach clarifies that while God is beyond genus and visual perception, He is known through His names, actions, and the evidence of His creation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dialectical context
The passage is a response to a hypothetical questioner. Recognize that Baqillani is addressing different possible meanings of the question.
2
Examine each of the five interpretations
Go through each of the five possibilities that Baqillani lists: genus, name, action, evidence, and visual demonstration. Note his specific answer for each.
3
Reflect on the lesson's key takeaway
Consider how this approach protects divine transcendence while still affirming that we can know God through His attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : فخبّرونا عن الله سبحانه : ما هو؟ . قيل له : إن أردت بقولك ما هو ما جنسه فليس هو بذي جنس لما وصفناه قبل هذا . وإن أردت بقولك ما هو ما اسمه فاسمه الله الرحمن الرحيم الحي القيوم . وإن أردت بقولك ما هو ما صنعه فصنعه العدل والإحسان والإنعام والسموات والأرض وجميع ما بينهما. وإن أردت بقولك «ما هو» ما الدلالة على وجوده فالدلالة على وجوده ما نراه ونشاهده من محكم فعله وعجيب تدبيره. وإن أردت بقولك «ما هو» أي أشيروا إليه حتى أراه فليس هو اليوم مرئياً لخلقه ومدركاً لهم فَنُرِيَكَهُ .
If someone says: Inform us about God, Glorified is He: What is He? He is told: If by your saying 'what is He' you mean its genus, then He does not have a genus, as we described before this. And if by your saying 'what is He' you mean its name, then His name is Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful, the Alive, the Self-Sustaining. And if by your saying 'what is He' you mean what He has done, then His doing is justice, beneficence, favor, the heavens and the earth and all that is between them. And if by your saying 'what is He' you mean the evidence for His existence, then the evidence for His existence is what we see and witness of His firm action and wondrous planning. And if by your saying 'what is He' you mean 'point to Him so that I may see Him', then He is not today visible to His creation nor perceptible to them that we might show Him to you.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical response to the question 'What is God?' (mā huwa). Baqillani considers five possible interpretations of the question and answers each. First, if the questioner asks about genus (jins), the answer is that God has no genus, as previously explained (i.e., He is not a member of any category). Second, if asking about His name (ism), the answer is that His names include Allah, al-Rahman, al-Rahim, al-Hayy, al-Qayyum. Third, if asking about His action (ṣunʿ), then His actions are justice, beneficence, favor, and the creation of the heavens and earth and everything between. Fourth, if asking about the evidence (dalāla) for His existence, the evidence is the firmness of His action and the wonder of His planning that we observe. Fifth, if asking for a visual demonstration—'point to Him so I may see Him'—then God is not visible in this world to His creation nor perceptible to them such that we could show Him. This response safeguards divine transcendence (tanzīh) while affirming God's knowability through names, actions, and evidence.

Key Terms
ما هو
الجنس
الاسم
الصنع
الدلالة
الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The question 'What is God?' has only one correct answer.
Correction: Baqillani shows that the question can be interpreted in five different ways, each requiring a distinct response. The answer depends on what the questioner intends.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani say if someone asks 'what is He?' meaning 'what is His genus?'
A) His genus is divine.
B) He has no genus.
C) His genus is spirit.
D) The question is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's approach shows that the question 'What is God?' must be understood in context, and the answer varies while always affirming God's uniqueness and transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Meaning of 'Where is He?' About God
Baqillani defines the question 'where' as asking about place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the question 'where' (al-ayn) asks about place, and why this is significant for discussing God's transcendence.

Core Idea
'Where' asks about place, and God is not in any place.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani begins to respond to a common question: 'Where is God?' He first clarifies what the question literally means. The word 'where' (al-ayn) is a question about place (al-makan). This sets the stage for the answer: God is not located in any place, because He transcends spatial dimensions. Although the passage is fragmentary, it establishes the key premise for affirming divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Baqillani defines the question before answering.
The passage ends abruptly, but the intended conclusion is clear.
2
Understand the key term
The word 'ayn (where) is defined as a question about place (makan). This is a linguistic clarification.
3
Consider the implication
If 'where' asks about place, then answering requires affirming or denying that God occupies a place. The intended answer is that God does not have a place.
This connects to tanzih (divine transcendence).
4
Connect to tanzih
This clarification is the basis for denying spatial attributes to God, affirming His transcendence.
Tanzih means keeping God free from imperfections and limitations.

Refuting the Question 'What is God?' – Distinguishing Five Meanings
Baqillani refutes the question 'what is God?' by distinguishing five possible meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question 'what is God?' is ambiguous and how Baqillani refutes it by distinguishing five distinct meanings.

Core Idea
Asking 'what is God?' is like asking five different questions — each has a different answer.

Baqillani anticipates a question that might seem simple but is actually loaded with ambiguity. When someone asks 'what is God?', they might be inquiring about His genus, His name, His acts, evidence for His existence, or the possibility of seeing Him. In this passage, Baqillani systematically addresses each possible meaning, demonstrating that the question itself requires clarification before any answer can be given.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repeated structure: 'if you mean... then...'.
2
Identify the five meanings
The passage presents five possible interpretations of 'what is God?'. Find each one and notice how Baqillani responds differently to each.
The five are: genus, name, creation, evidence, and visual indication.
3
Understand the first three meanings
For genus: God has no genus. For name: His name is Allah and His other names. For creation: His creation is justice, beneficence, and the heavens and earth.
These answers show God's transcendence and attributes of action.
4
Understand the last two meanings
For evidence: the signs of His existence are seen in His perfect actions. For visual indication: He is not visible in this world.
The last meaning is a refutation of anthropomorphism.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Why does Baqillani use this approach? How does it guard against misunderstanding God? Consider how this method protects tawhid.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب
فإن قال قائل : فخبّرونا عن الله سبحانه (۱) : ما هو؟ .
(۲) بذي جنس
قيل له : إن أردت بقولك ما هو ما جنسه فليس هو لما وصفناه قبل هذا (۳)) . وإن أردت بقولك ما هو ما اسمه فاسمه الله الرحمن الرحيم الحي القيوم . وإن أردت بقولك ما هو» ما صنعه فصنعه العدل والإحسان والإنعام والسموات والأرض (4) وجميع ما بينهما. وإن أردت بقولك «ما هو ما (٥) الدلالة على وجوده فالدلالة على وجوده ما نراه ونشاهده (۷) من محكم فعله وعجيب تدبيره. وإن أردت بقولك «ما هو» ، أي ؛ أشيروا إليه حتى أراه (۸) فليس هو اليوم مرئياً (۹) لخلقه ومدركاً (١٠) لهم فَنُرِيَكَهُ .
Section: If someone says: Inform us about God, exalted: what is He? It is said to him: If by your saying 'what is He' you mean its genus, then He does not have that, as we described before. And if by your saying 'what is He' you mean His name, then His name is Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful, the Living, the Self-Sustaining. And if by your saying 'what is He' you mean His creation, then His creation is justice, beneficence, bounty, the heavens and the earth and all that is between them. And if by your saying 'what is He' you mean the indication of His existence, then the indication of His existence is what we see and witness of the perfection of His action and wonder of His management. And if by your saying 'what is He' you mean 'point to Him so that I may see Him', then He is not today visible to His creation nor perceptible to them so that we could show Him to you.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the question 'what is God?' by distinguishing five possible meanings. The author first presents the question, then gives the answer structured according to different intentions of the questioner. The first meaning is asking about genus – God has no genus. The second is asking about name – the answer is the divine names. The third is asking about His act – His creation is justice and goodness. The fourth is asking about evidence of existence – the evidence is the created order. The fifth is asking for sensory indication – this is impossible in this world. The passage guards against anthropomorphism and confusion about how to ask about God.

Correction: Baqillani shows the question is ambiguous; without clarifying the intention, no single answer suffices. Each intended meaning requires a different response.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's response to 'what is God?' demonstrates that theological questions must be clarified before they can be answered, and that God transcends categories like genus and visual perception.

Refuting 'Where is God?': Transcendence Beyond Place
Baqillani refutes the question 'where is God?' by distinguishing literal and metaphorical meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the question of God's location by distinguishing the literal meaning of 'where' from the metaphorical scriptural expression.

Core Idea
God is not contained by place; 'above the throne' is not physical contact.

A common question people ask about God is 'Where is He?' In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani shows that this question misunderstands divine transcendence. He accepts the Qur'anic phrase 'above His throne' but immediately clarifies it is not a physical location. Let's see how he distinguishes literal and figurative meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic sentence carefully. Notice the structure: first the question, then the refutation, then the affirmation with a qualification.
The Arabic is concise; pay attention to the negation particles.
2
Understand the refutation
Baqillani says 'where' is a question about place, and God is not subject to place. This is the first move: reject the premise.
3
Analyze the affirmation
He then says 'He is above His throne' but adds 'not in the sense of contact or adjacency'. This shows the phrase is metaphorical, not literal.
The qualification is crucial for avoiding anthropomorphism.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Ask yourself: why does Baqillani use the scriptural language at all? Because we affirm what Allah says about Himself, but without attributing physicality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فإن قال قائل : أين (۱۶) هو؟ قيل له : الأين سؤال عن المكان وليس هو ممن يجوز أن يحويه مكان ولا تحيط به أقطار. غير أنا نقول: إنه على عرشه (۱)) . لا على معنى كون الجسم بالملاصقة والمجاورة (۲) تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً (۳) ! .

However, we say: He is above His throne. Not in the sense of a body being by contact and adjacency – exalted is He above that, greatly exalted!
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the question 'where is God?' by first clarifying that 'where' asks about place, and God is not in a place. Then the author affirms the scriptural language of 'above the throne' but immediately negates any physical interpretation: it is not by contact or adjacency. The passage upholds divine transcendence while accepting the Qur'anic expression.
