Affirming the Throne without Corporeality
Baqillani affirms Allah's Throne while denying corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that affirming Allah's Throne does not imply any physical contact or spatial containment, and that Allah is above all corporeal attributes.

Core Idea
Allah is upon His Throne, but not as a body touching it.

Baqillani begins by asserting that Allah cannot be encompassed by any place or region. Yet he affirms the Qur'anic statement that Allah is upon His Throne. This lesson clarifies the correct understanding: that this 'being upon' is not corporeal contact, but a reality befitting Allah's majesty, free from any anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice the balance between negation and affirmation.
The phrase 'لا على معنى كون الجسم' is key.
2
Identify the two claims
First, Allah cannot be contained by place. Second, He is upon His Throne, but not physically. Understand how these coexist.
3
Reflect on the meaning
Ask yourself: How does Baqillani avoid anthropomorphism while still affirming the Throne? This is a core principle of Ahl al-Sunnah.
This connects to the broader theme of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ممن يجوز أن يحويه مكان ولا تحيط به أقطار. غير أنا نقول: إنه على عرشه. لا على معنى كون الجسم بالملاصقة والمجاورة تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً.
فإن قال قائل: فمتى كان؟ قيل له: سؤالك عن هذا يقتضي كونه في زمان لم يكن قبله، لأن متى سؤال عن الزمان. وقد عرفناك أنه قديم كائن قبل الزمان، وأنه الخالق للمكان والزمان وموجود قبلهما وتوقيت وجود الشيء بعام أو مائة ألف عام يُفيد أن الموقت وجوده معدوم قبل الزمان الذي وُقت به. وذلك مما يستحيل عليه تعالى.
From whom it is impossible that a place encompasses Him and that regions surround Him. But we say: He is upon His Throne, not in the sense of a body touching or adjoining. Exalted is He above that with great exaltation. If someone says: When was He? It is said to him: Your question about this implies His being in a time before which He was not, because 'when' is a question about time. And we have informed you that He is eternal, existing before time, and that He is the Creator of space and time and exists before both. And timing the existence of something by a year or a hundred thousand years implies that the timed thing's existence was non-existent before the time it was timed to. And that is impossible for Him, Exalted.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal affirmation and refutation. It first asserts that God cannot be contained by place or regions, yet acknowledges the Qur'anic statement that He is upon His Throne, clarifying that this is not corporeal contact. Then it addresses a hypothetical question about God's temporal origin. The answer explains that asking 'when' presupposes a time before which He did not exist, but He is eternal and the Creator of time itself. Any attempt to situate Him in time implies temporal limitation, which is impossible for Him.

Key Terms
عرش
مالصقة
مجاورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that affirming the Throne means Allah is physically sitting or touching it.
Correction: Baqillani explicitly denies bodily contact; the 'being upon' is in a manner that befits Allah, not like created beings.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what does it mean to say Allah is upon His Throne?
A) That Allah is physically sitting on it.
B) That Allah is in contact with it like a body.
C) That it is a reality, but not in a corporeal sense.
D) That the Throne encompasses Allah.

Key Takeaway
Allah is upon His Throne in a way that transcends our understanding, without any physicality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear 'ar-Rahmanu 'ala al-arsh istawa', do you catch yourself forming a mental image of a sitting or resting? ?
Are you comfortable with affirming something you cannot fully imagine? Baqillani says we affirm the Throne but deny physicality—so we believe in a reality that transcends our senses. Is your faith in Allah's attributes based on your intellect or on submission to revelation? Ask Allah to grant you firm conviction in the unseen.

Refuting the Question 'When was Allah?'
Baqillani refutes the question about Allah's temporal origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why asking 'when' about Allah is invalid, because it presupposes time before His existence, but He is eternal and the Creator of time.

Core Idea
Allah is eternal, before time; asking 'when' implies a beginning.

Baqillani now addresses a hypothetical objection: 'When was Allah?' This question seems innocent, but it contains a hidden assumption that Allah exists within time. Baqillani exposes the flaw by pointing out that 'when' is a time question, and Allah is prior to time itself. This lesson unpacks the logic of his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and answer

The key phrase is 'يقتضي كونه في زمان لم يكن قبله'.
2
Understand the argument
Baqillani argues that 'when' asks for a time. If Allah were in time, He would have a beginning. But He is eternal. Therefore the question is invalid.
He also mentions that timing something by years implies its non-existence before that time.
3
Apply the principle
Reflect: How does this principle protect Allah's transcendence? Can you think of other questions that commit the same fallacy?
This applies to all temporal questions about Allah.

The Verse as Praise for Transcendence
Baqillani explains the verse negates physical and anthropomorphic attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' is a praise of Allah by negating physical and anthropomorphic descriptions, according to Baqillani.

Core Idea
The verse affirms transcendence by negating body, indwelling, resemblance, and parenthood.

Baqillani presents a profound interpretation of the famous verse. He shows that the praise lies in negating any physical or human-like attributes from Allah. This refutes various groups who ascribe such limitations. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let Baqillani's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the four negations listed.
2
Identify the four negations
The passage lists four things that are negated from Allah: being a formed spatial body, being an indwelling substance (as Christians claim), resembling anything (as anthropomorphists claim), and being a father or son. Find each one in the text.
3
Reflect on why this is praise
Ask yourself: why is negating these things considered praise? How does this interpretation protect Allah's transcendence?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحتمل أيضاً أن يكون وجه التمدّح في قوله لا تدركه الأبصار أنها لا تدركه جسماً مصوّراً متحيّزاً، ولا حالا في شيء على ما يقوله النصارى، ولا مشبهاً لشيء على ما يقوله أهل التشبيه، ولا تدركه والداً ولا مولوداً على صفة من يلد أو يولد تعالى عن ذلك! ويكون القصد بذلك الردَّ على من وصفه بهذه الصفات وليس لأحد من المعتزلة التعلّق بهذه الآية، لأن الله تعالى إنما نفي عندهم من إدراك الأبصار ما أثبته لنفسه في قوله وهو يدرك الابصار . ولم يَعْنِ بذلك عند البصريين أنه يرى الأبصار، وهو تعالى عندهم لا يدرك الأبصار لأنها مما لا يصح أن يدرك ؛ ولا يرى أيضاً شيئاً بتة عند البغداديين. وإنما عني عندهم أنه يعلم الأبصار، فيجب عليهم أن يكون إنما نفى بقوله : لا تدركه الأبصار علمنا به تعالى .
It is also possible that the aspect of praise in His statement 'Sight cannot perceive Him' is that they do not perceive Him as a formed, spatial body, nor as an indwelling in something as the Christians say, nor as resembling anything as the people of tashbih say, and they do not perceive Him as a father or a son in the manner of one who begets or is begotten—exalted is He above that! And the intent thereby is to refute whoever describes Him with these attributes. And it is not for any of the Mu'tazila to cling to this verse, because Allah the Exalted only negated, according to them, from the perception of sights what He affirmed for Himself in His statement 'and He perceives sights.' And He did not mean by that, according to the Basrans, that He sees sights, and He the Exalted, according to them, does not perceive sights because they are something that cannot be perceived; nor does He see anything at all according to the Baghdādīs. Rather, according to them, He means that He knows sights. So it must be upon them that He only negated by His statement 'Sight cannot perceive Him' our knowledge of Him the Exalted.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Sight cannot perceive Him). Baqillani first suggests that the verse is praising Allah by negating that He can be perceived as a physical body, or as an indwelling substance (as Christians claim), or as resembling anything (as anthropomorphists claim), or as having a parent. The purpose is to refute those who describe Allah with such attributes. Then he argues that the Mu'tazila cannot use this verse to deny the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife because they interpret the phrase 'يدرك الأبصار' (He perceives sights) as meaning knowledge, not literal vision. He points out the inconsistency: according to the Basran Mu'tazila, Allah does not perceive sights because sights are not perceptible, and according to the Baghdādīs, Allah does not see anything at all. So for them, the verse only negates our knowledge of Allah, not the vision of Him.

Key Terms
لا تدركه الأبصار
تمدح
تشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse only negates seeing Allah in the afterlife.
Correction: Baqillani shows the verse negates physical form, indwelling, resemblance, and parenthood, emphasizing transcendence rather than just denying vision.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the primary purpose of the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار'?
A) To prove that Allah cannot be seen in the afterlife.
B) To praise Allah by negating physical and anthropomorphic attributes.
C) To affirm that Allah sees everything.
D) To deny that Allah has knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' is a praise of Allah's transcendence by negating any physical or anthropomorphic attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even for a moment, imagined Allah as being like something you know – a light, a throne, a shape? What does this verse demand you to tear down in your imagination?
?
You hear the words 'la tudrikuhu al-absar' – have you let them only be a fact in your mind, or have you let them become a guard that stops every whisper of false imagining? What will you do the next time your heart drifts toward picturing Allah?
?
The Christians fell into incarnation because they tried to bring the divine near. What subtle forms of 'bringing Allah down' might you be guilty of in your dua, your hope, or your fear? Will you repent and exalt Him as this verse commands?

Refuting the Mu'tazila's Argument
Baqillani refutes Mu'tazila reliance on this verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani argues that the Mu'tazila cannot use the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' to deny the vision of Allah, due to their own interpretation of 'يدرك الأبصار'.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's own theology disqualifies them from citing this verse against vision.

After presenting the positive meaning, Baqillani turns to a polemical point. The Mu'tazila claim the verse proves Allah cannot be seen. But Baqillani exposes an inconsistency in their own understanding of the parallel phrase 'يدرك الأبصار'. Let's see his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the logic of the argument.
Notice the reference to 'يدرك الأبصار'.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila position
According to Baqillani, the Mu'tazila interpret 'يدرك الأبصار' in the verse 'وهو يدرك الأبصار' as meaning knowledge, not literal sight. This is key.
3
See the inconsistency
Baqillani argues that if 'يدرك الأبصار' means knowledge, then 'لا تدركه الأبصار' would negate knowledge of Allah, which the Mu'tazila do not accept. Thus they cannot consistently use this verse to deny vision.
This refutes their proof.

Source Text

ويحتمل أيضاً أن يكون وجه التمدّح في قوله لا تدركه الأبصار أنها لا تدركه جسماً مصوّراً متحيّزاً، ولا حالا في شيء على ما يقوله النصارى، ولا مشبهاً لشيء على ما يقوله أهل التشبيه، ولا تدركه والداً ولا مولوداً على صفة من يلد أو يولد تعالى عن ذلك! ويكون القصد بذلك الردَّ على من وصفه بهذه الصفات وليس لأحد من المعتزلة التعلّق بهذه الآية، لأن الله تعالى إنما نفي عندهم من إدراك الأبصار ما أثبته لنفسه في قوله وهو يدرك الابصار . ولم يَعْنِ بذلك عند البصريين أنه يرى الأبصار، وهو تعالى عندهم لا يدرك الأبصار لأنها مما لا يصح أن يدرك ؛ ولا يرى أيضاً شيئاً بتة عند البغداديين. وإنما عني عندهم أنه يعلم الأبصار، فيجب عليهم أن يكون إنما نفى بقوله : لا تدركه الأبصار علمنا به تعالى .
It is also possible that the aspect of praise in His statement 'Sight cannot perceive Him' is that they do not perceive Him as a formed, spatial body, nor as an indwelling in something as the Christians say, nor as resembling anything as the people of tashbih say, and they do not perceive Him as a father or a son in the manner of one who begets or is begotten—exalted is He above that! Interpreting Moses' Repentance
Baqillani refutes the objection that Moses repented from sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Baqillani's refutation of the objection that Moses' repentance implies sin, by analyzing the multiple valid interpretations of 'tubtu ilayka' and the linguistic meaning of tawba.

Core Idea
Moses' 'repentance' was not from sin but from overwhelming awe.

Some opponents argue that Moses admitted sin by saying 'I turn to You in repentance' after asking to see Allah. In this passage, Imam Baqillani refutes this by offering three possible interpretations consistent with prophetic infallibility. He also clarifies the linguistic meaning of tawba, showing that returning from a thing does not imply disobedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and translation. Note that the opponents claim Moses' statement 'tubtu ilayka' proves he sinned by asking to see Allah.
The objection is stated explicitly at the start.
2
Examine the three interpretations
Baqillani offers three possible meanings for Moses' repentance. List them and notice how each one avoids implying sin.
The three are introduced by 'يُحتمل'.
3
Understand the definition of tawba
Baqillani defines tawba linguistically as 'returning from a thing' and gives an example from the Quran where God 'turns' to people as a favor. This shows tawba does not always involve sin.
The root meaning is key to the refutation.
4
See the conclusion
Baqillani concludes that since tawba simply means 'return', and returning does not imply sin, the objection based on the verse is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة فإن قالوا : فما معنى قوله : تبت إليك (۲) ؟ قيل لهم : لم يقل جلّ (۳) اسمه إنه (4) تاب من مسألته إياه الرؤية. : فيمكن (٥) أن يكون ذكر (۱) ذنوباً (۷) له قد (۸) قدّم التوبة منها فجدد التوبة عند ذكرها لهول ما رأى، كما يسارع الناس (۹) إلى التوبة ويجددونها (١٠) عنـد مشاهدة الأهوال والآيات (۱۱) ويحتمل أن يكون المعنى في قوله : (۱۲) تُبْتُ إِلَيْكَ» : من ترك استيذاني لك في هذه المسألة العظيمة، ومثلها ما لا يكون معه تكليف لمعرفتك والعلم بك . ويحتمل أن يكون أراد بقوله : «تُبت إليك» أي : «تبت إليك (١٣) أن أسألك (١٤) الرؤية لهول ما أصابني لا لأنها مستحيلة عليك، ولا لأني عاص في سؤالي كما يقول القائل : «تبت (۱٥) من كلام فلان ومعاملته، ومن ركوب البحر، ومن الحجّ ماشياً» (١٦) ، إذ ناله في ذلك تعب (۱۷) ونصب (۱۸) وشدّة ، وإن كان ذلك مباحاً حسناً جائزاً (۱۹) . والتوبة هي الرجوع عن الشيء، ذلك سُمّي الإقلاع عن الذنوب والعود إلى طاعة(٢٠) الله تعالى توبة منها. وم معنى المراد بقوله : ثم (۱) تاب (۲) عليهم ليتوبوا ) بهم إلى التفضل والامتنان (4) ليرجعوا عما كانوا عليه. فقوله: «تبت إلَيْكَ» - أي : «رجعت عن سؤالي إيَّاك «الرؤية». وهذا هو أصل التوبة، وليس الرجوع عن الشيء يقتضي كونه عصياناً. فبطل تعلقهم بالآية .
Question: If they say: What is the meaning of His saying: 'I turn to You in repentance'? It is said to them: He—exalted is His name—did not say that he repented from his asking Him for the vision. Rather, it is possible that he recalled sins whose repentance he had already offered, so he renewed repentance upon their mention due to the terror of what he saw, just as people hasten to repentance and renew it at the sight of terrors and signs. And it is possible that the meaning in His saying 'I turn to You in repentance' is: from abandoning my asking permission of You for this great matter, and like it there is no obligation with it due to my knowledge and cognizance of You. And it is possible that he intended by his saying 'I turn to You in repentance' meaning: I turn to You from asking You for the vision due to the terror that befell me, not because it is impossible for You, nor because I am disobedient in my asking, just as one says: 'I repent from speaking with so-and-so and dealing with him, and from riding the sea, and from performing Hajj on foot,' since he incurred in that fatigue, exhaustion, and hardship, even though that is permissible, good, and allowable. And repentance (tawba) is returning from a thing; therefore, desisting from sins and returning to obedience to Allah is called tawba from them. And the intended meaning of His saying: 'Then He turned to them in forgiveness so that they might turn' (Qur'an 9:118) is: He graciously favored them to return from what they were upon. So His saying 'I turn to You' means: I return from my asking You for the vision. And this is the root of repentance, and returning from a thing does not entail it being an act of disobedience. So their clinging to the verse is invalidated.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This is a refutation of an objection. The opponents quote Moses' statement 'I turn to You in repentance' (Qur'an 7:143) to claim that asking to see God is a sin. Baqillani responds by offering multiple interpretations. First, Moses may have recalled past sins and renewed repentance due to the awe of the experience. Second, it could mean regretting asking permission for such a great matter, implying reverence, not sin. Third, it could be like someone repenting from a lawful but burdensome action (e.g., riding the sea) due to hardship, without that action being sinful. Baqillani then defines tawba linguistically as 'returning from a thing,' noting that it does not necessarily imply sin, just as God's 'returning' to the three companions in Qur'an 9:118 is an act of grace, not forgiveness of sin. Thus, Moses' repentance is merely returning from his request, not confessing disobedience. This invalidates the objection.

Key Terms
توبة (tawba)
الرجوع (returning)
الاستيذان (asking permission)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that tawba always implies sin, so Moses' saying 'tubtu ilayka' proves he sinned.
Correction: Baqillani shows that tawba means 'return' and can be used for returning from a permissible action due to awe or hardship, not only from sin.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the primary meaning of Moses' statement 'tubtu ilayka'?
A) He repented from a sin of disobedience.
B) He repented from asking for the vision due to its overwhelming effect, not because it was sinful.
C) He repented from previous idolatry.
D) He repented from failing to believe in God.

Key Takeaway
Moses' repentance was a return from asking for a vision due to awe, not a confession of sin, thus preserving prophetic infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you felt so overwhelmed by Allah's majesty that you retreated from a good deed out of sheer awe. Did you consider that a sin? How does this lesson challenge that view? What will you do next time such awe strikes?
?
Moses' repentance was from asking something lawful but weighted with awe. Are there lawful things in your life that you avoid out of a sense of unworthiness, not sin? How can you discern whether your turning away is humble reverence or misplaced fear?
?
If 'returning from a thing' does not imply sin, what does that say about your own frequent 'repentances' from daily distractions? Are you truly returning from disobedience, or merely from discomfort? How can you realign your repentance with the Quranic meaning?

The Meaning of Divine Manifestation (Tajalli) to the Mountain
Baqillani explains 'tajalla' as God's removal of barriers, not physical emergence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'tajalla' in the Qur'an does not imply God physically appearing but rather the removal of barriers to perception, preserving divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Divine manifestation is about perception, not location.

Imam Baqillani addresses a critical question about God's manifestation to the mountain in Surah Al-A'raf. The verse might seem to suggest God changes or moves, but Baqillani shows that the linguistic and theological meaning is far more subtle — it is about removing impediments so that created things can perceive Him, all while affirming God's absolute transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice how Baqillani structures his response.
The passage includes a hypothetical question, an answer, and a linguistic clarification.
2
Identify the question and answer
The questioners ask about the meaning of 'tajalla' in the verse. Find Baqillani's initial answer: that God showed Himself to the mountain, causing it to crumble.
This answer is meant to teach Moses a lesson about seeing God in this world.
3
Grasp the key clarification
Baqillani then clarifies that 'tajalla' means removing the impediment (al-afa) that blocked perception, not God physically emerging from behind a veil.
He distinguishes the correct meaning from a false one that implies corporeality.
4
Examine the linguistic evidence
Baqillani supports his interpretation with Arabic usage: 'colors became manifest to me' and 'visuals became manifest to a blind person' when sight is restored.
This shows that 'tajalli' linguistically means the removal of obstacles to perception.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this interpretation is crucial for maintaining tanzih (divine transcendence). God does not move or change; rather, His actions affect creation's ability to perceive Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة فإن (ه) سألوا عن (٦) معنى قوله (٦) : فلما تجلى ربه للجبل جعله دكا (٧) وعن تأويل ذلك وما معنى التجلّي منه (۸) . قيل لهم (۹) : معنى ذلك أنه أرى نفسه للجبل (١٠) فتدكدك وصار قطعاً قطعاً (١١) لما أحبّ تعالى من إعلام موسى صلى الله عليه(۱۳)، أن أحداً لا يراه في الدنيا إلا لحقه ما لحق الجبل لحكمه تعالى بجعل (١٣) الدنيا دار تكليف وإيمان بالغيب. ومعنى قوله : «تَجَلَّى» أي : أنه رفع عن (١٤) الجبل الآفة المانعة له من رؤيته تعالى (۱٥) وأحياه وخلق فيه الإدراك له (١٦) فرآه . وقد يكون التجلّي ظهوراً أو (۱۷) خروجاً من وراء السواتر والحجب . صفات الأجسام، والله يتعالى عن ذلك . وقد يكون التجلي بمعنى رفع الآفات (۱۸) المانعة من الإدراك . ومن قولهم : تجلت لي الألوان وتجلت للضرير المبصـرات، إذا أبصـر
Question: If they ask about the meaning of His saying: 'When his Lord manifested (tajalla) to the mountain, He made it level dust' (Qur'an 7:143), and about its interpretation and what is the meaning of 'tajalli' from Him. It is said to them: The meaning is that He showed Himself to the mountain, and it crumbled and became pieces, because the Exalted wanted to inform Moses, peace be upon him, that no one sees Him in this world except that what befell the mountain would befall him, due to the Exalted's decree making this world a realm of obligation and faith in the unseen. And the meaning of His saying 'tajalla': He removed from the mountain the impediment preventing it from seeing Him, and gave it life and created in it perception of Him, and it saw Him. And sometimes 'tajalli' means appearing or emerging from behind screens and veils—attributes of bodies, and Allah is exalted above that. And sometimes 'tajalli' means the removal of impediments preventing perception. And from their sayings: 'colors became manifest (tajallat) to me' and 'visuals became manifest to the blind person' when he saw.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The questioners ask about the verse where God 'tajalla' to the mountain, causing it to crumble, and what 'tajalli' means. Baqillani responds by interpreting it as God showing Himself to the mountain, not in physical form, but by removing the impediment (al-afa) that prevented the mountain from perceiving Him, and then giving it life and perception so that it saw Him. This was a lesson for Moses: no one can see God in this world without being destroyed. Baqillani then clarifies that 'tajalli' is sometimes misunderstood as physical emergence from behind a veil, which is an attribute of bodies, but God is transcendent above that. The correct linguistic meaning is simply the removal of obstacles to perception, as in the Arabic saying 'colors became manifest to me' or 'things became manifest to a blind person' when he gains sight. Thus, the verse does not imply divine corporeality.

Key Terms
تجلّي (tajalli)
آفة (impediment)
إدراك (perception)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that 'tajalla' means God physically emerges from behind a veil, like a body.
Correction: Baqillani states that this is an attribute of bodies, and Allah is exalted above it. 'Tajalla' actually means the removal of impediments to perception.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the correct interpretation of 'tajalla' in the verse?
A) God physically appeared from behind a veil.
B) God removed the impediment preventing the mountain from perceiving Him.
C) God transformed into light and struck the mountain.
D) God's glory was reflected onto the mountain.

Key Takeaway
Tajalli in the Qur'an means the removal of barriers to perception, not God's physical movement or change, affirming His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever expected God to reveal Himself to you in a dramatic, sensory way, only to feel disappointed? Baqillani explains that God's manifestation is about removing barriers within us. What barrier in your heart—pride, distraction, or doubt—is preventing you from perceiving His presence right now? What concrete step will you take to remove it today?
?
Baqillani says this world is a realm of obligation and faith in the unseen. Are you living as if you see God, or are you waiting for a visible sign? If you truly believed that God's veils are lifted for those who seek Him sincerely, how would your prayer life or your struggle with sin change starting tonight?
?
The mountain crumbled when God manifested to it. If God were to remove the veil from your eyes and show you His glory this instant, do you think you would be able to bear it? How does this awareness of your own spiritual fragility motivate you to purify your heart—through repentance, charity, or humility—before you stand before Him on the Day when every veil will be removed?

The Two Meanings of Hijab and the Veiling Verse
Baqillani defines the two meanings of hijab and interprets the veiling verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of 'hijab' as defined by Imam Baqillani and the interpretation of the veiling verse (83:15) regarding the disbelievers being veiled from Allah.

Core Idea
Hijab means prevention, not just a physical barrier.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani clarifies the term 'hijab' by distinguishing its two meanings: a physical covering barrier and an affliction placed in the eye that prevents vision. He then applies the second meaning to interpret a key Quranic verse about the disbelievers being veiled from seeing Allah on the Day of Judgment. This lesson will help you grasp the precise theological usage of the term and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Let the author's own wording set the foundation.
Pay attention to the term 'الحجاب' and its root.
2
Identify the two meanings of hijab
The passage lists two meanings: a physical covering barrier, and an affliction in the eye. Notice how these are distinct yet share the root meaning of prevention.
3
Connect to the verse interpretation
See how Baqillani applies the second meaning to the Quranic verse (83:15). He explains that the disbelievers are prevented from seeing Allah as a punishment.
This links linguistic analysis to theological doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجاب، فقد يكون بمعنى الساتر المانع؛ وقد يكون آفة موضوعة في البصر تمنع من إدراك المرئيات. وأصل الحجب المنع، ومنه سمي حاجب الأمير حاجباً لمنعه منه ودفعه عن الوصول إليه. ومنه قوله تعالى: كلا إنهم عن ربهم يومئذ المحجوبون، يعني الكفار، أي إنهم ممنوعون بالآفات الموضوعة في أبصارهم من رؤيته تعالى إهانة لهم وتفريقاً بينهم وبين المؤمنين.
As for the veil (al-hijab), it may be in the sense of a covering barrier, and it may be an affliction placed in the eye that prevents the perception of visible things. The root of al-hajb (veiling) is prevention. And from this the doorkeeper (hajib) of the prince is called hajib because he prevents him and repels others from reaching him. And from this is His statement, the Most High: 'No! Indeed, they will be veiled from their Lord on that Day' (83:15), meaning the disbelievers; that is, they are prevented by afflictions placed in their eyes from seeing Him, the Most High, as a humiliation for them and a distinction between them and the believers.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'hijab' (veil/barrier) and its two meanings: first as a physical covering that prevents access, and second as an affliction or defect placed in the eye that prevents the perception of visible objects. The root 'hajb' means prevention, and from this root comes the word 'hajib' for a prince's doorkeeper, because he prevents people from reaching the prince. The passage then applies this second meaning to the Quranic verse 'No! Indeed, they will be veiled from their Lord on that Day' (83:15), explaining that the disbelievers are prevented by afflictions in their eyes from seeing Allah on the Day of Resurrection, as a punishment to humiliate them and to distinguish them from the believers who will see Him.

الحجب
المنع
المحجوبون
رؤية الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'hijab' only means a physical barrier or covering.
Correction: Baqillani shows that 'hijab' also refers to an affliction in the eye that prevents vision, and the root meaning is prevention, not just covering.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the root meaning of 'hajb'?
A) Covering
B) Prevention
C) Affliction
D) Separation

Key Takeaway
The essence of hijab is prevention, and the disbelievers' veil from Allah is an affliction placed in their eyes as punishment, distinguishing them from the believers who see Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The root of hijab is prevention. What is preventing you from deepening your connection with Allah—your habits, your doubts, or your desires? Will you let that prevention continue, or will you tear it down today?

Refuting the Linguistic Objection to Moses' Request
Baqillani refutes the linguistic objection to Moses' request using three arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the linguistic objection that Moses' request could mean knowledge or a sign is invalid based on three Arabic linguistic rules.

Core Idea
The word 'nazar' with 'ila' always means physical vision in Arabic.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani responds to an objection that Moses' request 'Show me, that I may look upon You' could be interpreted as asking for knowledge or a sign. He provides three linguistic proofs that such interpretations are impossible in Arabic. This lesson will walk through each argument and show why the objectors are refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its context
First, read the objection that Moses could have meant something other than seeing Allah. Understand the two alternative meanings proposed: asking for necessary knowledge or a sign of the Hour.
2
Identify the three refutation arguments
The refutation contains three distinct linguistic points. As you read the passage, note each one and how it builds on the previous.
3
Understand the linguistic rule about 'nazar' with 'ila'
Pay special attention to the third argument, which is the strongest: when 'nazar' is transitive with 'ila', it exclusively means vision of the eye. This is a fixed rule in Arabic.
4
Connect the refutation to divine transcendence
Consider why this linguistic argument matters for preserving the transcendence of Allah: if Moses could not ask to see Allah, then no one can, affirming that Allah is not visible in this world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: ما أنكرتم أن يكون موسى عليه السلام إنما أراد بقوله: رب أرني أنظر إليك، أي عرفني نفسك اضطراراً، أو أرني آية من آيات الساعة؟ قيل لهم: أنكرنا ذلك لأنه غير جائز في اللغة، لأن القائل لا يجوز أن يقول لمن يسمع كلامه ويعرفه ولا يشك فيه: أرني أنظر إليك، وهو يريد عرفني نفسك، أو أرني فعلاً من أفعالك. هذا غير مستعمل في اللسان. ولأن النظر إذا أطلق فليس معناه إلا رؤية العين. وإن أريد به العلم فبدليل. ولأن النظر الذي في الآية معدى بقوله: إليك، والنظر المعدى بإلى لا يجوز في كلام العرب أن يراد به إلا نظر العين. فبطل ما قالوا.
If they say: What prevents you from saying that Moses, peace be upon him, only meant by his statement: 'My Lord, show me, that I may look upon You' (7:143) i.e., 'make me know You necessarily' or 'show me a sign of the Hour'? It is said to them: We deny that because it is not permissible in language, for it is not permissible for a speaker to say to one who hears his speech, knows him, and does not doubt him: 'Show me that I may look upon you' while he intends 'make me know yourself' or 'show me an act of Your acts.' This is not used in the tongue. And because the word 'nazar' (looking/seeing) when used absolutely only means vision of the eye. If it is intended to mean knowledge, then it requires a proof. And because the 'nazar' in the verse is transitive by 'ila'; and 'nazar' made transitive by 'ila' cannot, in the speech of the Arabs, mean anything but vision of the eye. Thus what they said is invalidated.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objectors claim that Moses' request 'Show me, that I may look upon You' could alternatively mean 'make me know You necessarily' or 'show me a sign of the Hour.' The answer refutes this on three linguistic grounds: First, such usage is not permissible in Arabic because a speaker does not say 'Show me that I may look upon you' to someone he knows and trusts when he means something else. Second, the word 'nazar' (looking) when used without qualification only means vision of the eye; if knowledge were intended, a clarifying proof would be needed. Third, and most decisively, the verb 'nazar' in the verse is made transitive with the preposition 'ila', and in Arabic, 'nazar' with 'ila' exclusively means vision of the eye. Therefore, the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
النظر
رؤية العين
معدى بإلى
أرني
اضطرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'nazar' can mean intellectual reflection even when used with 'ila'.
Correction: Baqillani shows that in Arabic, 'nazar' with 'ila' is strictly visual; it cannot mean knowledge or reflection.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of 'nazar' when made transitive with 'ila'?
A) Knowledge
B) Consideration
C) Vision of the eye
D) Anticipation

Key Takeaway
In Arabic, when 'nazar' is used with 'ila', it exclusively means physical vision, so Moses' request must be understood as seeking visual seeing, not metaphorical knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself reinterpreting clear verses of the Qur'an about Allah's attributes because they seem too anthropomorphic or uncomfortable? Baqillani insists that the literal meaning must be upheld unless there is a clear proof otherwise. Will you commit to studying the Arabic language deeply enough to avoid such distortions in your own understanding?
?
Moses, a great prophet, asked to see Allah with his eyes out of intense longing. How strong is your desire to see Allah? Does it drive you to actions that may seem 'impossible' to others? What practical step can you take today to increase that longing in your heart?
?
When you speak about Allah, do you use careless language? For example, saying 'I see God in nature' might mix the meanings of 'nazar'. Baqillani's warning about precise language applies to you. Will you be more vigilant in your speech about Allah from now on, especially when teaching others?

Interpreting Negation Verses in Light of Affirmation Verses
Baqillani establishes the principle of interpreting negation verses via the affirmation verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hermeneutical principle that the affirmative verse about seeing Allah on the Day of Resurrection specifies the meaning of verses that seem to deny vision of Allah.

Core Idea
Affirmative verses about seeing Allah contextualize and limit the negation verses.

How should we understand Quranic verses that say 'Vision perceives Him not' (6:103) and 'You will not see Me' (7:143) when other verses clearly affirm that believers will see their Lord? Imam Baqillani provides a key principle: the affirmative verse must guide our interpretation of the negative ones. This lesson unpacks that principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for its structure. Then read the English translation, noting the two types of verses mentioned.
The passage is incomplete at the end; focus on the established principle.
2
Identify the key verses
Identify which verses are quoted: the negation verses (لا تدركه الأبصار, لن تراني) and the affirmation verse (وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة). Understand their contexts.
The affirmation verse is from Surah Al-Qiyamah, describing the believers on the Day of Resurrection.
3
Understand the principle
Baqillani asserts that the meaning of the affirmation verse is firmly established as literal vision of the eyes. Therefore, the negation verses must be interpreted in a way that does not contradict this, leading to specification (تخصيص) of their application.
This principle avoids absolute negation while upholding the possibility of vision in the afterlife.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this principle is 'necessary' (يجب) for Baqillani. How does it resolve apparent contradiction between the verses?
This reflects a foundational hermeneutic in Sunni theology: the definitive text (muhkam) interprets the ambiguous (mutashabih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب الاعتماد في تخصيص قوله عز وجل: لا تدركه الأبصار وقوله: لن تراني على قوله: وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة من حيث ثبت أنه لا يجوز أن يكون معناه إلا رؤية الأبصار. وكذلك يجب أن يعتمد في أنه لا يجوز أن يكون عني بقوله: لا تدركه الأبصار وقوله: لن تراني وقوله
And it is necessary to rely, in specifying His saying, the Mighty and Majestic: 'Vision perceives Him not' (6:103) and His saying: 'You will not see Me' (7:143), upon His saying: 'Faces on that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord' (75:22-23), from the fact that it is established that it cannot mean anything but vision of the eyes. And similarly it is necessary to rely that it is not permissible that He intended by His saying: 'Vision perceives Him not' and His saying: 'You will not see Me' and His saying...
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage establishes a key hermeneutical principle for interpreting Quranic verses about seeing Allah. The verses that negate vision (like 6:103 'لا تدركه الأبصار' and 7:143 'لن تراني') must be interpreted and specified in light of the verse that affirms vision for the believers on the Day of Resurrection (75:22-23 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة'). Since it is established that the affirmative verse can only mean literal vision of the eyes, the negative verses must be understood as referring to this world or to specific contexts, not as an absolute negation. The passage emphasizes that this principle is necessary and must be relied upon.

Correction: Baqillani argues that such verses must be specified by the affirmative vision verse, which establishes that vision is possible in the afterlife, so the negation is not absolute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
Baqillani insists it is 'necessary' to interpret certain verses in light of others. Are you willing to submit your own immediate understanding of a verse to the broader context of the Qur'an? What might that cost you in terms of personal opinion?

Refutation of the Doubt about the Israelites' Request to See Allah
Baqillani refutes the claim that Allah denied vision because it is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's denial of the Israelites' request does not prove that vision of Allah is impossible, but rather was due to their obstinacy and rebellion.

Core Idea
Denial does not mean impossibility; Allah denied out of wisdom, not incapacity.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani addresses a common objection: if seeing Allah is possible, why did Allah deny the Israelites when they asked to see Him? Baqillani explains that the denial was not because the request was impossible, but because the Israelites asked in a spirit of obstinacy and doubt. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows how it preserves both divine power and proper theological method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the parallel Baqillani draws with the request for a book.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find the sentence where Baqillani states that the denial was not due to impossibility but due to obstinate intent. This is the core of his refutation.
He uses the phrase 'not because of the impossibility of that in His power'.
3
Reflect on the Quranic parallels
Baqillani cites the demand from Surah al-Isra. Consider why these demands were rejected: were they for knowledge or for rebellion?
This reinforces the theme that the manner of asking matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم : لم ينكر الله تعالى مسألة أخلاف بني إسرائيل أن ينزّل عليهم كتاباً من السماء، ومسألة أسلافهم أن يروا الله جهرة، لاستحالة ذلك وإنما أنكره لأنهم سألوا ذلك على طريق العناد لموسى ومحمد صلى الله عليهما، والشكّ في نبوّتهما والتقدم بين أيديهما والامتناع من فعل ما أوجب عليهم من الإيمان بالله عزّ وجلّ حتى يفعل ما يؤثرونه ويقتاتونه . فأنكر الله ذلك من فعلهم وقولهم، كما أنكر سؤالهم إنزال كتاب من السماء، لا لاستحالة ذلك في قدرته. وكما أنكر قول من قال : لن نؤمن لك حتى تفجر لنا من الأرض ينبوعاً إلى قوله: ﴿ أو ترقى في السماء ولن نؤمن لرقيك﴾ . لأن هذا أجمع إنما كان على وجه الاستخفاف بالرسل والتمرد ، لا على طلب الزيادة في العلم .
It was said to them: Allah Almighty did not deny the request of the later Israelites that a book be sent down to them from heaven, nor the request of their predecessors that they see Allah openly, because of the impossibility of that; rather, He denied it because they asked that in a manner of obstinacy towards Moses and Muhammad, peace be upon them both, and doubt about their prophethood, and rushing ahead of them, and refraining from doing what was obligatory upon them of belief in Allah Almighty until He does what they desire and choose. So Allah denied that from their action and speech, just as He denied their request to send down a book from heaven, not because of the impossibility of that in His power. And just as He denied the saying of the one who said: 'We will not believe you until you cause a spring to gush forth from the earth for us' up to His saying: 'Or you ascend into heaven and we will not believe in your ascension.' Because all of this was only in a manner of belittling the messengers and rebellion, not a request for increase in knowledge.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a doubt raised by opponents who claim that Allah's denial of the Israelites' request to see Him proves that vision of Allah is impossible. Baqillani explains that the denial was not due to impossibility but because the Israelites asked out of obstinacy and doubt in the prophethood of Moses and Muhammad. They demanded to see Allah and to have a book sent down as a condition for belief, which was a sign of rebellion, not a genuine quest for knowledge. Baqillani compares this to other demands mentioned in the Qur'an (Surah al-Isra), all of which were made in a spirit of belittling the messengers. Thus, the denial does not indicate that vision is impossible; rather, it indicates that the request was improper. The key point is that the reason for denial is the obstinacy of the askers, not the inherent impossibility of the request.

Correction: Baqillani clarifies that the denial was due to the Israelites' obstinate intent, not because vision is impossible in itself.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Allah's denial of a request does not imply that the requested thing is impossible; the context and intention matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
When you pray, do you examine your heart for obstinacy? Are there requests you make not out of humble need but out of a demand that Allah prove Himself to you on your terms? How would your prayer change if you confessed that attitude and repented?
?
Consider the deepest reason you resist submitting fully to Allah. Is there a 'seeing is believing' demand you hold back? This lesson shows that such demands are rooted in rebellion, not sincerity. Will you abandon your terms and accept faith on His terms alone, starting now?

The Interpretive Priority of the Vision Verse
Baqillani establishes the interpretive priority of the vision verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretive method for verses about seeing Allah: the affirming verse must be taken literally and the negating verses interpreted in light of it.

Core Idea
The verse affirming vision is the key that unlocks the meaning of negating verses.

When dealing with seemingly conflicting verses about Allah, scholars of kalam prioritize certain verses over others based on established principles. Baqillani presents a foundational rule: the verse that speaks of faces looking at their Lord (75:22-23) must be interpreted literally as seeing with the eyes, and any verse that negates vision must be understood in the context of this literal affirmation. This principle safeguards the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter while respecting all scriptural texts.

Guided Path
1
Read Baqillani's statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Focus on the verb 'يجب' (it is necessary) to sense the imperative tone.
2
Identify the two groups of verses
The passage mentions three verses: two that negate vision (لا تدركه الأبصار, لن تراني) and one that affirms (وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة). Notice which one is prioritized.
The affirming verse is taken as literal.
3
Understand the reason for priority
Baqillani states that the affirming verse 'cannot be other than vision with the eyes'. This decisive certainty gives it interpretive authority over the others.
4
Reflect on the method
Ask yourself: How does this method differ from taking all verses at face value or from philosophical reinterpretation? The principle here is scriptural prioritization based on established meaning.
This is a key methodological tool in Ash'ari kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب الاعتماد في تخصيص قوله عزّ وجلّ: لا تدركه الأبصار وقوله : لن تراني على قوله: ﴿ وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة﴾ من حيث ثبت أنه لا يجوز أن يكون معناه إلا رؤية الأبصار وكذلك يجب أن يُعتمد في أنه لا يجوز أن يكون عني بقوله لا تدركه الأبصار وقوله : لن تراني وقوله : فأخذتهم الصاعقة الدلالة على إحالة رؤيته تعالى، لأن الشيء لا يدل على استحالته . لأنه قد يُنفى عنه الجائز أحياناً، وينفى المستحيل الممتنع في صفته .
And it is necessary to rely, in specifying His saying, Exalted is He: 'Vision does not perceive Him' and His saying: 'You will not see Me' upon His saying: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' from the consideration that it is established that its meaning cannot be other than vision with the eyes. And likewise it is necessary to rely on the fact that it is not permissible that He intended by His saying 'Vision does not perceive Him' and His saying 'You will not see Me' and His saying: 'So the thunderbolt seized them' [as] the indication of the impossibility of seeing Him, Exalted is He, because a thing does not indicate its impossibility. For sometimes the possible is negated from it, and the impossible that is precluded in its attribute is negated.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage presents two key points. First, Baqillani establishes a methodological principle for interpreting conflicting verses about seeing Allah: the verse that affirms vision (Surah al-Qiyama 75:22-23) must be taken as literal, and the verses that negate vision (e.g., Surah al-An'am 6:103, Surah al-A'raf 7:143) must be interpreted in light of it. The reason is that it is established that the affirming verse cannot mean anything other than seeing with the eyes. Second, the author addresses the objection that the Israelites were struck by lightning when they asked to see Allah (Surah an-Nisa 4:153), arguing that this does not prove vision is impossible. The thunderbolt is not a conclusive proof because negation of a possible thing can occur for some reason, and negation of an impossible thing also occurs. Therefore, the thunderbolt does not definitively indicate impossibility. The overall argument defends the Ash'ari doctrine that vision of Allah is possible and will occur for the believers in the Hereafter.

Key Terms
تخصيص
لا تدركه الأبصار
لن تراني
وجوه ناضرة
رؤية الأبصار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the correct interpretive method for the verse 'Vision does not perceive Him'?
A) It should be taken literally and all other verses must conform to it.
B) It should be interpreted in light of the verse that affirms vision.
C) It should be considered abrogated by the vision verse.
D) It should be interpreted metaphorically to avoid contradiction.

Key Takeaway
The verse affirming vision is the interpretive key that specifies the meaning of verses negating vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter verses that seem to contradict each other, do you submit to the Quran's own method of letting the clear interpret the unclear, or do you rely on your own reasoning? What does that reveal about your trust in Allah's speech?
?
The affirming verse fills the believers with hope of seeing their Lord in the Hereafter. Does that hope stir your heart to long for that moment, or have worldly distractions dulled that longing? What will you do today to renew that desire?
?
If you honestly examine your beliefs about Allah, do you allow any ambiguous or negating statements to limit what He has promised through clear verses? Are you willing to adjust your understanding to match the Quran's own interpretive priority?

Refuting the Thunderbolt Objection
Baqillani refutes the thunderbolt objection against seeing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the thunderbolt incident does not prove that seeing Allah is impossible.

Core Idea
Thunderbolts prove nothing about impossibility; they only show that something was withheld.

Some might point to the story of the Israelites who were struck by a thunderbolt after demanding to see Allah as evidence that vision is impossible. Baqillani addresses this objection with a logical argument: the thunderbolt itself does not indicate that vision is impossible because negation of something can happen for various reasons. It could be a punishment or a reminder, not a demonstration of impossibility. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the passage carefully. Notice that Baqillani mentions the thunderbolt verse (Surah an-Nisa 4:153) and then gives his counter-argument.
2
Identify the key logical principle
Baqillani states: 'a thing does not indicate its impossibility'. This is the core of the refutation. The thunderbolt is the 'thing' that supposedly indicates impossibility, but it cannot do so.
3
Understand the distinction
He explains that negation can apply to both possible and impossible things. The thunderbolt could be negating a possible event (seeing Allah) for some reason, not proving it is impossible.
For example, it could be a punishment for their insolence.
4
Apply to the doctrine
Reflect: if the thunderbolt does not prove impossibility, then the verse is not a counter-argument to the possibility of seeing Allah. The affirming verse remains decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب الاعتماد في تخصيص قوله عزّ وجلّ: لا تدركه الأبصار وقوله : لن تراني على قوله: ﴿ وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة﴾ من حيث ثبت أنه لا يجوز أن يكون معناه إلا رؤية الأبصار وكذلك يجب أن يُعتمد في أنه لا يجوز أن يكون عني بقوله لا تدركه الأبصار وقوله : لن تراني وقوله : فأخذتهم الصاعقة الدلالة على إحالة رؤيته تعالى، لأن الشيء لا يدل على استحالته . لأنه قد يُنفى عنه الجائز أحياناً، وينفى المستحيل الممتنع في صفته .
And it is necessary to rely, in specifying His saying, Exalted is He: 'Vision does not perceive Him' and His saying: 'You will not see Me' upon His saying: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' from the consideration that it is established that its meaning cannot be other than vision with the eyes. And likewise it is necessary to rely on the fact that it is not permissible that He intended by His saying 'Vision does not perceive Him' and His saying 'You will not see Me' and His saying: 'So the thunderbolt seized them' [as] the indication of the impossibility of seeing Him, Exalted is He, because a thing does not indicate its impossibility. For sometimes the possible is negated from it, and the impossible that is precluded in its attribute is negated.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage presents two key points. First, Baqillani establishes a methodological principle for interpreting conflicting verses about seeing Allah: the verse that affirms vision (Surah al-Qiyama 75:22-23) must be taken as literal, and the verses that negate vision (e.g., Surah al-An'am 6:103, Surah al-A'raf 7:143) must be interpreted in light of it. The reason is that it is established that the affirming verse cannot mean anything other than seeing with the eyes. Second, the author addresses the objection that the Israelites were struck by lightning when they asked to see Allah (Surah an-Nisa 4:153), arguing that this does not prove vision is impossible. The thunderbolt is not a conclusive proof because negation of a possible thing can occur for some reason, and negation of an impossible thing also occurs. Therefore, the thunderbolt does not definitively indicate impossibility. The overall argument defends the Ash'ari doctrine that vision of Allah is possible and will occur for the believers in the Hereafter.

Key Terms
الصاعقة
استحالة
الدلالة
جائز
مستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that the thunderbolt incident proves that it is impossible to see Allah.
Correction: The thunderbolt does not indicate impossibility; it only negates the occurrence for a specific group at a specific time, which is consistent with the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani reject the thunderbolt as proof that seeing Allah is impossible?
A) The thunderbolt was a natural event, not a divine sign.
B) The thunderbolt does not indicate its own impossibility.
C) The verse about the thunderbolt is abrogated.
D) The thunderbolt only shows that the Israelites were sinful.

Key Takeaway
The thunderbolt incident does not prove that seeing Allah is impossible; negation of something does not imply impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or an unanswered prayer that seems to deny what Allah has promised, do you let that event shake your conviction, or do you maintain that the event itself does not negate what Allah has affirmed? How often have you confused God's withholding something with its impossibility? Examine one belief you have abandoned because of a difficult circumstance and reconsider whether that circumstance actually negates the truth.
?
The Israelites demanded to see Allah improperly and were punished. Have you ever made a demand of Allah in a manner lacking adab (proper etiquette), and when He withheld, you concluded that He was incapable or unwilling? How will you correct your approach to requesting from Allah, especially regarding matters of the unseen?
?
Baqillani argues that negation can apply to both possible and impossible things. In your own life, have you ever assumed that because Allah did not give you something (e.g., a specific blessing, a clearer sign), it must be impossible? How will you now differentiate between a temporary withholding and a real impossibility, based on what Allah has affirmed in His book?

Linguistic Proof that 'Nazar' Means Seeing with Eyes
Baqillani linguistically proves 'nazar' in the vision verse means seeing with eyes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses Arabic grammar and usage to prove that 'nazar' in the vision verse refers to seeing with the eyes, not waiting or reflection.

Core Idea
When 'nazar' is paired with 'face' and 'ila', it always means visual perception.

Imam Baqillani tackles a common objection: couldn't 'nazar' in the verse mean waiting or thinking? He shows that Arabic usage itself settles the matter. By examining how the word behaves grammatically—especially with the preposition 'ila' and mention of the face—he demonstrates that the verse can only mean seeing with the eyes. This lesson walks through his careful linguistic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Learn the multiple meanings of 'nazar'
Read the opening where Baqillani lists the possible meanings: waiting, reflection, compassion, and visual perception. Understand that context determines which meaning is intended.
2
Identify the grammatical key condition
Notice that when 'nazar' is accompanied by mention of the face (wajh) and made transitive with 'ila' (to), it signifies seeing. This is the core of the proof.
The face is described as radiant, linking it to the eyes.
3
Examine the Qur'anic and poetic evidence
Look at the examples Baqillani provides: the verse about Abraham's food proves 'nazar bi 'ayn'; verses about waiting show the opposite construction without 'ila' or face.
The contrast solidifies the rule.
4
Conclude the intended meaning
Since the vision verse uses 'ila' and mentions the face (wajh), it must mean seeing Allah with the eyes on the Day of Judgment.
This refutes those who interpret it as waiting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما صح أن قوله : وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة يقتضي النظر إليه بالأبصار لأن النظر في كلام العرب يحتمل وجوها : منها نظر الانتظار، ومنها الفكر والاعتبار ومنها الرحمة والتعطف ومنها الإدراك بالأبصار ؛ وإذا قُرن النظر بذكر الوجه وعُدّي بحرف الجرّ ولم يُضَف الوجه إلى قبيلة وعشيرة الوجه الجارحة التي توصف بالنضارة التي تختص بالوجه الذي فيه العينان فمعناه رؤية الأبصار. ألا ترى إلى قوله عزّ وجلّ : فانظر إلى طعامك وشرابك لم يتسنه أي : انظره بعينك. ولما لم يرد بقوله تعالى : فناظرة يم يرجع المرسلون نظر العين، لم يُعده بـ (إلى) ولا قرنه بالوجه. وكذلك قوله: ما ينظرون إلا صيحة واحدة، لما أراد به الانتظار دون نظر الأبصار، لم ينطه بالوجه ولا عداه بـ «إلى». قال الشاعر: فَإِن يَكُ صَدْرُ هَذَا الْيَوْم وَلَّى فَإِنَّ غدا لناظره قريب فلم يقرن النظر بذكر الوجه ولا عداه بـ «إلى» لما أراد الانتظار.
And when it is correct that His saying: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' necessitates looking (nazar) at Him with the eyes, because 'nazar' in the speech of the Arabs has multiple aspects: among them the aspect of waiting (intidhar), among them reflection and consideration (tafakkur and i'tibar), among them mercy and compassion (rahmah and ta'attuf), and among them perception with the eyes (idrak bil-absar). And when 'nazar' is accompanied by mention of the face ('wajh') and is made transitive with the preposition 'ila', and the face is not attributed to a tribe or clan — the face is the limb that is described with radiance (nadaarah) which is specific to the face that contains the two eyes — then its meaning is vision with the eyes. Do you not see to His saying, Exalted is He: 'Look at your food and drink, it has not changed' i.e., look at it with your eye. And when He did not intend by His saying: 'Then they are looking at what the messengers will return' the sight of the eye, He did not make it transitive with 'ila' nor associate it with the face. And likewise His saying: 'They await only a single blast', when He intended waiting (intidhar) rather than sight of the eyes, He did not associate it with the face nor make it transitive with 'ila'. The poet said: 'If this day's time has passed, then tomorrow is near for its expected one' — he did not associate 'nazar' with mention of the face nor make it transitive with 'ila' because he intended waiting.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a detailed linguistic analysis to prove that the word 'nazar' in the verse 'Wujoohun yawma'idhin naadhiratun ila rabbihaa naathirah' means seeing with the eyes. Baqillani first lists the possible meanings of 'nazar' in Arabic: waiting, reflection, compassion, and visual perception. Then he identifies a grammatical key: when 'nazar' is used with mention of the face (wajh) and is made transitive with the preposition 'ila', it invariably means vision with the eyes. He provides evidence from the Qur'an: in the verse about Abraham looking at his food (Surah al-Baqarah 2:259), 'nazar' with 'ila' means 'look with your eyes'. Conversely, when the intended meaning is not visual, as in 'Then they are looking at what the messengers will return' (Surah an-Naml 27:35) and 'They await only a single blast' (Surah Ya-Sin 36:49), the word is not used with 'ila' nor associated with the face. He also cites a line of poetry where 'nazir' means 'expected one' without the face or 'ila'. Thus, the grammatical construction in the vision verse conclusively proves that it refers to seeing Allah with the eyes in the Hereafter.

Correction: Baqillani proves that when 'nazar' is used with 'face' and 'ila', it always means seeing with eyes, not waiting.

B) It is made transitive with 'ila' and associated with mention of the face.
C) It is used in poetry.
D) It is used with 'anna'.

Key Takeaway
The word 'nazar' in the vision verse must mean seeing with eyes because it is used with the face and the preposition 'ila', a construction that consistently indicates visual perception in Arabic.

Refuting the Claim that Reason Precludes Vision of Allah
Baqillani refutes the objection that reason deems seeing Allah impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani refutes the objection that reason makes seeing Allah impossible, and you will see that reason actually permits it.

Core Idea
Reason does not forbid seeing Allah; it permits it.

In this short but powerful passage, Imam Baqillani addresses a key objection raised by those who deny the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife. The objector claims that reason itself proves vision impossible. Baqillani responds by clarifying that reason, properly understood, does not forbid vision but rather permits it. This sets the stage for further arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector asks: 'What prevents you from accepting that the proof is that reason deems seeing Allah impossible?' Understand their claim.
The objector assumes reason is the ultimate arbiter.
2
Analyze Baqillani's response
Baqillani asserts that reason permits seeing Allah and invalidates the objector's claim. Note the shift: he does not say reason proves vision, only that it does not preclude it.
3
Recognize the logical structure
This is an objection-and-answer format. Baqillani first states the objection, then gives his counter-claim. He also references previous and future arguments.
He builds a cumulative case across his work.
4
Reflect on the burden of proof
Baqillani places the burden on the objector: they claim impossibility, but reason does not support that. It is for them to prove impossibility.
In kalam, the default is permissibility unless evidence blocks it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا ما أنكرتم أن تكون الحجة في ذلك إحالة العقول لرؤيته؟ قيل لهم: العقول عندنا تجيز رؤيته وتبطل دعواكم، وقد بينا ذلك فيما سلف، وسنفسد ما تتعلقون به فيما بعد إن شاء الله.
Then if they say: What prevents you from [accepting] that the proof in that is the impossibility (iḥāla) of the intellects for seeing Him? Say to them: The intellects according to us permit (tujīz) seeing Him and invalidate your claim, and we have clarified that in what preceded, and we will invalidate what you cling to afterwards, if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that the proof that Allah cannot be seen is that reason itself deems it impossible. Baqillani responds that, on the contrary, reason does not forbid seeing Allah; rather, it permits it. He asserts that they have already demonstrated this in previous arguments and will continue to refute the objector's evidence later. The answer is brief: reason permits vision, and the burden of proof is on the objector.

Refuting a Poetic Objection on 'Faces'
Baqillani dismisses an obscure poem and clarifies the Quranic usage of 'faces'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes a poetic objection by distinguishing figurative from literal uses of 'faces' in Arabic and Quranic context.

Core Idea
Poetry cannot override clear Quranic usage: 'faces' can mean leaders in poetry but physical faces in the Quran.

Baqillani addresses a second poetic objection that might seem to support the view that 'faces' can 'look' without physical sight. He quickly dismisses the poem as unknown to scholars, but even if authentic, he explains that in such poetic contexts 'faces' refers to the nobles, not the physical limb. Then he turns to the Quranic verse that describes faces as 'radiant', a quality that only applies to a physical face, thus affirming that the verse must be taken literally.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Baqillani's dismissal
Notice the objector quotes a poem; Baqillani says it's not recognized by scholars. Consider why this dismissal is important.
This is a common tactic: challenging the authenticity of the objection's source.
2
Understand the distinction between uses of 'faces'
Baqillani explains that when 'faces' is mentioned with a town or tribe, it means leaders. This is a linguistic point.
This shows the importance of context in interpreting words.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Read the verse about 'radiant faces'. Baqillani argues that radiance is a property of a physical face, so the verse must refer to physical faces seeing Allah.
This is key: the attribute requires a physical subject.
4
Reflect on the refutation's strength
Consider how Baqillani uses both dismissal and positive linguistic/quranic argument to defend the Sunni position on seeing Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: فما معنى قول الآخر: وُجُوهٌ بِهَا لَيْلَ الْحِجَازِ عَلَى النَّوَى إِلَى مَلِكٍ رُكن الْمَغَارِبِ نَاظِرَهُ؟ قيل لهم: هذا شعر لا يعرفه أحد من أهل العلم، فلا حجة فيه، وإن صح لم يفسد ما قلناه، لأننا قد قلنا إن الوجوه المقرونة بذكر البلدة والعشيرة إنما يراد به سادة الناس. والله تعالى وصف الوجوه التي هي الجوارح بأنها تنظر إليه، لأنه تعالى وصفها بما لا يجوز أن يوصف به إلا الجارحة حيث قال: {وجوه يومئذ ناضرة} والنضارة لا تكون إلا في الجارحة التي هي الوجه.
Then if it is said: What is the meaning of the other's statement: 'Faces, with the night of Hijaz, in spite of distance, toward a king, the pillar of the West, looking at him'? Say to them: This is poetry that none of the people of knowledge recognize, so there is no proof in it. And if it were authentic, it would not invalidate what we have said, because we have said that faces when coupled with mention of a town or tribe are intended to mean the nobles of the people. And Allah Almighty described the faces that are limbs as looking at Him, because He described them with what cannot be described except as a limb, when He said: 'Faces that Day are radiant (nāḍira)' (75:22). And radiance (al-nadāra) only occurs in the limb which is the face.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage continues the response to a second poetic objection. The objector cites a line about 'faces looking at a king' to argue that faces can look at something without that thing being seen? Actually the line is obscure. Baqillani dismisses it as unknown to scholars, so it carries no weight. Even if authentic, it does not harm their position because when 'faces' are mentioned with a place or tribe, it refers to the leaders (sādāt al-nās), not the physical face. Then Baqillani contrasts this with the Quranic verse 'There will be faces that Day radiant (nāḍira)' (75:22), which describes physical faces (jāriḥa). The word 'nadāra' (radiance) can only apply to a physical face, so the verse must be taken literally – the faces are physical. This supports the Sunni position that believers will see Allah with their physical sight.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A poetic line about faces looking at a king proves that 'faces' can refer to something that sees without being a physical face.
Correction: Baqillani shows the line is unknown and even if authentic, 'faces' in such contexts means 'leaders', not the physical face. The Quranic usage of 'faces' with 'radiance' requires a physical face, so the vision is literal.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes a poetic objection by arguing that the poem is unreliable and, even if genuine, 'faces' in non-Quranic contexts can mean leaders, whereas the Quranic description of faces as 'radiant' demands a literal physical face.

Objection: If Allah is Seen, He Must Be a Body
Baqillani presents an objection listing ten consequences if Allah were seen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ten consequences the objector claims would follow if Allah were seen with physical eyes, and why these are rejected by Ash'ari theology.

Core Idea
The objection conflates the vision of Allah with the vision of physical objects.

This lesson presents a classic objection against the possibility of seeing Allah. The objector argues that if Allah were seen, He would have to possess attributes of created visible things. Baqillani lists ten such consequences. By studying this objection, we learn how Ash'ari theologians frame and respond to such challenges.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the full list of consequences.
Notice that the passage ends mid-sentence; the response is on the next page.
2
Identify each of the ten consequences
Go through the list and write down each consequence: being a body, substance, accident, limited, etc. Understand what each term means in kalam.
These are attributes of created physical things.
3
Understand the underlying assumption
The objector assumes that any act of vision entails the object having certain physical attributes. Ask yourself: is that necessarily true?
This assumption is what Baqillani will refute.
4
Consider Baqillani's purpose
Baqillani presents this objection to then answer it. Reflect on why he would detail the objection so thoroughly before refuting it.
This shows his method of thorough engagement with opponents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة. فإن قالوا ما أنكرتم من أنه لو رئي بالأبصار لوجب أن يكون جسماً أو جوهراً أو عرضاً أو محدوداً أو حالاً في محدود أو مقابلاً للمكان أو مبايناً للمكان أو متصلاً به الشعاع أو متصلاً بمكانه أو متخيلاً متمثلاً متصوراً بالقلوب عند غيبته، وأن يكون من جنس المرئيات لأننا لم نعقل
A question: Then if they say: What prevents you from [accepting] that if He were seen by the eyes, it would necessitate that He be a body (jism) or a substance (jawhar) or an accident (ʿaraḍ) or limited (maḥdūd) or in a location (ḥāl fī maḥdūd) or opposite to space (muqābil li-l-makān) or separated from space (mubāyin li-l-makān) or connected to it by a ray (muttasil bihi al-shiʿāʿ) or connected to its place (muttasil bi-makānihī) or imagined, represented, and pictured in hearts during His absence, and that He be of the same kind as visible things, because we have not understood [a visible thing except that].
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage introduces a new objection (mas'ala) against the possibility of seeing Allah. The objector lists ten consequences that would follow if Allah were seen with the physical eyes. These include: being a body (jism), substance (jawhar), accident (ʿaraḍ), limited (maḥdūd), occupying a location, being opposite or separate from space, connected by a ray (a reference to the theory of vision), connected to a place, being imagined in hearts, and being of the same genus as other visible things. The argument is that since Allah transcends all these categories, He cannot be seen. The objector concludes that we have never known a visible thing except with these properties. The answer to this objection is expected to follow, but it is cut off at this point. Baqillani will likely respond that the attributes of created visible things do not apply to Allah's vision, which is a unique act.

Key Terms

جوهر

محدود
مقابل
مباين
شعاع
متخيل
حال
متمثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the vision of Allah would necessarily involve physical attributes like being a body or occupying space.
Correction: Ash'ari theology distinguishes the vision of Allah from physical sight; it is a direct perception without the conditions of matter, space, or direction.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT among the consequences listed by the objector if Allah were seen?
A) Being a body.
B) Being limited.
C) Being omniscient.
D) Being a substance.

Key Takeaway
The objection forces us to clarify that Allah's vision is unlike any created vision and does not imply corporeality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider your own desire to 'see' Allah: are you hoping for a vision that fits your human expectations, or are you willing to accept that the vision of Allah is entirely unlike any experience you can imagine? Make a firm intention to submit to whatever Allah reveals about Himself, even if it contradicts your assumptions.
