The Parity Argument for Divine Vision
Baqillani responds to the objection that if God is seen, He must be a body, by using a parity argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani uses a parity argument to refute the objection that if God were seen, He would necessarily be a body.

Core Idea
If your logic forces God into creation, you have abandoned tawhid.

The objectors claim that seeing God would entail numerous creaturely attributes. Baqillani turns the tables: the same logic would force them to say God is a body when known by the heart, or even when considered as a thing. This parity argument exposes their inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objectors' list of supposed necessary consequences of seeing God. Note each attribute they claim must follow.
The list includes being a body, substance, accident, limited, in a place, etc.
2
Understand the parity argument
Identify how Baqillani applies the same logic to three other categories: heart-knowledge, being a thing, and being living-knowing-powerful. The argument is: if seeing forces X, then knowing by heart also forces X.
This shows the objectors' logic is flawed because it would lead to absurdities they reject.
3
See the dilemma
Baqillani presents a choice: either the objectors accept these consequences (which would force them to say God is a body in all three cases), in which case they abandon tawhid; or they deny the consequences, which invalidates their original objection.
4
Analyze the second objection
The objectors also claim that if seen, God would be imagined. Baqillani distinguishes between two possible meanings and refutes the incorrect one while affirming the correct one.
The distinction hinges on whether they mean imagining a form or knowing something incomparable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة
فإن قالوا ما أنكرتم من أنه لو رئي بالأبصار لوجب أن يكون جسماً أو جوهراً أو عرضاً أو محدوداً أو حالاً في محدود أو مقابلا للمكان أو مباين للمكان أو متصلاً به الشعاع أو متصلاً بمكانه أو متخيّلاً متمثلاً متصوّراً بالقلوب عند غيبته وأن يكون من جنس المرئيات لأننا لم نعقل مرئيّاً بالبصر إلا كذلك؟ .
يقال لهم لو وجب هذا لوجب، إذا كان معلوماً بالقلوب وموجوداً، أن يكون جسماً أو جوهراً أو عرضاً وفي العالم أو غيره من الأماكن أو ما يُقدَّر تقدير الأماكن، لأننا لم نعقل معلوماً إلا كذلك ؛ وإذا كان شيئاً أن لا يخلو من أن يكون جسماً أو جوهراً أو عرضاً، لأننا لم نعقل شيئاً إلا كذلك ؛ وإذا كان عالماً قادراً سميعاً بصيراً أن يكون جسماً مجتمعاً ذا حيّز في الوجود، لأننا لم نعقل حيّاً عالماً قادراً رائياً إلا كذلك. فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا التوحيد ؛ وإن أبوه أبطلوا ما سألوا عنه . وأما قولهم: إنه لو رُئِي بالأبصار لوجب أن يكون متمثلاً متخيّلاً فإنهم إن أرادوا به أنه إذا تعدم رؤيته نتخيل له شكلاً أو جسماً ونعتقد أنا رأينا ذا هيئة وشكل، فإن ذلك باطل لقيام الدليل على أن القديم سبحانه ليس من جنس المرئيات وإن عنوا به أنا نعلم بعد رؤيته أننا رأينا شيئاً ليس كمثله شيء، فإن ذلك صحيح . وتسمية هذا العلم تخيُّلاً وتمثلاً باطل بالإجماع .
Question: They said: 'What prevents you from acknowledging that if He were seen by sight, He would necessarily be a body, or a substance, or an accident, or limited, or in a limited location, or opposite to a place, or separate from a place, or connected to a ray, or connected to His place, or imagined, represented, pictured in hearts during His absence, and that He would be of the same kind as visible things because we only know visible things by sight to be like that?' It is said to them: If this were necessary, then it would also be necessary, if He is known by hearts and exists, that He be a body, or a substance, or an accident, or in the world or elsewhere, or in what is estimated as places, because we do not know something known except that way. And if He is a thing, He must not escape being a body, substance, or accident, because we do not know a thing except that way. And if He is knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing, He must be a body, composed, possessing spatial dimension in existence, because we do not know a living, knowing, powerful, seeing being except that way. If they pass over that, they abandon tawhid; if they reject it, they invalidate what they asked about. As for their saying: 'If He were seen by sight, He would necessarily be represented and imagined,' if they mean that after absence of vision we imagine for Him a form or body and believe that we saw something with shape and form, that is false because the proof stands that the Eternal (glorified) is not of the same kind as visible things. If they mean that after seeing Him we know that we saw something unlike anything else, that is correct. Calling this knowledge 'imagination' and 'representation' is false by consensus.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection that would force God to be a body if He were seen. The objectors list numerous consequences: being a body, substance, accident, limited, in a place, etc. The author responds with a parity argument: by the same logic, if God is known by the heart, He would also have to be a body, etc., because we only know things that way. Similarly, if He is a thing, He must be a body, and if He is living and knowing, He must be a body. The objectors cannot accept these consequences, so they must reject their own objection. The author then addresses a second point: the objectors say that if seen, God would be imagined. The author distinguishes: if they mean we would imagine a form after seeing, that is false because God is unlike creatures; if they mean we would know we saw something incomparable, that is true but calling it imagination is wrong.

الأبصار

جوهر

متخيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The parity argument is a mere tu quoque fallacy that does not refute the objection.
Correction: The parity argument shows that the objectors' logic would force them to accept absurdities they reject, thus exposing inconsistency in their position.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that if God were seen, He would have to be a body?
A) He denies that God can be seen.
B) He argues that the same logic would force the objectors to say God is a body when known by the heart.
C) He claims the objection is based on a false premise.
D) He says the objectors are ignorant of logic.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that the objectors' own reasoning would force them to say God is a body even without vision, thus their objection is inconsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself applying a double standard to Allah—accepting something for creation but denying it for Him, even though your own reasoning would force you to accept both? Where in your heart do you need to root out such inconsistency to preserve your tawhid?
?
When you say 'Allahu Akbar' or make du'a, do you ever catch yourself picturing something in your mind, even vaguely? What concrete step will you take today to train your heart to affirm His absolute incomparability without falling into any form of representation?
?
The objectors said, 'We have only known visible things to be like that.' Where in your faith do you rely on your limited experience to judge what Allah can or cannot do? Is there a verse of the Quran or a hadith that you find difficult because it doesn't fit your expectations? Will you submit your intellect to revelation, even when it stretches your imagination?

Why Is God Not Seen Now? Answering the Objection of Obstacles
Baqillani presents an objection and begins his answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God should be seen now if vision of Him is possible, and the beginning of Baqillani's response that the listed obstacles are not relevant.

Core Idea
Absence of vision does not prove impossibility of seeing God.

In this passage, Baqillani presents a common objection: if it is possible to see God, why don't we see Him now? The objectors argue that because God cannot have obstacles like subtlety, veil, or distance, vision should be immediate. Baqillani begins his response by rejecting that these are genuine obstacles. This lesson unpacks the objection and the initial counter.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the objectors list four obstacles that they claim are impossible for God.
The obstacles are: الرقة, اللطافة, الحجاب, البعد.
2
Identify the logical structure
The objection is a conditional: if vision were possible, then we would see now (because no obstacles). Identify the premise and conclusion.
3
Examine Baqillani's response
Notice that Baqillani begins by denying that the mentioned obstacles are relevant to vision. Think about what this implies for the objection.
The response is cut off, but the direction is clear.
4
Reflect on the key insight
Consider why the absence of vision does not necessarily mean impossibility. How might the response continue?

The objectors argue that if it were possible for God to be seen, we would see Him now because there are no obstacles like subtlety, refinement, veil, or distance—all impossible for God. The author begins answering by saying that none of these are true obstacles for vision. The answer is cut off, but the intent is to show that the absence of vision does not prove impossibility; the obstacles listed are not relevant to God.

Interpreting 'Ahsanu al-Khaliqin': Best of Determiners
Baqillani explains that khaliqin means muqaddirin and defends metaphorical naming

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani defends the oneness of Allah by interpreting the phrase 'ahsanu al-khaliqin' as 'best of determiners' and explaining that other 'creators' are only metaphorically so.

Core Idea
Allah is the sole creator; others are called creators only metaphorically.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani addresses an objection based on the Quranic phrase 'ahsanu al-khaliqin' (best of creators). Some argue this implies multiple creators, but Baqillani shows that the word 'khaliq' can mean 'determiner' (muqaddir) and that the verse uses metaphorical language. By examining Arabic poetry and linguistic conventions, he upholds tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that Baqillani uses poetic evidence and then two distinct answers.
2
Identify the two answers
The passage presents two responses to the objection. Find the first answer (tafsir of khaliqin as muqaddirin) and the second answer (metaphorical naming). Note how each is supported.
The second answer relies on Arabic idioms.
3
Reflect on the defense of tawhid
Ask yourself: How does each answer preserve the uniqueness of Allah's creation? Consider why the objection is problematic for tawhid.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشاعر زهير ولأنتَ تَفْري مَا خَلَقْتَ وَبَعْضُ الْقَوْم يَخْلُقُ ثُمَّ لَا يَفْرِي يعني تمضي ما قدرت ومنهم من يقدر ثم لا يمضي . وقال آخر : وَلَا يُنيطُ بِأَيْدِي الْخَالِقِينَ وَلَا أَيْدِي الْخَوَالِقِ إِلَّا جَيْدَ الأَدَمِ يريد أيدي المقدرين للأدم بأيديهم وقلوبهم . وإن استدلوا بقوله تعالى : فتبارك الله أحسن الخالقين ، فالجواب عنه أنه تعالى عنى وهو أعلم أحسن المقدرين تقديراً وأحسن المصوّرين تصويراً. يقول إن تصويره ألطف وأحسن من تصويرهم وإن تقديره التي هي إرادته وقصده أصوب من تقديرهم وارتيائهم . ويحتمل أن يكون الله تعالى لما ذكر نفسه مع غيره الذي ليس بخالق، سماهم باسمه مجازاً واتساعاً. كما قالوا «عدل العمرين» يعنون أبا بكر وعمر . وكما قالوا «الأسودان» يعنون الماء والتمر. وكما قال الشاعر : أَخَذْنَا بِآفَاقِ السَّمَاءِ عَلَيْكُمُ لَنَا قَمَرَاهَا وَالنُّجُومُ الطَّوَالِعُ يعني الشمس والقمر فكذلك قوله الخالقين، والخالق منهم واحد .
Zuhair the poet said: 'But you accomplish what you create, while some people create and then do not accomplish.' Meaning: you carry out what you determine, and among them is one who determines and then does not carry out. And another said: 'And nothing is attached to the hands of the creators nor the hands of the formers except the proper measure of skin.' Meaning: the hands of those who determine the measure (al-muqaddirin) of skin with their hands and hearts. And if they argue with His statement, the Exalted: 'So blessed be God, the best of creators' (Quran 23:14), then the answer about it is that He, the Exalted—and He knows best—meant 'the best of those who determine (al-muqaddirin) in determination and the best of those who form (al-musawwirin) in formation.' He says: His formation is more subtle and better than their formation, and His determination, which is His will and purpose, is more correct than their determination and deliberation. And it is possible that God, the Exalted, when He mentioned Himself along with others who are not creators, called them by His name metaphorically (majazan) and expansively (ittisa'an). Just as they said 'the two just ones' (adl al-umariin) meaning Abu Bakr and Umar; and just as they said 'the two black ones' (al-aswadan) meaning water and dates; and as the poet said: 'We took from the horizons of the sky over you, for us its two moons and the rising stars' meaning the sun and the moon. So likewise His saying 'the creators' (al-khalqin), and the Creator among them is one.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage presents Baqillani's response to an objection that the Quranic phrase 'ahsanu al-khaliqin' (best of creators) implies the existence of multiple creators. The passage begins by citing poetic evidence to show that the word 'khaliq' can mean 'muqaddir' (determiner). Two lines of poetry are quoted: one from Zuhair illustrating the distinction between creating and accomplishing, and another using 'khaliqin' to refer to those who determine the measure of skin. Baqillani then states the objection: opponents cite the verse 'fata baraka Allahu ahsanu al-khaliqin' (Quran 23:14). He gives two answers. First, God meant 'best of those who determine' (ahsana al-muqaddirin), as His determination is perfect and His formation is more subtle than any human formation. Second, it is possible that God mentioned Himself alongside non-creators and called them by His name metaphorically, as in Arabic idioms like 'the two just ones' for Abu Bakr and Umar, and 'the two black ones' for water and dates, and using 'two moons' for the sun and moon. Thus, the word 'khaliqin' includes only one true Creator; the others are called creators only metaphorically.

Key Terms
خالق
مقدر
مجاز
اتساع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'ahsanu al-khaliqin' implies that there are multiple creators and Allah is the best among them.
Correction: Baqillani shows that 'khaliqin' means 'determiners' and that the verse is either praising Allah's determination or using metaphorical language; it does not grant others the attribute of creation.

What is the primary meaning of 'khaliqin' in the verse 'ahsanu al-khaliqin' according to Baqillani's first answer?

B) Those who determine (muqaddirin)
C) Idols
D) Angels

Key Takeaway
Allah alone is the Creator; any other use of the term is metaphorical or refers to determination.

The poets used 'khaliq' to refer to human determination, but the Quran reserves true creation for Allah alone. The Alif of Exaggeration Does Not Necessarily Imply Shared Attributes
Baqillani refutes the claim that the alif of superlative requires shared attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the alif of exaggeration (ألف مبالغة) in Arabic can be used to single out someone as uniquely possessing an attribute, not only to indicate shared participation and gradation.

Core Idea
The alif of exaggeration can deny partners, not just compare equals.

In defending divine unity, Baqillani addresses a linguistic objection: the word 'ahsan' in the Quran might seem to imply that Allah shares the attribute of goodness with others, with comparison. But he shows that the same grammatical form can be used to refute any claim of sharing, making it a tool for tawhid. This lesson unpacks his argument and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opponent's claim: the alif of exaggeration only occurs when multiple entities share an attribute and you compare them. This would imply partners for Allah.
2
Baqillani's concession and qualification
Note that Baqillani admits the basic rule, but immediately adds an exception: it can also indicate exclusivity and refute false claims.
This is a classical scholarly move: concede the general principle but show a counter-purpose.
3
Analyze the first example: Surah an-Naml 27:59
Look at the verse 'Qul Allahu khayrun amma yushrikun'. Baqillani says the word 'khayrun' is used to deny any goodness in what they associate. It does not compare two good things.
The context is challenging idolatry.
4
Examine the poetic evidence
Read the lines by Hassan ibn Thabit and al-Farazdaq. In each, the superlative form is used to assert exclusive superiority, not to measure degrees among equals.
These show the form works for exclusivity in human speech too.
5
Reflect on the second Quranic example: Surah ar-Rum 30:27
The verse 'wa huwa ahwanu 'alayh' is interpreted as meaning 'in your belief', so it does not imply that another being finds anything easier. It refutes a false notion.
This ties the argument back to tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : الألف الذي في قوله «أحْسَنُ» ألف مبالغة لا يدخل مثل هذا الكلام إلا للاشتراك وإيقاع التفاضل في الوصف. قيل لهم : الأصل في هذه الألف كما زعمتم . إلا أنها قد تجيء للإفراد بالوصف وتكذيب دعوى من ادعى مشاركة ما ليس له الوصف نحو قوله تعالى (قُلِ اللهُ خَيْرٌ أَمَّا يُشْرِكُونَ) على وجه التكذيب لدعواهم الخير فيما يشركون به ؛ وقول حسان بن ثابت : تَهْجُوهُ وَلَسْتَ لَهُ بِنِدَّ فَشَرُّكُمَا لِخَيْرِكُمَا الْفِدَاءُ بين يعني إن كان في هجاء النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ما ادعاه وكذلك قول الفرزدق إنَّ الَّذِي رَفَعَ السَّمَاءَ بَنَى لَنَا بَيْناً دَعَالِمُهُ أَعَزُّ وَأَطْوَلُ يريد إن كان بيت جرير عزيزاً طويلاً على ما يدعيه. وكذلك قوله تعالى (وَهُوَ أَهْوَنُ عَلَيْهِ) يعني عندكم وفي اعتقادكم وظنونكم أن ابتداء الشيء على كل فاعل أهون عليه من إعادته فكذلك
If they say: The alif in His saying 'ahsan' is an alif of exaggeration (alif mubalagha); such expression only occurs for sharing and establishing gradation in the attribute. It is said to them: The basic rule of this alif is as you claimed. But it may come for singling out (al-ifrad) with the attribute and for denying the claim of one who claims sharing with what is not deserving of the attribute, as in His saying, the Exalted: 'Say: Is God better or what they associate?' (Quran 27:59) in the mode of denying their claim of goodness in what they associate; and the saying of Hassan ibn Thabit: 'You satirize him while you are not his equal, so the worse of you is the ransom for the better of you' meaning if his satire of the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, is as he claimed; and likewise the saying of al-Farazdaq: 'Indeed He who raised the sky built for us a house whose pillars are more mighty and longer' meaning if the house of Jarir is mighty and long as he claims. And likewise His saying, the Exalted: 'And it is easier for Him' (Quran 30:27) meaning [it is easy] in your view and in your belief and opinion that the beginning of a thing is easier for every agent than its repetition. So likewise.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and Baqillani's response. The opponents argue that the alif (الألف) in the word 'ahsan' (أَحْسَنُ) is the alif of exaggeration (ألف مبالغة) which, according to them, only appears in constructions requiring shared participation in the attribute and establishing gradation—thus implying multiple creators. Baqillani's answer: He concedes that this is the basic rule, but he points out that this alif can also be used for making a person unique in possessing an attribute (الإفراد بالوصف) and for refuting someone's claim of sharing an attribute that does not belong to them. He provides three examples: (1) The Quranic verse 'Qul Allahu khayrun amma yushrikun' (Say: Is God better or what they associate?) where the word 'khayrun' (better) is used to deny that what they associate has any goodness—it is not a comparison between equals. (2) Hassan ibn Thabit's line: '...the worse of you is the ransom for the better of you' meaning that the Prophet is better, not that both are good and one is better. (3) Al-Farazdaq's line about a house being 'more mighty and longer' meaning it is exclusively mighty and long in his claim, not that there are other houses equally mighty and long. (4) The Quranic verse 'wa huwa ahwanu 'alayh' (and it is easier for Him) is interpreted as meaning 'in your belief' that starting something is easier for every doer than repeating it—the verse does not imply that there is another being for whom it is easier. Thus, the alif of mubalagha can indicate exclusivity, not necessarily shared gradation.

Key Terms
ألف مبالغة
إفراد بالوصف
تكذيب الدعوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The superlative form in Arabic always implies a comparison with others who share the attribute.
Correction: Baqillani shows that the alif of exaggeration can be used to deny any partner entirely, such as in the Quranic verse 'Say: Is God better or what they associate?' which denies goodness in idols.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, for what purpose can the alif of mubalagha be used besides sharing and gradation?
A) To indicate a large quantity
B) To single out someone as uniquely having the attribute
C) To soften the statement
D) To emphasize a past action

Key Takeaway
The alif of exaggeration can function to assert exclusive possession of an attribute, thereby refuting any claim of partnership, which is essential for tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'the Most Merciful' or 'the All-Powerful,' do you ever unconsciously imagine that these attributes could apply to others in some lesser way? How does Baqillani's argument about the alif of exclusivity challenge that tendency in your heart?
?
In your daily life, do you ever attribute qualities to people or things that belong exclusively to God, even subtly ascribing ultimate power or control to yourself or others? What would it mean for you to repent from that and truly single God out?
?
The lesson shows that even a linguistic form can be twisted to support shirk if misunderstood. Are there any areas in your life where you have justified something contrary to tawhid by 'reasonable' arguments? Will you now revisit those justifications in light of this deeper understanding?

The Alif in 'Ahsan': Comparison or Exclusivity?
Baqillani refutes the claim that the alif in 'ahsan' always indicates comparison.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the alif in 'ahsan' can indicate exclusivity and refutation of false claims, not just comparison.

Core Idea
The alif in 'ahsan' is not always comparative; it can assert exclusivity against false claims.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani addresses a linguistic objection about the word 'ahsan' in the Qur'an. Some argue that the alif always implies comparison, but Baqillani shows it can also function to deny any partner in a description. Let's examine his argument and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection and the structure of the response.
Pay attention to the examples: they are key to understanding the argument.
2
Identify the two uses of the alif
The objectors claim the alif is only for exaggeration/comparison. Baqillani agrees it is the origin, but adds it can also be for exclusivity and refutation.
This is the central move in the argument.
3
Analyze the examples
Go through each example: Quranic, Hassan, al-Farazdaq, and the verse 'وهو أهون عليه'. See how each uses the alif to deny a false claim of partnership or equality.
The examples are meant to show the alif functioning in different contexts.
4
Connect to divine transcendence
Understand how this linguistic analysis supports the doctrine of tawhid by refuting those who claim partners with Allah.
The final verse 'أحسن الخالقين' directly refutes those who claim other creators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : الألف الذي في قوله «أحْسَنُ» ألف مبالغة لا يدخل مثل هذا الكلام إلا للاشتراك وإيقاع التفاضل في الوصف. قيل لهم : الأصل في هذه الألف كما زعمتم . إلا أنها قد تجيء للإفراد بالوصف وتكذيب دعوى من ادعى مشاركة ما ليس له الوصف خير الله خير مما يشركون على وجه التكذيب لدعواهم الخير فيما يشركون به ؛ وقول حسان بن ثابت : تَهْجُوهُ وَلَسْتَ لَهُ بِنِدَّ فَشَرُّكُمَا لِخَيْرِكُمَا الْفِدَاءُ يعني : إن كان في هجاء النبي ، صلى الله عليه وسلم ما ادعاه . وكذلك قول الفرزدق إنَّ الَّذِي رَفَعَ السَّمَاءَ بَنَى لَنَا بَيْناً دَعَالِمُهُ أَعَزُّ وَأَطْوَلُ يريد : إن كان بيت جرير عزيزاً طويلاً على ما يدعيه. وكذلك قوله تعالى : وهو أهون عليه ، يعني عندكم وفي اعتقادكم وظنونكم أن ابتداء الشيء على كل فاعل أهون عليه من إعادته . فكذلك قال : وله المثل الأعلى . فكذلك لما علم الله أن قوماً ادعوا مع الله خالقاً غيره منكم وممن سلف من إخوانكم ، قال : فتبارك الله أحسن الخالقين الذي ادعى المبطلون أنهم يخلقون ، فسقط ما توهموه.
If they say: The alif in His statement 'Ahsan' is an alif of exaggeration; this type of speech is only used for sharing (comparison) and causing superiority in the description. It is said to them: The origin of this alif is as you claimed. However, it may come for exclusivity in the description and refutation of the claim of one who claims participation for one who does not have the description, as in the saying of Allah: 'Is Allah better or what they associate?' on the basis of refuting their claim of good in what they associate. And the saying of Hassan ibn Thabit: 'You satirize him while you are not his equal, so the worse of you two is ransomed by the better of you two.' Meaning: if in his satire of the Prophet, peace be upon him, he claimed what he claimed. And similarly the saying of al-Farazdaq: 'Indeed, He who raised the heaven built for us a building whose pillars are mightier and longer.' He meant: if Jarir's house was mighty and long as he claimed. And similarly His saying, Exalted: 'And He is easier for Him' (Quran 30:27), meaning in your view and your belief and your assumptions that the beginning of a thing upon every agent is easier for him than its repetition. Thus He said: 'And His is the highest attribute' (Quran 30:27). Thus when Allah knew that a people claimed along with Allah another creator besides Him from among you and from among your predecessors, He said: 'So blessed is Allah, the best of creators' (Quran 23:14), which the falsifiers claimed that they create, so what they imagined fell away.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage records an objection and answer. The objectors argue that the alif in 'ahsan' (أحسن) is an alif of exaggeration (mubalaghah), which implies comparison and superiority. The response is that while the alif can be used for exaggeration, it can also be used for exclusivity (ifrad bil-wasf) and refutation of those who claim a partner shares in a description. The author provides several examples: Quranic 'خير مما يشركون' refutes the claim that partners have any good; Hassan's poem shows the alif is used to deny equality; al-Farazdaq's poem similarly denies that another's house is truly mighty; and the verse 'وهو أهون عليه' is interpreted as meaning 'according to your assumption' that beginning is easier than repetition. Then the author concludes that the verse 'فتبارك الله أحسن الخالقين' refutes the claim that others create. Thus false claimants are refuted.

ألف مبالغة
ألف إفراد
أحسن
تفاضل
اشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The alif in 'ahsan' always indicates comparison.
Correction: Baqillani shows it can also indicate exclusivity and refutation of false claims.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, the alif in 'Ahsan' can be used for which of the following?
A) Exaggeration only
B) Exclusivity and refutation
C) Just for comparison
D) Only for praise

Key Takeaway
The alif in 'ahsan' can serve to exclude partners from divine attributes, affirming Allah's uniqueness.

Will you commit to recognizing Allah's exclusive perfection even when your circumstances seem to indicate otherwise?
?
The passage refutes the claim that others create with Allah. In your own life, where might you be subtly claiming that you or others 'create' outcomes independent of Allah? How can you use the linguistic principle of exclusivity to reaffirm your tawheed?

The Meaning of 'No Disparity in Creation'
Baqillani refutes the objection that God cannot create evil because it is flawed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's refutation of the objection that God cannot create evil because His creation is described as flawless.

Core Idea
God's flawless creation refers to the heavens, not human actions.

This passage presents a common objection against divine creation of evil. The objector cites Quran 67:3 about no disparity in creation. Baqillani responds by clarifying the verse's specific reference to the heavens, thereby preserving God's transcendence while accounting for the existence of evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note how the objection is phrased and how Baqillani responds with a detailed exegesis of the verse.
The objection uses a Quranic verse as evidence; the answer restricts its application.
2
Understand the exegetical move
Notice that Baqillani specifies that the verse refers to the heavens (seven heavens, one above another). He interprets 'disparity' as physical cracks, not moral flaws.
This is a key hermeneutical point: the verse does not apply to all creation.
3
Reflect on theological implications
Consider why this distinction matters. If the verse were about all creation, it would imply God cannot create anything imperfect. Baqillani's interpretation preserves God's ability to create both good and evil while maintaining the perfection of the heavens.
This safeguards divine transcendence and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: أفليس قد قال الله: ما ترى في خلق الرحمن من تفاوت؟ فكيف يجوز أن يكون خالقاً للكفر والقبائح، وهي فاسدة متفاوتة؟ يقال لهم: إن الله تعالى خبر أنه لا يُرى في خلق السموات من تفاوت، لأنه قال: خلق سبع سموات طباقا يعني بعضها فوق بعض ما ترى في خلق الرحمن من تفاوت ـ يعني السموات والأرض؛ ثم قال: فارجع البصر يعني في السماء هل ترى من فطور يعني من صدوع وشقوق، يريد الإخبار عن إتقان فعلها وعجيب صنعها. والكفر لا فطور فيه ولا شقوق. ولولا الجهل ما تعلقوا بمثل هذا التأويل.
If they say: 'Did not God say, "You see no disparity in the creation of the Merciful"? So how is it permissible that He is the creator of disbelief and ugly deeds, which are corrupt and disparate?' It is said to them: Indeed God informed that no disparity is seen in the creation of the heavens, because He said: 'He created seven heavens, one above another' meaning some above others; 'You see no disparity in the creation of the Merciful' meaning the heavens and the earth. Then He said: 'Then return the gaze' meaning in the sky; 'Do you see any rifts?' meaning cracks and fissures. He intends informing about the perfection of His action and the wonder of His handiwork. Disbelief has no rifts or cracks. If not for ignorance, they would not grasp at such an interpretation.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector argues that since God's creation is described as having no disparity, He cannot create flawed things like disbelief. Baqillani responds by specifying that the verse refers specifically to the heavens, not to all creation. The 'disparity' (تفاوت) is about physical perfection, not moral quality. Disbelief is not a physical flaw. Thus the objection is based on ignorance.

Correction: Baqillani clarifies that the verse refers only to the physical creation of the heavens, not to moral or spiritual matters.

Checkpoint

A) Moral imperfection
B) Physical cracks in the heavens
C) Disparity in human deeds

Key Takeaway
The verse 'no disparity in creation' refers to the physical perfection of the heavens, not to the moral quality of human actions.

Refuting that Creating Evil Makes God Deserving of Evil
Baqillani refutes the claim that God becomes deserving of evil by creating it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's creation of insult, hatred, and other evils does not make Him deserving of those things, based on the principle that the agent of an act is not necessarily its recipient.

Core Idea
Creating an act does not make the creator its recipient.

Baqillani responds to an objection that if God creates insults and hatred directed at Himself, He must be worthy of them. Using clear analogies and logical reasoning, he establishes a fundamental principle: the doer of an action is not automatically deserving of that action. This lesson unpacks his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the initial objection from the opponents: if God creates insult and hatred against Himself and His messengers, then He must be worthy of those things.
Notice the opponents' assumption that creating something implies deserving it.
2
Examine Baqillani's analogies
Identify the four analogies Baqillani gives: (1) God creates the ability to insult but is not insulted; (2) God creates insanity but is not insane; (3) God creates punishment but is not punished; (4) a human creates his own insult but does not deserve it.
Each analogy shows the same logical flaw.
3
Grasp the principle
Understand the key principle: 'Not every doer of something must be deserving of it and worthy of it.' This refutes the objection.
This principle is the core of Baqillani's argument.
4
See the counter-argument
Note how Baqillani turns the table: if the opponents' logic were valid, God would have to create love and praise because He deserves them, which they would not accept—so their own claim fails.
This shows the inconsistency in the opponents' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة فإن قالوا : فإذا قلتم إن الله تعالى خلق شتم نفسه وشتم رسله وبغض من أبغض من عباده له ولرسله وسبّه لهما، فما أنكرتم أن يكون الله تعالى أهلاً للشتم والعداوة والبغض ومستحقاً لذلك؟ . قيل لهم : لا يجب ذلك، كما لا يجب عندنا وعندكم، إذا خلق سبحانه القدرة على شتمه وشتم رسله والبغض له ولهم، أن يكون هو تعالى ورسله أهلاً للشتم والعداوة والبغض؛ وكما لا يجب، إذا خلق الجنون والإقدار والإتيان، أن يكون أهلاً لما خلقه؛ وإذا خلق عذاب الكافرين ومحن الممتحنين أن يكون أهلاً لأن يُعذَّب ويمتحن تعالى عن ذلك وكما لا يجب عندكم، إذا خلق الإنسان الطاهر الوالدين شَتْمَ نفسه وأبويه وخلق قتل نفسه، أن يكون أهلا لأن يُشْتَم وأن يُشتم أبواه وأن يكون مستحقاً للشتم له ولهما ولقتل نفسه . وليس كل فاعل لشيء يجب كونه مستحقاً له وأهلاً له، فسقط ما قلتموه ويقال لهم : فيجب أن يكون الباري سبحانه خالقاً لحبّ المحبين له ولرسوله وثنائهم عليهما ومدحهم لهما لأنهما أهلاً لذلك. فإن لم يجب ذلك، لم يجب ما قلتم .
A question: For if they say: Since you claim that God Most High created insult to Himself and insult to His messengers, and hatred of whomever among His servants hated Him and His messengers, and abuse to them, then what prevents you from acknowledging that God Most High is worthy of insult, enmity, and hatred, and deserving of that? It is said to them: That is not necessary, just as it is not necessary in our view and your view—when He, glorified, created the ability to insult Him and insult His messengers and hatred for them—that He Most High and His messengers become worthy of insult, enmity, and hatred; and just as it is not necessary, when He created insanity, impotence, and ineptitude, that He becomes worthy of what He created; and when He created the punishment of disbelievers and the trials of the tested, that He becomes worthy of being punished and tested—exalted is He above that—and just as it is not necessary in your view, when a pure man created insult to himself and his parents and created the killing of himself, that he becomes worthy of being insulted, and that his parents be insulted, and that he becomes deserving of insult for himself and them and for killing himself. And not every doer of something must be deserving of it and worthy of it. So what you said has fallen. And it is said to them: Then it must be that the Creator, glorified, is the creator of the love of the lovers for Him and His messenger, and their praise of them and their commendation of them, because they are worthy of that. If that is not necessary, then what you said is not necessary.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The opponents argue: if God creates insult, hatred, and abuse directed at Himself and His prophets, then God must be deserving of those evils. Baqillani refutes this by showing that creating an action does not make the creator its recipient. He gives several analogies: (1) God creates the ability to insult, but that does not make Him worthy of insult. (2) God creates insanity and impotence, but He is not insane or impotent. (3) God creates punishment and trials for others, but He is not subjected to them. (4) Even a human who creates his own insult or his parents' insult is not thereby deserving of being insulted himself. The principle is: not every agent of an act is deserving of that act. Baqillani then turns the argument: if the opponents' logic held, then God would also have to be the creator of love and praise for Himself, because He is worthy of it. But if they deny that obligation, their own claim collapses.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creating evil makes God evil or deserving of that evil.
Correction: Baqillani shows that the attribute of the act does not transfer to the agent; the creator is not necessarily the recipient.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Creating an action does not make the creator its recipient; thus God is not deserving of the evils He creates.

Objection on Miracles of Liars and God's Leading Astray
Baqillani presents an objection and begins his answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if God can lead astray, He could empower liars with miracles, and see how Baqillani begins to refute it by showing the self-defeating nature of such an act.

Core Idea
Miracles supporting falsehood would contradict God's purpose of guiding to truth.

Opponents of the prophetic miracle argument raise a clever objection: if God can lead people astray, why couldn't He perform miracles through liars to further misguide? Baqillani starts his response by pointing out that this would incapacitate God from demonstrating truth, thus undermining His own guidance. This short passage sets up a key debate about divine attributes and the purpose of miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the opponents' premise: God leads astray, so why not use miracles through liars? Identify the logical structure of their challenge.
Note the phrase 'يُضلّ عن الدين' – leading astray from the religion.
2
Analyze Baqillani's initial response
Baqillani states that such an act would necessitate 'إيجاب تعجيزه' – necessitating incapacitation of God from showing truth. Consider how this is a self-reduction ad absurdum.
The argument is incomplete in this passage; think about how it might continue.
3
Reflect on the underlying principle
Baqillani's answer implies that miracles are inherently signs of truth; using them for falsehood contradicts their nature and God's wisdom.
This connects to the broader topic of divine attributes and the purpose of miracles.
4
Note the incomplete nature
The text ends mid-sentence; the full argument is not present. Use this as an opportunity to anticipate what the complete refutation might be.

Baqillani's reply starts: such an action would imply that God incapacitates Himself from demonstrating the truth to us. The argument is cut off, but the intended point is that God's wisdom and justice prevent Him from using miracles—which are signs of truth—to support falsehood, as that would defeat His purpose of guiding humanity. Divine Transcendence
beginner
6 min
God's creation of evil does not make Him deserve it
Baqillani defends God's transcendence from the objection that creating evil makes Him worthy of it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's creation of evil does not imply that He is worthy of blame, and the logical distinction between being an agent and being a recipient.

Core Idea
The agent of an action is not necessarily its subject or recipient.

This passage addresses a common objection against divine transcendence: if God creates the ability to commit evil, does that make Him worthy of evil? Baqillani responds with a clear logical principle and several analogies from daily life. As you read, notice how he distinguishes the act of creation from the act of being affected.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Focus on the objection and Baqillani's initial denial.
The objection is stated in the first line, and the answer begins with 'قيل لهم : لا يجب ذلك'.
2
Identify the key principle
Find where Baqillani states the principle: 'Not every agent of something must be deserving of it and worthy of it.' This is the core of his argument.
The principle is stated after the analogies: 'وليس كل فاعل لشيء يجب كونه مستحقاً له وأهلاً له'.
3
Examine the four analogies
List the four analogies Baqillani uses. Notice their common structure: creating X does not make the creator worthy of X. Each analogy reinforces the same point.
The analogies are: (1) creating ability to insult, (2) creating insanity, (3) creating punishment for disbelievers, (4) a person creating insult of himself.
4
Consider the counter-argument
At the end, Baqillani turns the tables: if the objectors insist on the correlation, they must accept that God creates love and praise because He is worthy. See why this shows their inconsistency.
This is a powerful dialectical move.
5
Reflect on the implication
How does this argument protect God's transcendence? Reflect on the difference between being the creator of an act and being the subject of an act.
This connects to the broader theme of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون الله تعالى أهلاً للشتم والعداوة والبغض ومستحقاً لذلك؟ قيل لهم : لا يجب ذلك، كما لا يجب عندنا وعندكم، إذا خلق سبحانه القدرة على شتمه وشتم رسله والبغض له ولهم، أن يكون هو تعالى ورسله أهلاً للشتم والعداوة والبغض؛ وكما لا يجب، إذا خلق الجنون والإقدار والإتيان، أن يكون أهلاً لما خلقه؛ وإذا خلق عذاب الكافرين ومحن الممتحنين أن يكون أهلاً لأن يُعذَّب ويمتحن تعالى عن ذلك وكما لا يجب عندكم، إذا خلق الإنسان الطاهر الوالدين شَتْمَ نفسه وأبويه وخلق قتل نفسه، أن يكون أهلا لأن يُشْتَم وأن يُشتم أبواه وأن يكون مستحقاً للشتم له ولهما ولقتل نفسه . وليس كل فاعل لشيء يجب كونه مستحقاً له وأهلاً له، فسقط ما قلتموه ويقال لهم : فيجب أن يكون الباري سبحانه خالقاً لحبّ المحبين له ولرسوله وثنائهم عليهما ومدحهم لهما لأنهما أهْلان لذلك. فإن لم يجب ذلك، لم يجب ما قلتم .
Is God Most High worthy of insult, enmity, and hatred, and deserving of that? It is said to them: That is not necessary, just as it is not necessary according to us and you, when He, glorified, created the ability to insult Him and insult His messengers and to hate Him and them, that He Most High and His messengers are worthy of insult, enmity, and hatred; and just as it is not necessary, when He created insanity, abilities, and [؟الإتيان], that He is worthy of what He created; and when He created the punishment of disbelievers and the trials of those tested, that He is worthy to be punished and tested — exalted is He above that — and just as it is not necessary according to you, when a pure human created the insult of himself and his parents and created the killing of himself, that he is worthy to be insulted, that his parents be insulted, and that he is deserving of insult for himself and for them and of killing himself. Not every agent of something must be deserving of it and worthy of it. So what you said falls. And it is said to them: Then it must be that the Creator, glorified, is the creator of the love of those who love Him and His Messenger, and their praise of them, and their commendation of them, because they are worthy of that. If that is not necessary, then what you said is not necessary.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer defense of divine transcendence. The objectors argue that if God creates the ability in people to insult Him and His messengers, then He must be worthy of insult. The answer begins by denying the implication: it is not necessary that the agent of an action is thereby worthy of that action. Baqillani provides four analogies from common beliefs: (1) If God creates the ability to insult, He is not thereby worthy of insult, just as when someone creates in himself the ability to insult himself, he is not necessarily worthy of that insult. (2) If God creates insanity or abilities, He is not thereby insane or able in that sense. (3) If God creates the punishment of disbelievers, He is not thereby deserving of punishment. (4) In everyday experience, if a person creates (causes) his own insult or killing, he is not thereby worthy of insult or deserving of death. The principle is that the agent of an action is not automatically its recipient. Thus the objection fails. Baqillani then adds a counter-argument: if objectors insist on this correlation, then they must say God creates love and praise for Himself and His Messenger because they are worthy of it; otherwise their own claim collapses.

Key Terms
الأهلية (worthiness)
الاستحقاق (deserving)
الفاعل (agent)
القبيح (evil/ugly)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God creates evil, He must be evil or deserving of blame, because the creator is responsible for what He creates.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that being the agent of an action does not make one the subject or recipient of that action. Creating evil does not make the Creator evil or deserving of blame, just as creating insanity does not make one insane.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, if God creates the ability to insult Him, does that make Him worthy of insult?
A) Yes, because He is the agent
B) No, because He is not the one doing the insult
C) Yes, because He created the ability
D) No, because He is above all actions

Key Takeaway
God's creation of evil actions does not make Him deserving of them because the agent of an action is not necessarily its recipient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever blamed God for the evil in your life or in the world? After learning that being the creator of an action does not make Him its subject, how does this change your response to suffering? Will you now direct your anger and questions toward the real agents—yourself and others—rather than attributing blame to God?
?
When you sin, do you ever excuse yourself by saying 'God created my action' or 'It was destined'? How does this lesson's distinction between agent and subject hold you personally accountable for your choices, even though God is the ultimate creator? Will you now take full moral responsibility for your deeds?
?
If God is not deserving of blame for creating evil, what does that demand of you in terms of your own agency? Are you quick to blame circumstances or other people for your own wrongdoing? This lesson calls you to reflect: will you repent of shifting blame and instead own your actions, knowing that God's creation of them does not reduce your accountability?

God's perfection prevents lying even if He could
Baqillani refutes the claim that God could lie, based on His eternal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari scholars argue that God cannot lie based on His eternal attribute of truthfulness, not on a theory of good and evil.

Core Idea
God cannot lie because His truthfulness is necessary, not because lying is evil.

The objectors press a clever point: if God can do what is evil for us, why can't He lie? Baqillani's answer draws on God's essential attributes. This passage clarifies that God's perfection precludes certain actions by necessity, not by moral category.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the objectors speak first, then Baqillani responds.
2
Identify the objectors' premise
The objectors assume that since Ash'aris allow God to do what is evil for humans, they should allow lying.
3
Note Baqillani's key distinction
Baqillani says impossibility is due to God's eternal attribute of truthfulness, not because lying is evil.
He compares it to disability being impossible for the powerful.
4
Understand the proof
The proof that God is eternally speaking, powerful, truthful establishes the impossibility of lying.
5
Conclude the argument
The objection fails because it confuses moral categories with necessary attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فإذا أجزتم فعل القديم لجميع ما يقبح منا، وإن لم يقبح ذلك منه ، فأجيزوا عليه الكذب في خبره ولا يكون ذلك منه قبيحاً قلنا: إنما نُحيل عليه كما نحيل عليه العجز والسهو، على أنه لم يزل متكلماً قادراً صادقاً، لا لقبح ذلك منه . لقيام الدليل على فبطل ما ظننتم .
If they say: Since you permit the Ancient to do all that is evil for us, even if that is not evil from Him, then permit lying in His news and that would not be evil from Him. We say: We only deem it impossible for Him, just as we deem impossible disability and forgetfulness, due to the proof that He has never ceased to be speaking, powerful, truthful; not because that is evil from Him. Due to the establishment of proof, so what you thought is nullified.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is a third objection and answer. The objectors argue: if Ash'aris claim that God can do what would be evil if done by humans (since acts are not intrinsically good or evil), then why can't God lie? The answer distinguishes between what is possible in principle and what is precluded by God's necessary attributes. Baqillani says that lying is impossible for God not because lying is inherently evil (قبيح), but because we have proof that God is eternally truthful (صادق), just as we have proof He is powerful and speaking. Lying would contradict His attribute of truthfulness, so it is impossible by necessity, just as disability is impossible for the eternally powerful. The impossibility derives from the nature of His perfection, not from a moral category.

Key Terms
الكذب (lying)
القبيح (evil/ugly)
الصدق (truthfulness)
الدليل (proof)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'aris believe God could lie, since they reject inherent good and evil.
Correction: Ash'aris deny God could lie based on His eternal attribute of truthfulness, not on a theory of evil.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani say God cannot lie?
A) Because lying is inherently evil for anyone
B) Because God is eternally truthful by nature, not because lying is evil
C) Because God does not have the power to lie
D) Because lying would contradict His power

Key Takeaway
God's truthfulness is a necessary attribute, so lying is impossible for Him by the very nature of His perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life have you treated a small lie as permissible because it seemed 'harmless'? How does the fact that God's truthfulness is a necessary perfection—not a choice—change your view of your own accountability to truth?
?

?
The objectors assumed that without an inherent evil in lying, God could lie. Do you sometimes act as if certain sins are acceptable because they 'aren't that bad'? How does Baqillani's shift from evil to divine attribute challenge your excuses?

Impossibility of Hell as Believers' Abode and the Condition for Ajl
Baqillani refutes a misconception about the term of life and Hell's abode.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that Allah's knowledge of a possible scenario does not make that scenario actual, using analogies of Hell, Paradise, and marriage.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge does not turn possibility into actuality.

Baqillani addresses a subtle misconception: if Allah knows that something could happen under certain conditions, does that make it bound to happen? He uses clear analogies from the afterlife and human relationships to show why not. This lesson explores his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense with analogies.
2
Identify the main claim and analogies
The passage refutes a misconception about ajal and Hell. Find the three analogies used: Hell/Paradise, marriage, and the term of life. Notice how each analogy illustrates the same principle.
Write them down in your own words.
3
Reflect on the principle: possibility vs. actuality
Baqillani distinguishes between what is rationally possible under a hypothetical condition and what is actual. Ask yourself: How does Allah's knowledge of a possible outcome differ from its actual occurrence?
This connects to the core idea.
4
Apply the reasoning to divine predestination
Consider why this argument is important for understanding qada and qadar. It shows that Allah's foreknowledge does not force events to happen; they happen by His will and decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمتد حياته إليه أجلاً له، إن كان المعلوم من حاله أنه يخترم دونه. كما لا يجوز أن تكون جهنّم دار الأنبياء والصالحين وسائر المؤمنين، لأنه جائز في العقل أن يدخلوها لو كفروا، وأن تكون الجنّة دار الفراعنة والكفرة على أنهم لو آمنوا لسكنوها. وكما لا يجب أن تكون المرأة التي يعلم الله أن الإنسان لو بقي لتزوّج بها زوجةً له على معنى أنه لو بقي وتزوجها لكانت زوجة له فكذلك لا يجوز أن يكون ما لم يَبْقَ إليه من الأوقات أجلاً له لأنه لو لم يمت لصح أن يبقى إليه.
Its life extends to a term for him, if it is known from his state that he will be cut short before it. And just as it is not permissible that Hell be the abode of the prophets, the righteous, and all the believers, because it is rationally possible that they would enter it if they disbelieved, and that Paradise be the abode of the Pharaohs and disbelievers on condition that if they believed they would dwell in it. And just as it is not necessary that the woman whom Allah knows that if a man remained alive he would marry her be a wife for him, in the sense that if he remained and married her she would be his wife, so likewise it is not permissible that what does not remain until it from the times be a term for him, because if he had not died, it would be valid for him to remain until it.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a misconception regarding the term of life (ajal) and the abode of believers in the afterlife. The author argues by analogy: First, the idea that a life extends to a specific term is only meaningful if it is known that the person will die before reaching that term. Second, it is not permissible to say that Hell is the abode of believers, because it is rationally possible that they would enter it if they disbelieved, but they do not disbelieve. Similarly, Paradise could be the abode of disbelievers if they believed, but they do not. This shows that possibility does not entail actuality. Third, if a man had remained alive, he would have married a woman, but that does not make her his wife in actuality because he did not remain alive. Likewise, a time that will not be reached because of death cannot be considered the term (ajal) of that person, because if he had not died, he would have lived to that time. Therefore, Allah's knowledge of what would happen under hypothetical conditions does not make those conditions actual. This is a key point in the Ash'ari doctrine of qada and qadar.

Key Terms
أجل
جهنم
جنة
جائز
يخترم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if Allah knows a possible outcome, that outcome becomes necessary or actual.
Correction: Allah's knowledge of what would happen under hypothetical conditions does not make those conditions actual; actualization requires His will and the actual occurrence of the conditions.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Baqillani's argument in this passage?
A) That Hell is the abode of believers only if they disbelieve.
B) That Allah's knowledge of a possible scenario does not make that scenario actual.
C) That the term of life is fixed regardless of conditions.
D) That marriage is invalid if not actualized.

Key Takeaway
Allah's knowledge of what would happen under hypothetical conditions does not make those conditions actual; actuality depends on His will and decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often claim to trust Allah's plan, but do you secretly believe that because He knows your future, you have no real choice? How does this passage expose that belief as a hidden fatalism? What step will you take today to act as if your choices matter, even though Allah knows them?
?
Think of a sin you feel you would never commit because you are 'too good' a Muslim. This passage says it is rationally possible for you to enter Hell if you disbelieved. Does that possibility frighten you enough to make you check your arrogance? What specific change in your prayer life will you make to remind yourself of your utter dependence on Allah's mercy?
?
When you imagine what your life would be if you had made different decisions, you might feel regret or pride. This passage teaches that hypotheticals are not actual. Are you wasting your present moments dwelling on 'what if'? What one thing will you stop regretting and start doing, knowing that Allah's actual decree for you now is what matters?

Allah's Exclusive Guidance and Misguidance: Quranic Proofs
Baqillani affirms divine guidance and misguidance with Quranic evidence and clarifies their primary meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses Quranic verses to affirm that Allah alone creates both guidance (faith) and misguidance, and how he distinguishes this creative sense from other meanings of hidayah.

Core Idea
Allah creates faith in the heart; guidance in the Quran primarily means this creative act.

Baqillani opens his chapter on guidance and misguidance by directly affirming that Allah guides believers and misguides disbelievers. He immediately supports this with decisive Quranic verses, and then clarifies that the core meaning of divine guidance is the creation of faith, not mere calling or showing the way. This lesson examines his argument and scriptural evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the question and answer.
The passage begins with a hypothetical question: 'Do you say that Allah guides the believers and misguides the disbelievers?' Baqillani answers with a firm 'Yes.' This frames the entire chapter.
2
Note the Quranic verses cited and their role.
Baqillani cites four verses: 1) Allah invites to peace and guides whom He wills, 2) You cannot guide whom you love, but Allah guides, 3) Allah expands the breast for Islam for whom He wills to guide, and constricts it for whom He wills to misguide, 4) Whom Allah guides is guided, whom He misguides are losers. These verses attribute guidance and misguidance exclusively to Allah.
These are from Surah Yunus, Al-Qasas, Al-An'am, and Al-A'raf respectively.
3
Understand Baqillani's key interpretation.
He states that in these verses, 'guidance' must mean the creation of faith (khalq al-iman) in the heart, and 'misguidance' must mean the creation of disbelief. This is the primary meaning in the context of divine action.
4
Recognize that other meanings are possible but secondary.
Baqillani acknowledges that 'hidayah' can also mean da'wah (calling) or leading to Paradise, as seen in other verses. This shows he is not denying the lexical range, but asserting the primary theological meaning.
Example: 'Indeed, you guide to a straight path' means 'you call.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الهدى والإضلال فإن قال قائل : فهل تقولون إن الله يهدي المؤمنين ويُضلّ الكافرين؟ . قيل له : أجل . اعلم، وفقك الله تعالى لمرضاته أن كتاب الله العزيز اشتمل على أي دالة على تفرد الرب تعالى بهداية الخلق وإضلالهم. وهي نصوص لإبطال مذاهب مخالفي أهل الحق منها قوله : والله يدعو إلى دار السلام ويهدي من يشاء إلى صراط مستقيم ) وقوله تعالى : إنك لا تهدي من أحببت ولكن الله يهدي من يشاء ) وقوله تعالى : ﴿ فمن يرد الله أن يهديه يشرح صدره للإسلام ومن يرد أن يضله يجعل صدره ضيقاً حرجاً ) وقال عزّ وجلّ : من يهد الله فهو المهتدي ومن يضلل فأولئك هم الخاسرون ) واعلم أن الهدى في هذه الآي لا يتجه حمله إلا على خلق الإيمان، وكذلك لا يتجه حمل الإضلال على غير خلق الضلال ولسنا ننكر ورود الهداية في كتاب الله عزّ وجلّ على غير المعنى الذي رمناه. فقد يرد والمراد به الدعوة ؛ قال الله تعالى : ( إنك لتهدي إلى صراط مستقيم معناه إنك لتدعو وقد ترد الهداية ويراد بها إرشاد المؤمنين إلى مسالك الجنان والطرق المفضية إليها يوم القيامة، قال الله تعالى: ﴿ فلن يضل أعمالهم سيهديهم ويصلح بالهم
Chapter of Guidance and Misguidance. So if a questioner asks: Do you say that Allah guides the believers and misguides the disbelievers? It is said to him: Yes. Know, may Allah grant you success to His pleasure, that the Mighty Book of Allah contains many indications of the exclusivity of the Lord, Exalted, in the guidance of creation and their misguidance. They are texts for invalidating the doctrines of the opponents of the people of truth. Among them is His saying: 'And Allah invites to the abode of peace and guides whom He wills to a straight path.' And His saying, Exalted: 'Indeed, you do not guide whom you love, but Allah guides whom He wills.' And His saying, Exalted: 'So whoever Allah wants to guide, He expands his breast to Islam; and whoever He wants to misguide, He makes his breast tight and constricted.' And He said, Mighty and Majestic: 'Whom Allah guides, he is the guided; and whom He misguides, those are the losers.' And know that guidance (al-huda) in these verses cannot be interpreted except as the creation of faith, and likewise misguidance cannot be interpreted as other than the creation of misguidance. And we do not deny that guidance (al-hidaya) occurs in the Book of Allah, Mighty and Majestic, in a sense other than what we intended. For it may occur meaning da'wah (calling); Allah said: 'Indeed, you guide to a straight path,' meaning you call. And guidance may occur meaning the guidance of the believers to the pathways of Paradise and the roads leading to it on the Day of Resurrection; Allah said: 'He will not let their deeds go to waste. He will guide them and set right their condition.'
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the chapter on guidance and misguidance. The author first affirms the question that Allah indeed guides believers and misguides disbelievers. He then provides evidence from the Quran, citing several verses that attribute both guidance and misguidance exclusively to Allah. He clarifies that in these verses, hidayah (guidance) means the creation of faith (khalq al-iman) in the heart, and idlal (misguidance) means the creation of misguidance (khalq al-dalal). He acknowledges that hidayah can also appear in the Quran with other meanings, such as da'wah (calling) to Islam, or guiding believers to Paradise in the afterlife. This shows that the primary meaning in the context of divine action is creative, not merely instructional.

Misconception: Some think that divine guidance only means showing the right path through prophets and signs, without any internal creative act.
Correction: Baqillani shows that the Quran attributes guidance to Allah's direct creation of faith in the heart, as seen in verses about expanding the breast for Islam.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the primary meaning of hidayah (guidance) in the Quranic verses he cites?
A) Calling people to Islam (da'wah).
B) Showing the right path (irshad).
C) Creating faith (khalq al-iman).
D) Leading to Paradise in the afterlife.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani argues that divine guidance primarily means Allah's creation of faith in the heart, and misguidance His creation of disbelief, as proven by multiple Quranic verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If guidance is solely from Allah, not from your own efforts or even from the best da'wah, what does that mean for your sense of control over your own imaan? Do you truly trust Allah to guide, or do you rely on your own plans to increase faith?
?
You have been told that hidayah includes calling and showing the way, but this lesson insists it primarily means creation of faith. Has your heart ever truly felt that imaan is a direct gift from Allah, or have you acted as if you can manufacture it through rituals alone? What changes if you internalize that every moment of belief is a new act of divine creation?

The passage cites the verse 'Indeed, you do not guide whom you love, but Allah guides whom He wills.' Think of someone you deeply love and wish were guided. Are you willing to release your attachment to being the cause of their guidance, and instead plead to the One who alone creates faith? Will you pray for them with the understanding that only Allah can shape their heart, and not just try to convince them with arguments?

The First Objection and the Ash'ari Response on Divine Actions
Objector lists seemingly unjust divine acts; response argues God is absolute owner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against divine justice raised by the Mu'tazila and the Ash'ari response based on God's absolute ownership and sovereignty.

Core Idea
God's actions are not judged by human standards because He is the absolute Owner with no commander over Him.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange where an objector lists seemingly unjust divine acts, triggering a deep discussion on the nature of divine justice. We will explore how the Ash'ari theologian responds by grounding morality in God's absolute sovereignty, distinguishing His actions from human ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the dialectical structure. Notice the interplay between objection and response.
The passage includes a digression on Mu'tazila views; keep track of the main argument.
2
Identify the objector's list of criticisms
The objector enumerates several divine actions that seem unjust: commanding animal slaughter, inflicting pain without benefit, subjugating animals, eternal punishment for finite sins, impossible obligations, and creating people for punishment.
This list challenges the notion of divine justice and sets up the need for a response.
3
Examine the initial response and the challenge
The response affirms that these acts are just, but the objector asks why they are good from God but bad from humans. This challenge highlights the problem of moral analogy.
4
Understand the Mu'tazila excursus
The author presents various Mu'tazila theories on pain and compensation. This background shows the alternative views that the Ash'ari answer aims to refute.
This section is a digression but helps understand the context of the debate.
5
Grasp the final Ash'ari answer
The answer states that these acts are bad from us only because God prohibited them. Since God has no prohibitor, they are not bad from Him. This is rooted in the concept of God as absolute Owner and Sovereign.
Compare this with the Mu'tazila positions to see the difference in approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عوض (1)، وأن يأمر بذبح الحيوان وإيلامه لا لنفع يصل (2) إليهم، وأن يسخر بعض (3) الحيوان لبعض، وأن يفعل العقاب الدائم على الأجرام المنقطعة، وأن يكلف عباده ما لا يطيقون، وأن يخلق فيهم ما يعذبهم عليه، وغير ذلك من الأمور (4)؟ . قيل له : أجل ذلك عدل، من فعله، جائز مستحسن في حكمته (5). فإن قال (6) : فكيف (7) : فكيف (۷) جاز ذلك منه (۸) وحسن مع قبح ذلك منا أجمع (10)؟ . على الآلام أحط رتبه من الثواب الملتزم على التكليف، واختلفوا في أن العوض هل يدوم دوام الثواب أم لا؟ . ذلك واضطربت أجوبتهم في أنه هل يتصور التفضل بمثل الأعراض ابتداء؟ فصار بعضهم إلى أن ممتنع ، كما يمتنع التفضل بمثل ثواب التكليف إذ ذاك مجمع على امتناعه، وصار من انتمى إلى التحصيل منهم إلى أن التفضل بأقدار الأعواض ممكن غير ممتنع . فمن قال بامتناع التفضل بأمثال الأعواض لم تحسن الآلام عنده لمحض التعويض، بل قال إنما يحسن بوجهين لا بد من اقترانهما ؛ إحداهما التزام التعويض والثاني : اعتبار غير المؤلم بتلك الآلام وكونها ألطافاً في زجر الغاوي عن غوايته . وذهب عباد الصيمري إلى أن الآلام تحس بمحض الاعتبار من غير تقدير تعويض عليها . فهذه أصول المعتزلة في إيلام البهائم والأطفال. ثم من تمام أصلها إن ما يحسن الألم لأجله لو علم، فإنه يحسن إذا اعتقد أو غلب على الظن ما يحسن الآلام لأجله في عادات الناس. قالوا : وكذلك يحسن في عادات الناس العقلاء التزام المشقات لتوقع منافع زائدة عليها وإن كانت عواقبها منطوية عن العباد وعلام الغيوب المستأثر بعلمها . انظر الإرشاد للجويني فصل الآلام وأحكامها .  قيل له : إن (۱) ذلك إنما قبح منا وصار جوراً من فعلنا لأجل نهي مالك (۲) الأعيان والأشياء لنا عن فعله ؛ فلولا (۳) تقبيحه لذلك ونهيه عنه ، لما قبح منا . وقد أوضحنا ذلك فيما سلف لما قلنا إن ذلك ليس بقبيح في العقل لنفسه، لأنه كان أن يشترك في علمه العاقلين، ولكان يجب جميع [؟] ... والباري عـزّ المالك القاهر الذي الأشياء له وفي قبضته (۷) لا آمر عليه ولا ولا حاظر. فلم يجب أن يقبح جميع ما ذكرناه من فعله قياساً على قبحه منا .
And that He commands the slaughter of animals and inflicting pain on them without benefit reaching them, and that He subjects some animals to others, and that He does perpetual punishment on perishable bodies, and that He charges His servants with what they cannot bear, and that He creates in them what He punishes them for, and other such matters? It was said to him: Yes, that is justice from Him, permissible, deemed good in His wisdom. For he said: How is that permissible from Him and good while it is bad from all of us? Regarding pains: they are lower in rank than the reward obligated for obligation, and they differed on whether compensation lasts as long as reward or not. And their answers were confused about whether it is possible to bestow the like of accidents initially? Some held it impossible, just as it is impossible to bestow the like of reward for obligation since that is unanimously impossible. And those who attributed themselves to verification among them held that bestowing amounts of compensations is possible, not impossible. So whoever said bestowing the likes of compensations is impossible, for him pains are not good for mere compensation; rather he said they are good by two aspects that must be conjoined: one is undertaking compensation, and the second is consideration of the one not pained by those pains and their being kindnesses in deterring the erring from his error. And 'Abbad al-Saymari held that pains are good by mere consideration without estimating compensation for them. These are the principles of the Mu'tazila regarding inflicting pain on animals and children. Then from the completion of their principle: if that for which pain is good were known, it is good if it is believed or it is most likely that for which pain is good in people's customs. They said: and likewise it is good in the customs of rational people to undertake hardships in anticipation of additional benefits even if their consequences are hidden from servants, and He is the Knower of the unseen, sole possessor of His knowledge. See al-Irshad by al-Juwayni, chapter on pains and their rulings. It was said to him: That is only bad from us and becomes injustice from our action because of the prohibition of the Owner of entities and things to us from doing it; were it not for His making it bad and His prohibition of it, it would not be bad from us. And we have clarified that previously when we said that it is not bad in the intellect for itself, because it would be that rational persons would share in its knowledge, and it would be necessary that all... And the Blessed and Exalted is the Owner, the Subduer, to Whom all things belong and in His grasp; there is no commander over Him nor any prohibitor. So it is not necessary that all that we mentioned be bad from His action by analogy to its badness from us.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical objection-and-answer sequence on the problem of divine justice. The objector lists several divine actions that appear unjust: commanding animal slaughter, inflicting pain without benefit, subjugating some animals to others, eternal punishment for finite sins, impossible obligations, and creating people with traits for which they are punished. The initial response is a brief affirmation that these are just and good in God's wisdom. The objector then presses: how can these be good from God while they would be bad from us? The author then inserts a detailed exposition of Mu'tazila theories on pain and compensation, describing their views on whether compensation for pain is necessary, whether God can bestow favors similar to rewards, and the positions of various Mu'tazila scholars. After this background, the answer is given: the actions are bad from us only because God has prohibited them; if He had not prohibited them, they would not be bad. God, as the absolute Owner and Subduer, has no commander or prohibitor above Him. Therefore, His actions cannot be judged bad by analogy to human actions. The argument hinges on the Ash'ari principle that moral value (husn/qubh) is determined by divine command, not by intrinsic properties of acts.

Key Terms
المالك
الجور
القبيح
العوض
الإيلام
الألطاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that Ash'aris deny any objective basis for divine justice, implying that God could do anything without being just.
Correction: This passage shows that Ash'aris affirm divine justice, but define it in terms of God's ownership and sovereignty, not by human standards. God's actions are just because He is the absolute Owner and there is no authority over Him.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response in this passage, why are actions that would be unjust from humans not unjust from God?
A) Because God has greater wisdom and knows hidden benefits.
B) Because God is the absolute Owner and there is no prohibition over Him.
C) Because such actions are actually good in themselves but humans are forbidden from them.
D) Because God compensates all suffering in the afterlife.

Key Takeaway
God's actions cannot be judged by human moral standards because He is the absolute Owner with no prohibitor; what seems unjust from humans is not necessarily unjust from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you suffer or witness injustice in the world, do you secretly judge God as unfair? How does the fact that He is the absolute Owner with no prohibitor challenge that judgment? What would it take for you to surrender your own sense of fairness to His will today?
?
Think of a time you complained about God's commands feeling too heavy. After this lesson, do you still see them as impositions from a distant commander, or as the rightful directives of your Owner? Will you consciously start seeing yourself as His property, not an independent agent?
?
How often do you measure God by your own moral standards, as if He must answer to your sense of right and wrong? This lesson declares that His actions are not analogous to ours. Are you ready to stop demanding that God fit into your box of justice and instead submit to His? What is the first step you will take to change that attitude?
