The Rational Possibility of God's Acts: Afflicting Prophets, Rewarding Disbelievers
Baqillani affirms rational possibility of God afflicting prophets before revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari principle that before revelation, God's actions are not subject to rational moral judgment — He may afflict prophets and reward disbelievers if He wills.

Core Idea
Before revelation, God's actions are beyond human moral judgment.

Baqillani addresses a critical objection: If reason alone cannot impose obligations on God, then could God rationally afflict prophets and reward disbelievers? This passage clarifies that such acts are rationally permissible before revelation, because morality depends on divine command. The answer affirms divine freedom and underscores the foundational Ash'ari stance that reason alone does not bind God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objector's question carefully: Is it rationally valid that God afflicts all prophets and delights all disbelievers before revelation? Notice the phrasing 'from the standpoint of reason before the coming of revelation.'
The question tests the consistency of the Ash'ari position.
2
Identify the answer
Baqillani's answer is a clear 'Yes, He has that.' Then he adds that if God did it, it would be permissible and not objectionable. Identify the Arabic terms for 'permissible' (جائز) and 'not objectionable' (غير مستنكر).
3
Grasp the implication
This teaches that reason alone cannot impose moral categories on God's actions. Only revelation tells us what is actually good or bad. Reflect on how this differs from other theological schools.
This is a cornerstone of Ash'ari ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : فهل يصح على قولكم هذا أن يؤلم الله سبحانه سائر النبيين وينعم سائر الكفرة والعاصين من جهة العقل قبل ورود السمع ؟ قيل له : أجل له ذلك . ولو فعله لكان جائزاً منه غير مستنكر من فعله .
Then if a questioner says: Is it valid according to this statement of yours that God, exalted, afflicts all the prophets and delights all the disbelievers and the sinful from the standpoint of reason before the coming of revelation? It was said to him: Yes, He has that. And if He did it, it would be permissible from Him, not objectionable from His action.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a brief objection-and-answer pair. The objector asks whether, according to the Ash'ari position that moral obligations depend on revelation, it is rationally possible for God to afflict all prophets and reward all disbelievers and sinners before revelation comes. The answer is a clear affirmation: Yes, God has that power, and if He did it, it would be permissible and not objectionable from Him. This underscores the Ash'ari principle that God's actions are not subject to prior rational moral constraints; only revelation tells us what God actually commands and prohibits. Thus, before revelation, there are no moral obligations, and God's actions are simply beyond human judgment.

Key Terms
العقل
السمع
جائز
مستنكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason alone can determine that God's actions are unjust if He afflicts prophets.
Correction: Ash'aris hold that before revelation, there is no moral standard; God's actions are not rationally evaluable as just or unjust. Revelation defines justice.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari position on the rational possibility of God afflicting prophets before revelation?
A) It is impossible because prophets deserve reward.
B) It is permissible and not objectionable.
C) It is impermissible because it would be unjust.
D) It is necessary for testing faith.

Key Takeaway
Before revelation, God's actions are rationally permissible and not objectionable, because moral obligations are established only through revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If before revelation God's actions are beyond human moral judgment, does that shake your comfort in God's justice, or does it deepen your trust that God defines justice perfectly? Examine your heart: do you truly accept that God's actions are always right, even when they seem harsh by human standards?
?
This passage says it is rationally possible for God to afflict prophets and reward disbelievers. If you were a believer facing severe trials, would you still trust God's wisdom, or would you question His justice? How does this lesson challenge your reliance on your own moral reasoning?
?
When you read that God's actions are 'not objectionable' before revelation, do you feel a tension? How does your own sense of justice compare to God's? Are you willing to submit your judgment to His, even when you cannot understand?

Objection and Answer: Can God Punish Prophets Before Revelation?
Baqillani answers an objection about divine actions before revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Baqillani, reason alone does not impose moral obligations on God before revelation, and that divine actions are not constrained by human ethical judgments prior to scriptural commands.

Core Idea
Before revelation, God's actions are not judged by human reason.

This short passage presents a sharp objection to Baqillani's position that harm is not inherently evil. An interlocutor asks whether his view implies God could punish all prophets and reward all disbelievers before revelation comes. Baqillani's answer clarifies the limits of rational judgment in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the objector frames the challenge: does Baqillani's position logically allow God to cause pain to all prophets and grant blessings to all disbelievers?
The phrase 'before revelation' is crucial.
2
Identify Baqillani's answer
Baqillani affirms that it is permissible (جائز) for God to do so, and adds that if He actually did it, it would not be unacceptable.
3
Reflect on 'before revelation'
Why does Baqillani qualify his answer with 'before revelation comes'? This shows that reason alone does not determine good and evil.
Revelation is what defines moral obligations.
4
Connect to the broader principle
This passage illustrates the Ash'ari principle that good and evil are known through revelation, not reason. God's actions are not constrained by human moral judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : فهل يصح على قولكم هذا أن يؤلم الله سبحانه سائر النبيين وينعم سائر الكفرة والعاصين من جهة العقل قبل ورود السمع ؟ . قيل له : أجل له ذلك . ولو فعله لكان جائزاً منه غير مستنكر من فعله .
If a speaker says: Is it valid according to this statement of yours that God—glorified is He—should cause pain to all prophets and bestow blessings upon all disbelievers and sinners from the perspective of reason before revelation comes? It is said to him: Yes, that is permissible for Him. And if He did it, it would be permissible from Him, not unacceptable from His action.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector asks whether, according to Baqillani's position that harm is not inherently evil, it would be rationally valid for God to inflict pain on all prophets and grant blessings to all disbelievers and sinners before any revelation (السمع). Baqillani answers affirmatively: yes, that is permissible (جائز) for God. He adds that if God actually did that, it would be permissible and not unacceptable from Him, because reason alone does not impose moral obligations on God before revelation. This establishes that divine actions are not constrained by human ethical judgments prior to scriptural commands.

Key Terms
قبل ورود السمع
جائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's justice requires Him to always reward prophets and punish disbelievers, even before revelation.
Correction: Baqillani argues that before revelation, reason alone does not determine what is good or evil, so God's actions are not constrained by such judgments.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, before revelation comes, is it rationally valid for God to punish prophets?
A) No, because that would be unjust
B) Yes, it is permissible
C) No, because prophets are innocent
D) Yes, but only if they commit sins

Key Takeaway
Before revelation, divine actions are not subject to human moral categories; they are judged only by revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has there been a time when you questioned God's actions in your life because they seemed unfair from your perspective? How does this lesson challenge you to submit to His will without demanding that it align with your sense of justice?
?
If God's justice is not bound by human standards, what does that mean for your trust in Him during suffering? Are you willing to accept that His actions are always right, even if they feel wrong to you? What would that look like practically in your daily prayers?
?
Imagine you are standing before God and He asks you: 'Did you ever accuse Me of injustice because I did not meet your expectations?' What would you say? After this lesson, what change must you make in your heart and your speech about God's decrees?
